And then, when approximately 40 days have passed, administer a properly made bandage, and let him wear it for another 40 days.
And be careful of work, overeating, drinking, and loud voices, for if he follows this regimen of complete healing, he will be cured.
And I will explain the treatment for ruptures in other parts of the belly, and if, at the beginning, you want them to not break out, cauterize the rupture with a round cautery, proportionate to its size, and make it dry like a third of it; then treat it with what we have said, and it is not adjusted.
And some earlier practitioners might say that a rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery, following this shape, since it is marked with a probe:
And a line is drawn across the widest part of the rupture, and another line above it; and place your hand with a wedge-shaped cautery, but the first cautery is lighter and better.
Chapter 46.
Of cauterization of twisting.
When pain in certain limbs arises from injury or blows, and that pain persists for a long time and you do not find a cure through medicine, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And let the cautery be of each limb according to its size and smallness, its strength and weakness, and the location of the twisting and pain in it.
Therefore, if it is healed by the first cautery, good; and if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is the custom of those pains that they are repeatedly cauterized on the limb that is closest to the affected one, so that one must cauterize repeatedly until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Of the cauterization of leprosy.
Some leprous people receive great benefit from cauterization, and especially if the type of leprosy is caused by the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy.
And therefore, when you wish to cauterize that person, consider if the leprosy is of a kind that can be cured with what we have said in the division, and not otherwise.  If you fear that the corruption has spread throughout the patient's body and entire constitution, then cauterize the affected area on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, a second below that, near the forehead at the edge of the hair; and two more on each side of the head; and one on the back of the head, at the base of the neck, or nape.
And finally, press the cautery into the bone a little until a small indentation is made, and the skin is displaced, so that the expulsion of thick vapors may be relieved; and cauterize over the spleen as needed.
And if the leprosy has already spread over the patient and is evident, then you should cauterize it with those cauteries that we described for the head;  and with a cautery on the end of the nose, and two cauteries on both cheeks, and two cauteries on two vertebrae of the neck, and six cauteries on the spinal vertebrae of the back, and one large one on the vertebrae near the hips, one on the hips themselves, and two on the thighs, one on each thigh, and two on the shins, one on each shin, and two on the calves, and two on the muscles, and two on the tendons of each foot; and cauterize it on all the joints of the fingers and toes, and on each heel of the feet, and on the soles of the hands.
And be careful not to burn the nerves that are above the heel.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery on the hip bone, and another on the opening of the stomach, and another on the liver.
And know that the more cauteries you apply, the more helpful and beneficial it is; and know that the patient feels no pain from the cautery, just as they don't from their cuts, because their body is already numb.
And the cauteries should be large or small according to the limbs and joints, as previously described by the types of instruments.
And then treat the cauterized area with flour of the earth cooked with honey and the other treatment until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
Of cauterization for numbness.
When any limb is numb or asleep and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and is not healed, then you cauterize that numb limb with cauteries, according to the size of the limb.
And let your cautery enter the skin a little bit. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes numbness is cauterized which affects the hands and feet, and the vertebrae of the back and the branches of the nerves which move that limb, and the numbness is removed.
However, do not presume on this unless you are knowledgeable about the anatomy of the limbs and the exit of the nerves that move the entire body.
Chapter 49.
Of the cauterization of ulcers that are morphea.
When an ulcer is chronic and you do not find any remedy in medicinal ointments, then you cauterize it with a cautery, slightly according to the extent of the ulcer's depth, until the whiteness and discoloration are removed, and its color is changed.
And then treat it with flour of barley and lentils, mixed with rosined oil and arnica leaves, and with a portion of dove's blood or swallow's blood, all mixed together.  Then, a cloth should be moistened and placed on the area until it heals.
Chapter 50.
Of cauterization of cancer.
When cancer is beginning and you wish to remove it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery around its circumference as it is coiled.
And the learned ones say it should be cauterized with the rear cautery in the middle of the area, but I have not seen that, for I am afraid it will become ulcerated, and I have already seen that happen several times.
And it is more appropriate that it be cauterized around its circumference with a circular cautery, as we have said, or with many cauteries.
Chapter 51.
Of cauterization of malignancies.
When a malignancy is delayed in its maturation, and this is due to the excess that produces it, or due to the patient's age, for they are old and decrepit, with little blood, or due to the passage of time, and you wish to hasten the malignancy's maturation, then cauterize around it with many small, puncturing cauteries.  And then leave it, for this will hasten its maturation.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then you heat the cautery, of which this is the form: and place it in the middle of the malignancy until it penetrates the skin.
And if the cautery is in a lower location, so that the arm's circulation is eased; and then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
On the cauterization of a corrosive (or eating) ulcer.
A corrosive is a wandering corruption in the limb, which consumes it as if a fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, if that corrosive is in a location suitable for cauterization with fire, then you should apply small, pointed cauteries, or larger ones depending on what is appropriate for the location of the corrosive.
And then cauterize it from all sides until you eradicate the entire corruption and no trace of it remains.
Then leave it for three days, and apply dressings of oil mixed with lint over the cauterized areas until all the scabs are removed and all the corruption is gone.
And then treat it with healing ointments that promote flesh growth.
But if after three days, healthy flesh grows in the areas where there was no corruption, it is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization on the remaining corrupted areas.
And sometimes it is treated with a sharp medicine, for that acts as a substitute for cautery. However, cauterization with fire is a more readily effective cure.
And I have already mentioned the treatment of this with sharp medicine in the division, therefore use that when you need it.
Chapter 53.
Of cauterization of wounds and other similar conditions.
Often in the parts below the affected foot, a disease called a "claw" develops, a serious, painful condition that afflicts the foot; and cauterization in those cases is done in two ways, that is, cauterization with fire and cauterization with hot water.
But cauterization with fire is done by igniting a concave cautery, resembling a small knife, made of iron sized to match the extent of the wound, and with fine, sharp edges.
Then place that heated cautery on the wound, and rotate your hand with the cautery around the wound until the cautery reaches the depth of the wound. And leave it there for three days until it begins to discharge pus. And then apply a poultice of crushed wild mallows with salt, and leave the poultice on it for one night, for this will eradicate it from its roots.
And then treat the area with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
And if this does not work, and this often happens on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, then you should apply cauterization with water.  Take a cannula of bronze, iron, or a hawk's feather, and place it over the wound; then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula.  Hold your hand steady, and rotate the cannula with your hand all around, pressing the edge of the cannula into the root of the wound.  The hot water will travel to the base of the wound.  Allow the patient to endure the hot water's effect for an hour, and then remove it, for the wound, with its roots, will be eradicated.
And thus do with these and then with the others until all of those [afflicted areas] on their body are treated.
And then treat those pus where the roots of the wounds are eradicated with ointments which generate flesh.
Chapter 44.
Of cauterization for tremors.
When someone has tremors due to cold in the nerves or a cold-causing fever, or another similar cause, then you should cauterize that with four or five cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, one between each vertebra; and with a cautery on the soles of the feet, and on the stomach with an olive-oil cautery.
And the tremor in him subsides and hastens the recovery from the cold illness.
Chapter 45.
Of cauterization for white spots (or lesions) on the body.
When white spots appear on the body, and pustules and sores are formed from corrupted thick matter, then at the beginning, as soon as it appears, it should be cauterized over the entire head of the fistula with a thin cautery made of myrtle wood, whose tip is heated; or with long resinous roots; or with a lens-shaped cautery.
And sometimes, cauterization at the beginning of its expulsion, in this manner, is also beneficial; and it is not believed, and the excess matter is broken down in this manner, and it heals.
However, this should be done near the time of evacuation by bloodletting.
Chapter 56.
Concerning the cauterization of accidental blood flow by archer's incision.
Very often, an arrow's blood flow, which is incised towards the wound that lies outside, or towards the incision of abscesses or cauterization of the limb, and similar instances, are difficult to control.
Therefore, if this happens to someone, then you should apply your hand to the opening of the arrow wound, and place your index finger over it, pressing it several times until the blood flow beneath your finger stops, and it no longer flows out.
Then place the small and large olive-shaped cauteries, many in number, in the fire, and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then take one of those, small or large, according to the location and the wound where the artery is broken, quickly place it over that vein after you have removed your finger, and hold the cautery there until the blood is cauterized and stopped.
And if the blood spurts from the artery's opening when you remove your finger and release the cautery, then take another cautery quickly from the ones prepared in the fire, and then continue doing this again and again until the blood flow is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve that is here, and to cause further harm to the patient.
And understand that when blood flows from the artery, it is not possible to stop it, especially if the artery is large, except by one of four methods: either with a cautery as we have said, or by extraction if it is not already extracted, for when it is extracted, the extremities of the patient are burned and the blood is stopped, or by tying it with a thread or bandage with a strong constriction, or by placing medicines, whose virtue is to stop the blood, on top of it; and these are pressed with a cushion with a proper constriction.
However, whoever administers constriction with bandages or by applying burning substances, does not achieve a prompt result.
But if this happens to someone and the physician is not present, nor is there any medicine, then one should, with a gentle pressure of the finger on the opening of the vein, as we have said, and applying a great constriction until the blood is stopped from the source of the wound, and the finger should not be removed from the artery; and over the artery should be sprinkled with very cold water until the blood is congealed and thickened; and one should seek more to use cauteries or medicine if needed.
Of incisions, perforations, cupping, phlebotomy, wounds, and similar conditions.
We have already discussed in the first chapter every illness for which cauterization with fire and/or burning medicines is appropriate, along with their causes and occasions, and the necessary instruments and forms of cauteries.  I have organized the chapters from the head to the feet in order of their importance.
And I will proceed in this chapter in the same manner, so that the seeker of this information may be aided in whatever they are inquiring about.
And before I begin this, children, understand that this chapter builds upon the first chapter on cauterization.
And therefore, great care is required, for in the operations of this chapter, many times there is evacuation of blood which sustains life, through the lancing of a vein, or incision over an abscess or opening of a fistula or healing of a wound, or removal of an arrow, or incision of a stone and similar procedures. And all of these are accompanied by fear and trembling, and in many of them lies death.
And I warn you that this does not fall upon you lightly, for sometimes there are numerous ways of dealing with the many diseases of men. And some of them become enraged by their illness, and death is near to them, and this is due to the severity of their ailment, and the length of their illness, and the illness itself is a fear which signifies death.
And of those is the one who gives you their wealth and says, "you will recover," and their illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not consent to that which comes to you in this manner; and your caution should be greater than your greed and your haste; and do not presume any cause upon this without certain knowledge that verifies it to you, and to which a praiseworthy outcome will follow.
Use prognostication and indication of the path to health in the treatment of all your patients, for in this is your aid in acquiring fame and memory, praise and glory.
O, children, may God inspire your understanding and not withhold direction and protection from you, for this is in His power.
And I have arranged this chapter, distinguishing it by sections, following the previous chapter on cauteries, from the care of the head to the feet, so that what you seek and need concerning this will be made easier for you.
First Chapter.
Of the treatment of water which is contained within the head of children.
Know that this illness often affects children during or after birth, and when the nurse presses the child's head too tightly.
And sometimes it arises due to hidden causes that are unknown.
However, I have only seen this illness in children, and all those I saw ended up dying, and thus, I have left the treatment of it to others.
And I have seen a child whose head was filled with water, and his head was always swelling until he could no longer support himself due to the size of his head, and further, that the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture either collects between the skin and the bone, or collects under the bone over the spine.
When the operation is on this area where the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then you should cut open the middle of the head with a lengthwise incision, and the incision should be about two fingers long until the moisture flows out.  This is the method of treatment for spatum:
And therefore, if the moisture is more extensive and the abscess is larger, then there are two incisions in this manner:
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, a sign of this is that you see the skull's sutures open on all sides, and the water is squeezed out when you press it with your hand on the inside edges; this is not hidden from you. You should cut open the middle of the head with three incisions in this manner:
And then extract all the moisture from the incision; then compress the incisions with cloths and dressings; then apply wine and oil to it until the fifth day; and then loosen the ligature and treat the wound with bandages and ointments; and bandage the head equally; and have the patient eat dry foods with little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And there is another way of incision, considering where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear, for sometimes it is more in the occipital region, or in the front part, or on the right, or on the left; and therefore, make the incision where the abscess and the fluid collection appear, and do it in the way that seems best to you.
And be careful not to cut the artery, lest blood flow out and the patient die from that blood loss while evacuating the moisture.
Second Chapter.
When there is a persistent cold or inflammation in the eyes or feet, and the usual treatments with medicine are not effective, then the final treatment in this case is the incision of those two arteries.
And therefore, when you want to incise those [arteries] where the patient has a swollen head, first rub the area with a rough cloth so that the arteries become apparent, and then wrap the patient's neck with a cloth; and then consider where the vein pulses; and mark the locations of each, which are near the ears, and these [arteries] are hidden, but in some men they are not.
And then press on the artery with a probe, and then incise each with a pointed knife, making an incision that penetrates to the bone, and let this be done according to the breadth of the head.
And if you wish, insert the knife below the artery and sever it at the points above.
And let the length of the incision be about two fingers together, for when any vein is incised, the blood comes out with a pulsating flow, which leaps outward in frequent spurts.
But if the artery is not apparent to the touch, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear the amount of space of three fingers, and then press with a probe, and penetrate to the bone.
And the amount of blood that needs to be drawn is about one ounce, though more or less is drawn easily, and all of this is according to the apparent strength of the patient and the fullness of their artery.
And then consider the wound, and if any cause of inflammation remains on the bone, then excise that so that a suppurating abscess does not develop.
And then press the wound with pledgets and bandages of linen, and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Of the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
When in some cases you encounter chronic headaches or acute fevers, caused by acrid humidities and the quality of the lacerations in the temples, or if a chronic and severe ailment resembling these is present, and it is not relieved by the usual medicinal treatments, we have found, in these illnesses, the extraction of the arteries in the temples, or cauterization of them, as we have shown, to be effective.
And the method of the operation and extraction of those arteries is that the patient removes the skin that is in the temples; and locate the artery appearing in the temple, because it is shown to you by its pulse, and is hidden except to a few people due to the intensity of cold.
And if it is hidden from you, then the patient's neck should be tightened with a cloth; and then rub the area with a rough cloth or apply warm water until the artery becomes clearly visible to you. And then you take the instrument, of which this is the form: And then you gently scrape the artery with that instrument until you reach the artery; and then carefully remove it from its surrounding tissues until you extract it from the artery and expose the layers beneath it, on all sides.
But if the artery is delicate, then wrap it with the tip of the instrument, and then cut a section from it according to the amount required to extend the ends and constrict them, and no blood flow will result. Because if not cut, blood will not fully ascend.
And then drain the blood by one to three ounces.
And if the artery is large, then you must tie it in two places with strong double-braided silk or strong cord, and this will prevent putrefaction before the wound heals and prevent blood flow.
And then cut away the excess tissue between the two ligaments; and do this at that time or at another time.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery until the bone is reached, with a sharp cautery until its edges are cut, and it will remain in place of the same operation.
And it is a better course of action if the patient does not have a fever or is not of a hot constitution; for cauterization is from those things that help to eliminate moistures, and therefore is better in convalescence.
And it is necessary after the removal of the arteries that the area be filled with old cotton, and then be placed over it with well-made powders, and then treated with drying medicines that promote flesh growth and with some other remedies until it is healed.
And if, while operating, a flow of blood from the artery occurs again, then you should try to stop the blood, either with a cautery or by filling the area with cotton, and pressing firmly with your hand until the blood is stopped. And if none of those things are available to you, then place your finger over the area until the blood has congealed, and apply water of intense coldness until the activity subsides and it is constricted as needed.
And the easiest way to remove an artery is to cauterize the vein with this cautery, having two blades.  Having marked the two spots with a probe, and carefully placing each, very hot, until they reach the bone and divide the vein in two places, so that what is between its extremities is stretched out, since these are not fully consolidated with this cautery.
And this is the form of the cautery:
And the sharpness of the two blades be equal. However, if the sharpness is less than that of two blades, it is better, for if it is sharp like two blades it is close to burning each time, and does not cut the flesh quickly.
Therefore, since there is some thickness in those places, the heat of the fire is retained there and the flesh is separated easily.
And this method is better than any other method and quicker.
And the space between the two blades should be the width of a finger.
Fourth Chapter.
Of the treatment of acute and acrid humors in the body.
When the humors are acrid, and they affect the veins around the head, and you find no medicinal cause for the cure, and sometimes the patient's face reddens and they feel a crawling sensation on their forehead as if ants were crawling, and the eyes are weakened, moist, and the skin is irritated, and the eyelids are inflamed by the acridness of the tears, the ancient physicians in the treatment of such cases used this method, and called this treatment 'spassa'.
And you order the patient to shave the skin on their forehead, and then you make three equally spaced incisions on the forehead, along its length. And if the length of the incision is two fingers wide, the first incision should be precisely positioned along the length of the nose, in the middle of the forehead, the second along the length of the eye sockets a little way from the tear ducts, and the third on the other side.
And be careful not to injure the arteries that run on both sides, and keep your hand away from the space between both jaws. And if the space between each incision is the size of three fingers laid end to end.
And if you have a piece of prepared sponge or very dry cloths with which to absorb the blood.
And then insert the spatula with two sharp ends, and this is the form of the incision on it: that is from the temple area up to the second incision. Scrape with that all the clot that is between the two incisions, along with the clot that is on the bone, and then do this from the middle incision to the second incision. And then remove this spatula and insert another instrument, called a scalpel, sharp on one side and blunt on the other, into the first incision again.
And place the sharp edge of that instrument against the flesh adjacent to the clot, and the blunt edge inside the bone, and push it in until it reaches the middle fissure, and cut with that all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes.
But the incision should be continued to this appearance of the clot, and then do this in the middle fissure until the other fissure.
And since a quantity of blood flows, express the places of the congealed blood clots.
And then place in each fissure lint from old cloth, and place upon those powders mixed with wine, oil, and sour wine, and be wary that it does not become a hot abscess.
And on the third day, loosen the ligament and administer a large infusion of warm or lukewarm water, and then treat it with basilicon ointment mixed with rubbed oil and others until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 5.
Of the treatment of ulcers by cauterization and of sores descending from parts inside the head.
When a person has sharp and persistent cauterization, and the sores on the patient are small, and their healing is weak, and the eyelids are ulcerated and their skin is damaged, and there is acute pain and subsequent weakening in the area of the head, then know by these symptoms that the materials and cauterization come only from many veins originating from the parts inside.
And the best treatment in these cases is this: order the patient to shave their forehead, and then make a small incision in the middle of the forehead, (starting with a cut from the left temple towards the right temple; and the incision should go down to the bone; and move your hand away from the temple cuts, which are weak toward the chewing area), until the bone is exposed.
Dry all the blood with a sponge or a dry cloth, and then fill the two edges of the incision with old cotton or linen cloths. And then apply a poultice made from powders mixed with wine and oil to prevent a hot abscess from forming.
And when you remove this and see that the abscess has lessened, then you must rub the bone until new flesh begins to grow, and then treat it with a drying regimen to promote the growth of flesh, such as taking two parts wheat flour and four parts colophony, mixing them, and creating a plaster from this to be applied to these wounds to stimulate the birth of flesh.
Chapter 6.
Concerning the treatment of that which afflicts the ears.
Every affliction in the ears belongs to one of four types: either it is a mineral, or something similar to a mineral, such as iron or glass; or it is from a plant, like a thorn or a bean; or it is a flowing substance, such as water, sour wine, and things similar; or some sort of animal.
And therefore, when a stone or a similar substance that does not grow or adhere within the ear enters the ear,  and you expose the ear to the sun, and if you move the stone, then instill into it some violet oil or mustard, and then apply extraction of it, and make it sneeze with some substance, and block the nostrils to support the sneezing event. Then place some pieces of cloth or wool around the ear, and gently stretch the ear outward, because often this material is forced out with these methods.
And if it is not expelled, then apply extraction with the instrument called a *gesti epicicarios subtil*, of which this is the form:
And if it is expelled with the *gesti*, good; and if not, then apply extraction with a thin, small spoon (a *onci subtil de pauca duplicacio*).
And if it is not expelled by that, then make a brass cannula and insert the tip of the cannula into the ear opening, and seal around the cannula with softened wax and oil, so that only the cannula is open. And then extract with your breath with strong suction, for often this, with what we have described, works.
But if it is not expelled in this manner, then take a heated iron (or, alternately, a device for holding birds) a small amount, and place it at the end of the wound, and then wrap it with appropriately placed cloth. And then insert that into the ear opening easily, and then dry the ear of moisture.
And therefore, if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then you should consider cutting it before it becomes a festering boil or spasm.
And the way to cut it is that you first bleed the patient from the sephasic vein and draw blood according to its virtue, and then make the incision between your hands and turn the ear outwards, and make a small incision in the root of the ear towards its base, at the well-pressed place. And let the incision be in the shape of a crescent until it reaches the stone, and once the stone is found, remove it if you can with appropriate instruments; and then close the incision at that moment quickly, and heal it until it is healthy.
And if the cause of the ear affliction is one of the growing and swollen masses, then administer its cure and extraction; but if you do not succeed in that way, then you should take a thin spatum according to this method: and administer with that the excision of that mass which has fallen into the ear.
And do not do this when you think that it is already moistened by the ear's vapor, and break it into small pieces, and carefully remove them with tweezers or with a subtle action, or with an incision as we have said, because the extraction is thus eased.
But in the case of water that has entered the ear, the patient should initially use sneezing with care, and fill their ear with some old cloth, and lie on that ear in which the water is, and if by this it is forcibly expelled, it is good; and if not, then take small, smooth stones according to the length of the finger, and heat them slightly in the fire, and the patient should insert one of them into the hole of their ear and place one foot on that side and strike with another stone on the stone that is inside the ear. Therefore, do not cease this with a stone until all the water is expelled.
Therefore, do not cease this with a stone until all the water is expelled. And when the water is thus expelled, you should take some paper or cloth, put one end in the ear, and hold the other end with fire until the majority of it is burnt.
And then put in more cloth and do this repeatedly until all the water is expelled, and remove it with a cannula as previously described for the stone extraction method.
However, in the extraction of an animal entering the ear, if it is small, like a worm or something similar, treat it as we have described in the division; but if it is large and causes visible distress, then administer its extraction with tools and forceps, and it is easier to deal with than any other things which enter the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm that has grown in the ear, when you treat it with what we have discussed in the division and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not work, then you should expose the ear to the sun.
And therefore, if any sign of worms appears, then extract it with fine forceps or tweezers; but if no sign appears, then take a cannula, narrow at the bottom and wider at the top, of which this is the shape: And insert the narrow end into the ear according to the amount that the patient can tolerate; and then suck strongly with it, and do this many times, until all the worms are expelled.
But if it does not yield to this treatment, then you should seal and close around the cannula with wax as we have said; then administer distillations that the ancients have used in treating worms, and you will find this in the treatise on distillations.
And let your application be with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear with this instrument, of which this is the form: Make it of silver or brass, narrow at the bottom, with a small hole, and broad at the top.
And therefore, it should be as if pressing into the cavity of the cannula where it is necessary. And if you wish, take a rod and wrap its end neatly in cotton, then pour oil into the cavity of the instrument, or the juice of any herb, or whatever medicine you want, into the cannula—and this is in the ear—then insert the rod with cotton on top and press with your hand gently until the oil is expelled into the cavity of the instrument, and the patient feels the oil inside.
And whatever is to be inserted into the ear should be warmed a little with fire. And be careful that the substance you are distilling into the ear is not too cold, for ear medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
Of the treatment of ear congestion.
Sometimes children inherit this from their mothers' wombs, and this happens in some men, congestion in the ear canal due to a plague or some flesh growing there; and this congestion sometimes occurs at the opening of the ear canal, preventing discharge, and sometimes it is on the outer parts.
And that which occurs at the opening, preventing discharge, is in the most difficult situations to cure.
And in that which allows discharge, you should place the patient's ear in the sun and observe it. And therefore if congestion appears, open it with a subtle instrument, of which this is its form:
If its extremity, where the width is small, has one sharp side and the other not; and the instrument has two leathers [or straps] so that it does not damage the ear.
And therefore if the congestion is due to flesh grown in the ear or in its canal, then remove it with fine hooks and cut it away with forceful ease until all the flesh is gone.
And if the congestion is in the front of the ear, then take a thin, pointed [or sharp] instrument and heat it a little with fire, and then place it in the ear canal.
And therefore, if you have sensed congestion with which the patient has difficulty hearing, ease the oppression with gentleness; and be careful not to injure the nerves of the ear.
Then place a cloth, in proportion to the width of the hole that you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment, in the ear, and do this for a few days until you are certain of the wound's healing, or take some [substance] and pulverize it over it, rubbing it in, and use it. And if the ear has already become suppurated, then you must extract the substance and replace it with another substance wrapped in a plaster made from heated oil, until the abscess subsides; and then treat it until it is healed.
But if blood flows, then immerse a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear, and use other treatments that restrict the flow of blood, which is mentioned in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8.
Of the treatment of a swelling that comes to the eyelids.
Swellings that appear on the eyelids of the eyes sometimes are moist, sometimes are dry.
Therefore, you should hold them with forceps or tweezers, and separate them from their roots with a knife, and then apply a compress to the affected areas.
But if pain with blood flow occurs, then cauterize those areas with a small, lens-shaped cautery.
And the best cauterization is near the incisions, and often they return if any root cause remains; and when cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
Treatment of swelling that occurs on the eyelids.
Often on the eyelids there is a cause resembling swelling in its strength and hardness, and therefore it is called swelling.
And it is a collection of thick moisture on the eyelid above and below.
Therefore, the procedure in these cases is to consider whether the swelling is originating from something visible on the eyelid and spread to all parts; and the cause of this is slight.
Therefore, make a cut across it at the width, then gently scrape it from all sides until it gives way, then lift it with forceps and cut it off.
And if the figure is large, then you should stitch it up and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will not suffer any harm, as the ointment will restore and close it.
And if the size is receding towards the inside of the eyelid, away from the surface, then open the eyelid, lift the size with forceps, rather than making an incision, and press it from all sides.  If the eyelid is stretched by an incision, this does not harm in any way.
Then, after the incision, wash the wound with saltwater and treat the area with something that closes it until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Concerning the treatment of a swelling on the eyelid above the eye.
A swelling is a sore that forms on the eyelid above the eye, particularly among children, irritating the eyes of those afflicted and causing them trouble in the morning; and they cannot bear the sunlight because of this, as their tears collect there.
And so you often find them lying on their faces at all times, sleeping on their faces; and their eyelids underneath their eyebrows are damp, already covered with inflammation, and an imminent swelling is apparent in the eye; and that when you press the area with your fingers, you feel swelling there.
And a method of treatment is that the patient rests their head on your lap, then place a cloth over them and warm it, and make a circle of the cloth that fits the swelling on all sides, and then place this over the swelling and press with your fingers so that the swelling is contained within the circle, and then open a small hole in the middle of the cloth over the dampness with a slit, and the slit should not be larger than the slit made in a lancing incision.
But in the process, you must cut the skin completely until you reach the swelling, for in most cases, the swelling develops from a white grease-like substance.
Then remove it with cloths that you have wrapped around it with your hands on either side, and roll it between your right and left hands until it disappears.
And be careful not to press too hard on the slit so that with the pressure you do not pierce the swelling and damage it.
But if the swelling does not appear at the beginning of the slit, but you gently press on the slit until swelling occurs; and then remove it as we have said; and then soak cloths in warm water and vinegar and place them over the area and compress them with powders.
And some people sprinkle salt and place it in the cavity of the slit to absorb the remaining moisture.
And then treat it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess occurs in the area, then treat it with cooling poultices until it is healed.
Chapter 11.
Methods of treating the eye.
When in the eyelids of the eyes, swelling occurs due to inflammation beneath the natural skin and it is prolonged, then that irritates the eye and causes various ailments, like constant tears, softening of the eyelids and surrounding tissue, until it causes damage to the eye, which is called eye inflammation.  It is treated in four ways: either by cauterization with fire; or with a sharp medicine, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization; or inflammation is treated by incision and suturing; or by using tubes (or cannulae), as I will explain.
Therefore, you should place the patient's head on your lap; then, gently hold the eyelid with your left hand, and if it is already open that's fine; but if not, carefully insert a needle into the part where the pus is collected just below the eyelid, and push the needle with pus upwards and outward, close to the edge of the eyelid where the eyelashes begin and close to the eyelashes themselves. Pull the pus upwards along with the eyelid, and hold the eyelid open with your instrument.
Then, in the area beneath the eyelid, along the inflamed skin, make an incision from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct; then remove the pus and place small cotton or linen powders underneath the eyelid.  Then gently cover the eyelid with a bandage, shaped like a myrtle leaf.
However, the shape of the bandage should be adapted to the amount of swelling in the eyelid, as this varies between individuals.  In those cases where significant incision of the eyelid is needed, it should be proportionate to the degree of softening of the eyelid.
Then, using the scalpel, make an incision along the two lines you have marked, beginning at the larger tear duct and extending to the smaller one. And if your incision is close to the natural eyelashes, it should be as wide as the king's thumb.
And then place the forceps on one of the two corners of the wound, and then scrape the entire area clean.  Then, use stitches to close the two edges of the wound with fine wool or lint, and wipe away any blood.  If desired, adhere any excess lint to the eyebrows with some adhesive material, but if not, don't worry about it.
And leave the stitches in place for three or four days, and then treat the wound.
And if you wish, leave the wound without stitches, and treat it with something that dries and constricts it, because the eyelid is raised due to the sealing of the wound and the accumulation there; but stitches are better.  Thus say the ancients of yore.
But nonetheless, be diligent in your care of the patient, and it is a good action; and there should be no fear involved.
And there is another method in treating this, which is to shape the eyelid like a myrtle leaf, and then lift the eyelid using three forceps, joined and positioned in this way:
But if that is not possible, holding the forceps, and this is not appropriate, then take a needle with thread and place it in the middle of the shape, and insert the other stitch near the larger tear duct, and the second stitch near the smaller tear duct, and adjust the stitches evenly between your fingers.  Then, with a firm lift, raise the entire flap that is shaped as we have described.
And then close the two edges of the wound with stitches, and treat it until it is healed.
But if an abscess develops near your incision or fissure, treat that abscess with poultices and similar remedies, and with ointments.
And sometimes, the eyelashes might need to be trimmed. In that case, use what we have said about incisions; however, a stitch in each is more artful/delicate.
To treat with canes is according to this form: and it is that you turn the eyelid and cut the figure within according to what I have recounted; and then, artfully make two thin canes or sticks, whose length should be according to the length of the eyelid and whose width should be smaller than the width of the space. And you have already incised at the extremities of each part, where you have the threads/stitches.
Then carefully add each one to the superfluous part of the eyelid, and tightly bind the canes to each part, and leave it for a few days, because the flesh will die and be denied and rot until it falls off by itself.
But if it has lingered, then break it with forceps, and then treat the wound until it is healed.
Therefore, when it is solidified, the eyelid is raised and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the method for two canes which you will make according to this form: and have the quantity of the length and width neither more nor less.
Chapter 12.
Of the lifting of pricking hairs in the boil with a needle when the hair is hard.
When there are unnatural, hard, rough, and prickling hairs in the boils, then the operation on them is that you take a fine needle and place it in that delicate strip of silk and linen, and then that you carefully adjust the ends of it, twisting each in a very delicate way. And the length of the strips of thread should be about the length of a palm.
And then you place another fine thread beneath it briefly, and tie the ends of that one if you wish.
And then place the patient's head on your lap, and let it be near the sun so that your work is clearly visible, for it is a subtle and delicate operation.
And then introduce the needle into the root of the adjusted hair, which needs skill, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you pull that hair out above the natural hair.
And then pull the hair out above it and put it in a small container, and let the hairs be one or two or three, and no more.
And then remove your hand with it until the hair is pushed out, along with the small container, from the eyelid, away from the natural hair.
But if the small container is pulled out and the hair is not expelled, you will pull the container in small parts downwards with the ointment which you have prepared with that, until the small container is ejected from the hole above.
And remove it from the eye's sight, for then you will throw away the container and the ointment, and everyone washes and closes the eye, and leave it closed for two or three days, until the hair adheres to its place and good flesh grows over it.
And if the hair is shorter, then you attach a long hair from the natural ones, and lift with that, or let it until it is stretched to some distance, and then you lift it.
Chapter 13.
Of the treatment of an abrasion which appears on the eyelid above.
When an abrasion occurs in the eye, and the eyelid is contracted above, either on the right or left side, due to poor healing from a wound, incision, or cautery, and it is called leporine, and this abrasion is either natural or accidental, and is caused by the poor healing of wounds, incisions, or cauteries, or by similar poorly healed wounds.
The method of treatment for it is to break apart that scar tissue and separate the edges, and place a piece of linen cloth between them, and tie them together until they are healed.
And you should not use treatments that dry or constrict it; for if you do, the abrasion will return worse than it ever was before.
Use remedies that soften it, such as fenugreek, and apply it with water in which marshmallow and flax seeds are soaked, or diachylon ointment, which has been liquefied with some oil.  Soak a cloth in this mixture and use it for treatment.
And the key to your treatment is to break open any unhealed area according to the original form of the wound.
And this procedure, which we have described, is merely a correction of the abrasion, returning the area to its previous form.
Chapter 14.
Of the treatment of an abrasion on the lower eyelid.
This abrasion on the lower eyelid, properly called an abrasion, can be either a natural or an accidental injury.
But an accidental abrasion is caused by a blow, or a cut, or a cautery, or other similar injuries.
And the method of treating it is that you take a needle with a double thread, and insert it into the flesh, and have it pass from the left lacrimal duct to the right lacrimal duct, until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then spread the flesh apart with the needle and stitch it with the needle, and cut with a broad spatula. And thus, if the eyelid returns to its proper shape, it is good; and if not, take a spatula and place it in the split opening, and with it bring the eyelid back together, and close the two fissures in the lower eyelid.
And let the ends of the two fissures be two angles of the incision you have made until they meet, and let their junction be an angle, so that when they come together, their shape is formed.
And this shape is the Greek letter: lambda.
And then remove the flesh from that area, the amount that is the sharp edge beneath the eyelid.
And then bring the separated parts together and sew them with wool thread; and then treat them with the softening medicines and poultices we mentioned until healed.
And if a laceration is to be treated by fissure, suture, or cautery, then you should make a flat fissure on the eyelid skin as previously described. And then separate with a poultice between the two lips, as we have described.
In the summary of the treatment of lacerations, whether above or below, let the operation proceed according to the preparation of the laceration, and this is in various forms.
And the skilled craftsman always prepares instruments in every possible way until the shape returns to the natural form, or nearly so, and the patient according to what is appropriate for the operation and the instruments in the best arrangements.
Chapter 15.
Of the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
When this adhesion occurs in many such cases where the eyelid is adhered to the white of the eye, and is prevented from its natural movement and functions, then you must insert the tip of a probe under the eyelid, and lift or extend it with a forceps.
And then you carefully incise the adhesion with a blunt instrument, avoiding sharp incisions so that the patient is not disturbed by the procedure, and leave the instrument so that it does not injure the eye with its sharpness.
And your incision should carefully sever the adhesion until the eyelid returns to its natural shape and all the adhesion is removed.
And then you place saline water in the eye with which you wash it; or you distill rosewater dissolved in the water.
And then you separate the eyelid and the eye with a cloth, and place on the eye wool soaked in egg white, and then on the third day use healing substances until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Concerning incisions of the nail and protruding flesh of the lacrimals.
Know that a nail is of two types, either it is nervous, and is like a hard, fine, pointed thing; or it is non-nervous, resembling frozen white moisture, which, when touched with an iron, or if forced to be taken with a forceps, it is cut off and the forceps are not stuck in it.
And both types (of nail) begin only from the larger lacrimal, gradually spreading to cover the eye and obstruct the light and movement of the eye.
But the method of operating on it (the nail) is that the patient should rest their head on your shoulder, then open their eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand, and then grasp the nail with forceps of little curvature and gently pull it away from the surrounding area. Then take a needle and thread it with horse, ox, or cow hair, or similar strong thread, or twist the tip of the needle a little and place it in the middle of the nail, then pierce it with the needle and tie the nail with the thread; and gently pull it away from the surrounding area, and peel away the layer of nail that covers the pupil. And thus you secure it (the nail) with the thread all the way back.
Then you cut the remaining part at the root of the lacrimal gland with a thin blade or small scissors, leaving the natural tissue of the lacrimal gland intact, so that cutting it doesn't cause a continuous flow of tears.
And sometimes the nail, using only forceps or thread, as we have said, and then scraped with a lion's-claw-shaped scraper (the instrument), which has this form:
And be careful that with the scraper you do not touch the cornea and cause a rupture, and that the pupil is pierced.

And if the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle, or if it is not embedded in the nail bed, then often this type of nail is treated with medicines and is healed as I have described in the section.
And if you want to incise it, then you open the wound of the patient and take a sharp, slender lion's-claw scraper of this shape: with subtlety. And then repeat your procedure on it with reason until it is healed.
If a swelling of the tear-duct flesh is seen in the wound, causing great harm, it should be suspended with a bandage; and you should cut it back in one part, but do not extend the incision so as not to cause a flow of tears.
And then instill into the tear duct red ochre and cinnabar until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Concerning the incision of a boil and what arises from hardened flesh in the wound.
Sometimes in the wounds of some people red flesh grows, and it overgrows until it covers the pupil, or it approaches or tightens on the eyelids, and forcefully presses the eyelids inwards and outwards, and is shaped like the teeth of a battlement.
But the operation on him is that the patient rests their head on your lap; then open the wounds of that area and collect all that flesh with appropriate forceps or tweezers, hold it with a forceps or tongs; then slice the flesh in a continuous motion until it is finished with the scalpel, and be careful not to damage the wound during your operation.
And your incision should be done with the same instrument as used in cutting the nail, or with small scissors, with which the tissue is removed, according to the form described earlier in this chapter.
So, once your incision is complete and all the flesh has been removed, then fill the wound with crushed salt, or apply red powder similar to corrosive medicines, and place a cloth with egg white over the wound until it is securely protected from a festering inflammation.
And if some lingering cause of inflammation remains and a hot, painful swelling is feared, then leave the wound and treat that inflammation with whatever resolves that swelling, and subsequently repeat the operation until it heals.  Do the same for any inflamed flesh that occurs in the wound of this type.
However, you should avoid all treatment of the wound through natural or accidental means involving iron, and do not place your hand on any of these operations until you see that the limb is enduring the operation.

Pus comes out of the inflamed areas that are swollen above the wound, and forbids treatment to its exterior, and the wound should be treated with extended days.

Chapter 19.
Of the reduction of a nasal injury.
When you treat a nasal injury with a cautery or with a sharp, burning medicine, according to the narrative that preceded it, and it is not healed, then it is not enough to treat the affected area. And place that on the corrupted part of the bone, then gently rotate it between your fingers, and press your hand little by little until you know that the corruption is scraped away.

Chapter 20.
Of the reduction of the prominence of the hole/opening.
When the swelling/bulge emerges completely and you do not encounter any harmful or diminishing effects during the appearance, then you must let the patient rest, and then follow it, and then apply leeches to the neck without incisions and apply suction well; and then apply a liniment made of castor, aloe, and incense and sarcocolla to the swelling, and then press the plaster over the swelling with powdered materials.

Chapter 21.
Of the incision of the eminence of the uvea tunic.

Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of gout.
This ailment, called gout, is nothing but a fluid that has gathered in a swelling, like descending water, but is not that.
But the method of treatment is to have the patient sit on a prepared and upright chair, and then take their head in your hands in two places and gently hold it until you see the fluid emerging to the lower parts, and then let it dry and not descend, and block the light, and let the patient see the causes as they would see them.
And if it does not descend lower, know that it is water.
But if the descent of the fluid is not prepared with what we have said, then make the patient sit between your hands and then take a fine, dry cloth and apply it to the inflamed area slightly inside the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea in the corner (into the empty area) until the fluid is expelled.
Then, once it is expelled, you instill warm water into the swelling, in which you have mixed honey or water in which you have boiled fenugreek and honey.
And then treat with the other remedies until it is healed.

Chapter 24.
Of the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
When various fleshy growths appear in the nose, some of which are caused by something resembling a very heavy scorpion or a fish-like polyp; and this illness is called polyps because it resembles that described fish. And from it grows cancerous, ulcerated flesh, dark in color; and from it is also leonine flesh, not dark in color.
Therefore, in those fleshy growths that are not fraudulent or cancerous, you should place the patient between your hands, expose them to the sun, and clear their nostrils, and prick those growths with your lancet. Then extract that to the exterior, and then cut what you have grasped of those growths with a sharp, thin spatula, until you know that all the flesh is removed; for if any part remains from which the excision is possible, then you scrape it with one of the sharp instruments with ease until no part remains. But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that inside it is flesh or pus adhering to the excess bones of the nose to which the excision has not reached.
Therefore, it is necessary to introduce a piece of lint to which some thickness is given, and use many pieces, and place the amount of a finger or less between each piece.  Then bind the patient and insert one end of the lint into the nose with a stick or other appropriate implement.
And after this has been done, draw the lint in until it reaches the channels of the nose, and it comes out over their throat, because many times children in schools do something similar to this action, and it's an easy thing to do for anyone who wants to.

Then remove the lint, and place a poultice over the nasal area, in the immediate vicinity of the wound, and do this for three days or more until the poultice has completely consumed all remaining flesh.
And finally, place a lead cannula in the nose for several days until it is healed.

Chapter 25.
Of the ointments for the nasal extremities.
Often ointment is applied to the nasal extremities, and it is increased and augmented over several days until it becomes very obvious.
And therefore, you should cut it at the beginning of its appearance, and remove all of it. And then place a cautery or a burning medicine in its place.
However, if its incision is delayed until it has grown large, then consider if it is hardened, hard, dark colored, and with little feeling.  If so, do not touch it with iron, for it is a deep-seated abscess; for often the one who incises such an abscess brings great harm upon the one it was affecting.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch and not dark-colored, and if incision is possible throughout it, then drain the patient and cut it without fear, and treat the area with whatever dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suturing of the nose, lips, and ear when the continuity is broken by plague or similar causes.
Know that when you encounter a break in continuity in one of these cartilages, you achieve little in that operation except for some men (or: in some cases).
Therefore, when you encounter any of those causes, consider if it is fresh plague with its blood, and if so, you should bring together the two sides of the wound with sutures, then treat it until healed.
And if the edges of the broken skin are already separated and each is healthy, then you must scrape each side of their edges until blood comes out. Then bring together the two edges with sutures, and tighten them.  Powder the area with dragon's blood and crushed herbs, and place a piece of diachronic plaster, or another type of consolidative plaster, over the powder.  Leave it bound for two or three days, then remove it and change the plaster, and leave it until the pus is separated, then treat with ointment until healed.
The method of suturing is that you either join the broken skin with needles, as we have described in the suturing of the belly, or you join the broken skin with glue, as has been shown to you.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those things that are found in the two edges of the internal parts.
Often, hard abscesses resembling grains of millet form on the inner parts of the lips in men, and some are smaller, some larger.
Therefore, you must turn the lips inside out and remove each of those hardnesses completely, and then, after the excision, fill the place with some crushed sage until the bleeding is stopped, and then wash the area with sour wine, and treat the place with a styptic until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of the incision of the flesh adhering to the gums.
Often, flesh adhering to the gums, which the ancients called "albulus," forms.  In that case, you should detach it with forceps or scissors, and sever it at its base, allowing the discharge (pus or blood) to drain.
And then apply crushed sage or other styptic or astringent powders to the area.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it again and cauterize it, for the cautery will prevent it from returning.
Chapter 29.
Of tooth extraction with an iron.
When on the surface of the teeth, both on the inner and outer parts and between the gums, rough, hard, and sometimes black, yellowish, or greenish scabs form, causing a corruption of the gum and making the teeth brittle.
And therefore, you should place the patient between your hands, rest their head on your knee, and scrape the teeth where these scabs and similar, gritty substances appear until no trace of them remains, thereby removing the blackness, yellowness, and other such impurities.
Therefore, if you have removed what was present at the beginning of the procedure, it is good; but if not, then you repeat the procedure the next day, and the day after that, and again on the third day until the condition reaches the desired state.
And know that teeth require many different types of rasps and shapes, depending on the preparation for your operation. And therefore, the rasp used to scrape the inner side of the tooth is different from the rasp used to scrape between the teeth, according to other shapes, and these rasp shapes must be prepared by you; you will see them shortly.
Chapter 30.
Of the eradication of teeth.
You must treat the tooth's pain with all possible means and be slow in any tooth extraction, for there is no cause that compensates for the tooth's being removed, for it is a subtle substance until it is removed from the extraction.
However, when the patient insists on extracting the tooth, you must be absolutely certain which tooth is diseased, for many times the pain deceives the patient and seems to be in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one, and the pain does not cease until the diseased and painful tooth is extracted, for we have already seen this through the operation of barbers.
Therefore, how should you be certain which tooth is diseased, and that you know it well?  Then you should scarify the area around the tooth with a substance possessing some virtue, until the tooth and the gum are separated on all sides, and then work at it with your tools, or with small, sharp forceps, bit by bit, until it yields.
And then, use strong pliers for a good grip or leverage, and place the patient's head between your knees to hold it firmly so it doesn't move.  Then pull the tooth straight, in a manner that does not break it, nor cause the patient excessive pain.
And if it is not fully removed, then take one of those instruments and place it beneath it from all sides with ease, and try to remove it gently as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or decayed, then you should cover that hole with cloth and compress it with a fine, thin instrument so that it does not crack during the time you are using the pliers to extract it.
And you should carefully open the gum surrounding the tooth with incisions on all sides, and be very careful to not break it and leave a portion of it remaining, and return to the patient with the malice that the new pain is greater than the first.
Beware that you do not do as some ignorant barbers do in their rashness and audacity regarding tooth extraction, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they cause great illnesses in people that are more difficult to cure than the original problems, and the roots of the teeth remain, some or all, or they extract them with some of the jawbone, as we have often testified.
And after the extraction, rinse the mouth with warm wine or vinegar with salt.
And if bleeding occurs afterward, as often happens, then apply a small quantity of alum and sprinkle it on the wound; and if that alum doesn't help, cauterize it.
The shape of the forceps and delicate pliers with which the tooth is initially grasped should be long at the extremity, with a short, thick handle, so they do not bend when working with them.
And they should be like those with thick handles, so that when you squeeze with them, they don't bend; and have short extremities.
And they should be made of Indian iron, well-made with straight extremities, and on the ends of those are teeth that interlock and grip with a strong constriction.  Sometimes the extremities of those are shaped like the beak of a stork, or a kind of hooked shape, and they are strongly constricting.
