Chapter 32.
Of the treatment of teeth protruding above the others.
When teeth protrude beyond their natural position due to irregular growth, especially in women, and particularly in the most venerable young women;  therefore you must consider: if the tooth is close to another tooth, and separation or trimming is not possible, then extract it; but if it is not connected to another tooth, then cut it away with this instrument, which has the shape of a pig's snout, and is made of Indian iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision, with which you cut it, be gradual over several days, due to the tooth's hardness, so that it does not damage the other teeth.
And if the protruding tooth is suitable for filing, then file it with an Indian iron file, of which this is the shape:
And let it be entirely of Indian iron, easily sharpened; and let it have very small points, just like the file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over many days with ease, so that no other tooth is damaged or broken. And then trim it at the end, and extract the root with some razors.
And if a part of the tooth is fractured and injures the tongue, then it is necessary that you file it until the roughness of the fracture is removed and smoothed, and is made even so that the tongue is not injured nor the speech corrupted.
Chapter 33.
Of the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver filings.
When, due to blows to the front teeth, there is a concussion and displacement through impact or forceful striking, and the patient cannot hold onto what is missing, and you have treated it with astringent medicines, but your treatment has been unsuccessful, then, to avoid decay, it is better to use gold filings to strengthen the parts, and not silver; for silver turns green and can quickly rot after a few days, whereas gold does not change in any way.
And the filings should be so fine and granular, according to the amount that can fit between the teeth; and the method of application is to take the filings and place double the amount between two healthy teeth, and then, using two twists of the filings, fill between the broken teeth, whether one or several, until it reaches the healthy tooth on the other side. And then gently, repeatedly press it back to the starting point, holding it firmly and skillfully until it is no longer broken.
And your grip should be strong enough to hold the filings against the tooth roots, so they don't fall out.
And then, using forceps or scissors, cut off any excess filings, and carefully place them in each gap, shaping and hiding them between the healthy tooth and the broken tooth, so as not to injure the tongue. And then leave it like that, firmly in place.
But if it is loose or broken, hold it or them with another filing in such a way, all the time.
And this is the method for teeth and the technique of joining two healthy teeth and two broken ones, as seen in these examples:
And when one or two teeth are repositioned into their sockets, and are reattached as we have described, and remain in place.
And only skillful and knowledgeable artisans can do this.
And sometimes a piece of cow bone is shaped like a tooth and placed in the gap where the tooth is missing, and is secured as we have described, and remains and serves in that place for a long time.
Chapter 34.
Concerning the inflammation of the ligament that affects the tongue and inhibits speech.
This ligament, which sometimes lies beneath the tongue, is either natural, present from birth, or accidental, caused by a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it involves opening the patient's mouth, their head being held by you; lifting their tongue, and then cutting that nervous ligament along its width until the tongue is freed from its constriction.
But if there is any hardness or knotting there, and this is due to the healing of a wound, then you use forceps and cut it along its width until you have severed the ligament and the knot has been dissolved.
And be careful not to create a tear in the underlying flesh, so that you don't accidentally cut a blood vessel and cause a flow of blood.
And then wash the area of the incision with water or with vinegar or with cold water; then place a linen pad underneath the tongue; this is a device that the patient will keep under their tongue every night to prevent it from adhering again.
But if a flow of blood occurs, apply powdered vitriol to the area; however, if the bleeding persists, then cauterize the area with a smooth cautery iron suitable for this purpose. And then treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
Of the extraction of a ranula under the tongue.
Sometimes, an abscess resembling a small tumor forms under the tongue, hindering the tongue's natural function; and it grows significantly until it fills the mouth.
And the procedure involves opening the patient's mouth, sitting them facing the sun, and examining the abscess.
And if you see it is dark colored or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation, then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it has a whitish hue, indicating moisture, then you place a lancet on it and cut it with a very thin scalpel, and sever it completely.  If blood flows during your procedure, then place a crushed piece of egg white, or vitriol, on it until the bleeding stops.
Then return to your work until you have thoroughly cleaned it, washing it with sour wine and salt.  And treat it with the other appropriate remedies until it heals.
Chapter 36.
Of the extraction of abscesses from both ears, and those found on the neck, and other abscesses.
When there is an abscess on the neck, these are glands which are connected together (likely swollen).  They protrude outwards and are called "two ears." If you treat them with what we have previously discussed and they do not heal, then consider whether the abscess is hard, dark-colored, and with little sensitivity.  In such a case, do not touch it with an iron (instrument).
And if it is reddish in color and the root is thick, then do not touch it with an iron for the flow of blood.  Rather, allow it to mature, and then either you will drain it or it will break open on its own.
And if it is white in color, soft, and its root is subtle, then it is necessary to make an incision.
And the operation on it is that you should consider, before your operation, if the abscess has already rested,  being fully calmed or if it has lessened somewhat; and then make the patient sit facing the sun, and the patient's head should be in your direction, and open the patient's mouth, and the attendant should hold the patient's tongue down with a very delicate instrument, the shape of which is as follows:
And it should be made of silver or bronze, and be thin like a knife.
So, as you press with it on the tongue and the abscess is shown to you, and you focus on it with your vision, then take a lancet and place it into the opening, and pull it towards the edges as much as you can. After that, if you pull out any part of the tissues, then cut it with an instrument of this shape:
After that, if you pull out any part of the tissues, then cut it with an instrument of this shape: and it resembles forceps, but the ends are curved, so that the opening of each one is in line with the other, very sharp, which should be made of tempered iron.
But if this instrument is not available to you, then cut it with a scalpel of this shape:  And it should be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you have incised one wound, cut the other in the same manner, and after the incision, the patient will recover from cold water.
But if there is a flow of blood, then treat it with water decoction of pomegranate rind or myrtle leaves, or similar things that have styptic properties, until the flow of blood is reduced, and then treat it until it is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses appear around the throat or near those two ears, and cut them as I have described in the incision of two wounds previously.
And I once treated a woman with an abscess that had formed in her throat. This abscess was dark coloured, with little sensitivity. It had almost completely closed off her throat, and she was breathing with great difficulty.  Eating and drinking water had already been forbidden, and she would have died within a day or two.
And the abscess had grown so large that it had formed two bumps before bursting through two openings.
So I treated it surgically, inserting a lancet into one of the openings, and removed a large portion of it.  Then, I incised the area surrounding the other opening, and opened the patient's mouth, pushing down their tongue, and inserted the lancet into that abscess.
And I then excised a large part of it, and only a little blood flowed from it. Therefore, the woman's throat was completely cleared, and she immediately started drinking water and then eating solid food. I continued to lance that abscess many times over a long period, and another abscess formed in its place, which I also lanced until it was resolved.
So I cauterized and treated the wound in her throat,  thus adding to the cure; and then I wandered, not knowing what God would do to me in the aftermath.
Chapter 37.
Of the lancing of a boil when it is suppurated.
When a sore develops on the uvula, and it is suppurated and long, it is then called a "perpendicular" sore; and if it is in the lower part, thick and round, then it is called a boil.
But when it is treated with what we have described in the division, and you do not see improvement, and sometimes a suppurated boil has already burst and is small, then you should incise that part which is thick and round; and if it is of that length, and is bloody, of dark colour or black, and is not tender to the touch, then you should try to incise it, for there is danger to the patient in that case.
And when you see it according to the way I have described, by its whiteness and length, have the patient sit down, and press the tongue with an instrument that has the proper shape, and then place the lancet on the new sore, and pull it down and incise it with one of the two instruments, which are described in the lancing of abscesses.
And you should only incise what is appropriately located on the natural boundary without adding anything, for if you incise it further, you will harm the patient's speech.
And after the incision, use the method we described for lancing abscesses, and treat it until it is healed.
However, if the patient is frightened by the incision, you should use cauterization without fear, and the method of cauterization is only with sharp water.  You should place the patient's head on your lap, then press their tongue with the instrument we mentioned, and then take the water described in the chapter on cauterization, and cauterize the affected area using it.  Place it neither thick nor thin, and fill the cavity of the instrument, which is of this form:
Let the end of the cavity where the medicine is placed be similar to the cavity of a spoon's bowl, and place the instrument with the medicine on the ulcer.  The patient should lie on their side so that any saliva or mucus from the mouth flows away from the medicine, so that no material from the medicine goes down the throat and irritates it.
Then apply your hand with the medicine, and press it onto the uvula for half an hour, until you see that it has been blackened and quieted by the medicine's action.
And if you wish, take a piece of cloth and wrap it around the end of the probe, and immerse the cloth in the medicine.  Insert the probe with the cloth in a tube until the cloth adheres to the tube, and do this multiple times until you achieve the desired cauterization of the uvula.  Then leave it there for it to dry and fall off in approximately three or four days.
And if you need to repeat the application of the medicine, repeat it. Then, after cauterization, cleanse the area around the uvula with cloth soaked in broth, and remove any remaining medicine from the surrounding area.  Wash it with cold water, and treat it externally with an application, and internally with remedies, until it is healed.
And this type of treatment is safer than incisions and less fearsome in its outcome.
Sometimes the uvula is treated with something less harsh than cauterization or incisions, through fumigation in this way: take balm, hyssop, rue, rue, Arabic gum, chamomile, borage and similar herbs, and combine all or some of these in a pot, and simmer them in sour wine; and the pot should be tightly sealed with appropriate clay, and a small hole should be placed in the middle of the pot, over which a concave bronze instrument should be placed, constructed of silver or bronze.
And the end of the instrument should be inserted into the patient's mouth until the vapor rises to the uvula through the tube, until the uvula is significantly dimmed.
And then repeat this many times until it is dry, and be careful not to perform this treatment at the beginning of an abscess, for it would spread to the abscess, and you should not do this without a doctor's declaration of a hot abscess.
But if this instrument is not available, then take a reed and place a hard-boiled egg shell on its end, so that the patient's mouth is covered, for the egg shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth.
And this treatment is good for health.
Chapter 38.
Of the extraction of thorns and what adheres to the throat and other things.
Many times thorns or fish bones or some other thing adhere to the throat.
Then you must extract what is apparent, on which you focus your gaze, after pressing the tongue with an instrument in front of the sun, so that what is in the throat becomes clear to you.
And what is hidden and unseen in the throat, you must make the patient vomit before the food is digested in the stomach, for the cause adhering to the throat will be easily expelled by vomiting; or the patient should swallow a piece of bread or lettuce root or a piece of dry bread; or let the patient take a piece of dry sea sponge, and bind it firmly with thread; and then as the patient swallows it, if it reaches the spot where the thorns are, it will be expelled. Or take an eel and swallow it, tied with thread, and it will be put inside and suddenly drawn out; or take a leek and quickly place and quickly draw it in.
But if it is not expelled by those methods we have mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this way:
And it should be larger than a fingernail, and at its end should be a cavity which you can easily insert into their throat.
And have the patient lift their head, and be careful not to touch their throat bone, so that it does not hurt them, and with that you push the bone or thorn, or the doctor does this with their hand.
But rather that the patient himself inserts it, through his knowledge of the location of the cause, and pushes it down, and pulls their hand with the instrument up, all according to the quantity which is prepared for him until it is expelled.
Chapter 39.
Of the extraction of bloodsuckers attached to the throat.
When the bloodsucker is medicated with what we have described in the section on treating with medicines and not with cauterization, then carefully consider the bloodsucker in the patient's throat, facing the sun, after you have compressed the patient's tongue with the instrument we have previously described.
Therefore, if you see the bloodsucker, extract it with a small knife or with skillful, gentle movements.
And if it is not possible for you to remove it with that, then take a concave cannula and insert it into the patient's throat, and then place a heated iron in the concavity of the cannula, and do this repeatedly (until the bloodsucker is dead and is detached from the throat) and keep the patient abstaining from water all day.
And then, take a bowl full of cold water, and open the patient's mouth and rinse the mouth with it; and let him not swallow anything and maintain the water in the mouth for about an hour using his hand, because the bloodsucker immediately withdraws when it feels the water.
But if it is not expelled by the methods we have described, then fumigate the patient with incense or with asafoetida using the instrument we previously described for fumigation of the uvula.
Do this, so that it falls off.
And there is another method of fumigation for this: take a bowl containing burning coals, cover the bowl with a lid having a hole in it, and place the tip of the instrument in that hole.  Then perform the fumigation over this, and place the patient's mouth over the tip of the cannula, and have him close his mouth so the fumigation doesn't escape until you know the fumigation has reached the bloodsucker, for the bloodsucker will then fall off immediately.
And if it doesn't fall off, then you repeat the fumigation many times, and keep the patient from drinking, and let him eat salty foods and herbs, and do not let him drink water, and with this regimen it is necessary that it be expelled.
And this is the form of the instrument with which a bloodsucker from the throat is treated when it is seen; and it is similar to forceps in some ways, but has a curve in it in some places. Which is placed on the throat, and its tip is similar to a stork's beak, which is smooth, where a file has been applied so that, when grasping something, it doesn't slip.
Chapter 40.
In which is the summary of the discussion on the appearance of abscesses and their incision.
Abscesses are many types, of many varieties, according to their appearance, each described in this book.
And they are diverse in their opening and incision in two ways, of which one is according to the nature of the abscess itself; and according to the moisture we find in them.
And the second way is according to the location on the body where it occurs, for an abscess in the head is different from an abscess in the buttock; and an abscess in fleshy tissue is different from an abscess in a joint; and for each of them there is a judgment for the operation.
And further, some abscesses should not be opened until the pus has matured within them or reached fullness.
Other abscesses should be lanced when they are inflamed and have not matured according to their fullness, such as abscesses near the joints, for when an abscess is near a joint, its existence is prolonged until that which is in its surroundings is corrupted, and the ligaments or nerves of that joint are severely damaged, which causes the contraction of that limb; or it is near a vital limb, and if you delay its incision until it matures, it may harm the limb; or if it is near the anus, then lance it unripe;  and you should not lance one that is inflamed, incompletely formed, unless it is near the anus, so as not to pierce the rectum and cause it to extend to the internal parts of the anus and create a fistula or damage the area beyond repair. But you must know the time for opening the abscess, and this is when it has matured.
And you know that it has matured when there is a cessation of pain and fever, and a decrease in swelling and throbbing, and the sharpness of the abscess head, and the other signs.
And therefore, you must make the incision at the point of the pus beneath the abscess and from the pus coming from the inflammation's surface.
And the incision should always be along the flesh or the body, if the abscesses are in the limbs or feet, and in places away from the tendons, nerves, or arteries; and, finally, in all similar places where there is no reflection; for in those that are reflected, use an incision suited to that particular limb.
However, when the abscess is in fleshy areas, it is better to let it be until the digestion within it is confirmed, as we have said; for if you incise it before then, you prolong the course of the illness and increase the unpleasantness, and the edges of the affected area become hard and deep.
However, some abscesses are incised across the width of the flesh or body, according to the need, or according to what the limb requires.
And you should make a small incision for small abscesses, and a large, wide incision or several cuts for large abscesses, according to the difference in size of the abscesses.
And for other abscesses where the skin should be scooped out and cut, when it is done in such a way that it can be left, as in those that occur with boils and buboes.
And some are incised with three angles; and some are incised in the shape of a myrtle leaf, like the abscesses of the groin.
And others are those where a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are used, and similar ones.
But those that are not like that, as in superficial lion-like abscesses, you should pierce them with a superficial, or simply flat, incision.
And when the abscess is large and has collected much pus, you should pierce it and not hurry to remove all the matter at once.  Having removed a portion, then close the abscess until the next day, and then remove a larger portion, and do this repeatedly according to a graduated process until it is completely drained.
And especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a small child, or an old, decrepit person, for the animal spirit is often weakened by the discharge of pus, and the patient easily dies, and you should be careful.
Guard this chapter with great caution, and after making your incision into these abscesses, you should clean the sores and consider whether the abscess wound is small, or a simple flat incision, in which case you should apply some linen or old cloth; and if the abscess is large, and the fissures of the incision are numerous, then you should place a medicament in each opening until they come together.
And if a part of the skin has been separated from the abscess or you have hollowed it out in the circumference, you should fill it with old cloth or a thread made from linen, as when you have extracted bread or roots, without moisture; and keep it tight for up to three days; and then you should proceed with the treatment and cure it with the appropriate ointment until it is healed.
But if blood flows during your operation, then you should apply cold water and vinegar in which you have soaked a linen cloth, and place this over the area of the bleeding repeatedly.
But if the bleeding persists, then you must administer a regimen and cure, using the powders which are described in many places in this book and in the divisions.
But if it is wintertime, and the area of the abscess is a region with many nerves, then you should mix powders in warm wine and oil, and apply these to the affected areas.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy, then you should apply powders mixed with water and oil, or with wine and oil, and keep them cool until the third day, as we have said; and then you should drain the abscess, cleanse it, and administer the appropriate treatment until it is healed.
And this is what is necessary for understanding the cure of abscesses in general, but according to the method of distinguishing, I have already described how each type of abscess should be treated, separately, in chapters.
Chapter 41.
Of the incision of abscesses that occur on the nape of the neck.
Small abscesses occur on the nape of the neck, and these are of various types of nodes, which have tunics formed around them; and the types of their contents are various.
And some of them contain pus, and some of that contain moisture resembling lime. And some of them contain moisture resembling dust, and similar to them; and some are stony, hard.
And in the incision of all of them, and in the extraction of those, there is no fear, since extracting them does not cause a contrary effect on the body.
And the procedure for incising them is that you probe them with an instrument shaped roughly like this, called a probe, until you know what they contain.
If what it contains is moisture, then you incise it with a lengthwise incision, a superficial incision in this manner:
And begin the incision from line B to line G.  Therefore, once the moisture has been evacuated, you then scrape away the skin that contained the moisture, and do not leave behind any residue whatsoever, for it frequently returns if any residue remains.
Then soak a cloth in Egyptian ointment, if available, or otherwise in water and salt; and fill the wound with that and leave it until the next day, for this will corrode any remaining bits of the skin.
And then apply Egyptian ointment to that cloth a second and third time, if necessary, until you are confident there is no remaining residue of the skin; then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.
And if the abscess contains fatty nodes, then you incise it in this manner: with an incision shaped like a cross, and insert hooks between the two edges of the incision and scrape it from every side, and exert your strength in extracting the membrane which contains it.
But if your effort encounters resistance, then do what I have previously described.
And similarly, do the same for a stony abscess in terms of incision and treatment as we have told you; and an incision on a stony abscess is easier, for it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are on the head in some men, in which there is no moisture at all; the reason for this is that I incised an abscess on the head of an old woman and found the abscess like a hard, rough, white stone, which could not be broken, and if one had struck someone with that, it would have hurt them.
However, for other abscesses occurring on the head besides this one, this chapter does not apply, such as abscesses that form near the heads of children and around the roots of the ears, but the incision of all these should be a superficial one.
And make the perforation of those in the lower part of them so that the body is relieved of the pus in the lower parts.
And then treat those with what is suitable for that treatment.
Chapter 42.
Of the incision of scrofulas (tuberculous swellings/inflammations) which occur in the neck.
Often these abscesses appear in the neck and armpits and groin; and sometimes they appear in other parts of the body.

And it is more important that the vein or nerve not be cut.
And if the pus is not very acute, do not apply your incision forcefully or distress the patient, and do not cut what is not necessary to cut; for if you cut a vein or artery and impede your operation, then apply crushed vitriol or other powders that stop bleeding, and compress the wound, and leave it until the acuteness of the abscess subsides and the wound is softened and begins to suppurate. When the blood is drawn/removed.
Therefore, return to your operation until you have completed it.
And then, check with your index finger if there are any other small causes of the scrofulas, therefore cut those and cleanse them.

And then, fill the wound with old linen, which you have already dipped in Egyptian ointment, and then treat that.
And if you cut the scrofulosis completely, then you must bring together the two edges of the wound and stitch it up as soon as there is no more excess fluid.
But if you see that there is excess fluid due to the size of the scrofulosis, you must understand to cut the root of that, and draw it out and administer stitches and what is stated.

Chapter 43.
Of the incision of the abscess's pus in the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the abscess, but no one in our land has seen it done.
And this is the writing of their speech.
However, in people with back pain, the removal of the abscess should be avoided, because it would not be beneficial, as all the veins and major organs of the patient are located there.
However, in those who have hot abscesses in the mouth, throat, or ears, when the illness is not in the throat, one should use the incision of the abscess to avoid the harm done by cauterization.
Therefore, when we want to do this, we should incise the abscess below three or four small circles around the abscess,  along a line between two circles, making the incision in a semicircle, not into the cartilage, because this location is suitable for incision, as it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant.
And if the physician is hesitant, then he should extend the neck's cartilage with a probe, and then cut the cartilage until it reaches the canal, but avoid the blood vessels if any are visible.
And then, cut the semicircle as we described; and the incision of the canal is indicated by the pus that flows out with the broken tissue and by the cessation of the voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.

And then administer medicines that promote healing until it is healed.
And Albucasis says that the summary of the treatment we have described is no more than this: you see that some of these abscesses have already blocked the patient's airway, and the patient is close to death, and the patient's breath is cut off, and some people cut the epiglottis so that the patient breathes through the wound for some breathing, and may be saved from death.
And therefore, I command that the wound be left open until the malignancy of the illness is consumed; and the malignancy lasts for three days; and then, I command that the wound be stitched up, and heal it until it is healed.
And this I myself have seen, that a servant took a knife and placed it in her throat and cut a portion of her lung's airway.  I was called to treat her, and I found her gasping as one does in death, and I uncovered the wound and found little blood coming from the wound.
Therefore, I was informed that she had not cut a vein or artery, and the air was coming from the wound, and I treated it until she was healed.
And it happened to the servant only because of difficulty in breathing, and after some days she returned to a healthier state.
And now I say that there is no fear in the cutting of an epilepsy.

Chapter 45.
Of the cutting of the types of tumors.
There are many types of tumors, and I have already described all their types in the division.
And it is necessary for me to recount here the difference between a tumor and a fistula, in case of doubt.
Thus, I say that with a fistula, there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the ebullition of the superfluity is subsided; and when putrefaction is complete, then the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a tumor, there is no heat, fever, nor pain, and it resembles that lion-like swelling that is in the proper sac.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is like a small bump and is formed like a sac, whether larger or smaller.  There are two types, for it is either fatty and contains moisture, and the colors of the moisture are many, as I have stated in the division.
Therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a tumor, you should explore it and investigate it at the beginning with an instrument called an *intromes* (probe), as its form will be shown in the chapter nearby.
And this is the method of inquiring into abscesses and all tumors:  you should take this instrument and insert it into the most moist area you find in the abscess, and you should rotate your fingers with the small instrument bit by bit until you know that the instrument has pierced the skin.
And then, place your hand according to the size of the abscess, and then withdraw the probe and consider what has come out on its tip.
And if the flowing moisture, whatever its color, is expelled, then you should cut it with a superficial incision as I have described in other abscesses.
And if there is no moisture on the probe's tip, then know that it is greasy (or fatty).
And dry it with a cross-shaped incision following this shape: In the way I have shown you, and suspend it with tongs, and scrape away the scab from all sides easily, and keep the skin or sack, if possible, over it, and extract that pus with the needle.
But if it breaks during the operation, it is not possible to extract all the pus at once, and often this happens, and then you extract it in pieces until nothing remains.
For if any part, little or much, remains, the needle will return according to the [nature of the] ailment.
And if you see that any small part remains, then fill the wound with some sharp corrosive powders, and compress the wound, and apply a hot poultice over it, and treat it with the other remedies until it is healed.
And if the wound is large, then you suture the two edges of it and treat it with whatever causes it to heal.
But if there is a pulsating or non-pulsating vein there, then you cauterize and fill the area with crushed vitriol, leaving it tight for two or three days until the wound can heal and the blood's boiling stops; then return to incising what remains of the wound.

Chapter 49.
Of the incision of abscesses that occur from arrow wounds or vein wounds.
When an arrow wound is inflicted, and the scab over it becomes consolidated, abscesses often develop.  The signs by which one recognizes that an inflammation or abscess is caused by an artery or vein are:  when an abscess is caused by an arrow wound, it is usually located more superficially on the body; and if you press on the abscess with your fingers, you feel a whistling sound (or "stridor").
And the one caused by a vein is deep and evident within the body.
And incision on this abscess is dangerous, especially those beneath the armpits, in the joints, in the neck, and many other parts of the body; if it is large, then its treatment with iron should be avoided.  The ones in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
However, for those that arise from inflammation of the wound opening from an arrow wound, make a longitudinal incision through the scab.  Then, using forceps, open the incision further and expose the arrow wound, freeing it from the surrounding tissues until it is uncovered.
Then insert a needle and drive it through to the other side, and pull the arrow out with a double-edged fillet in two places—which method I taught you in the extraction of two arrows lodged in the temples.
Then prick the area between the two ligaments with a lancet until all the blood is drawn out, and the abscess is dissolved.
Then administer treatment in the area where pus has formed until the ligaments have subsided; and then treat it with appropriate ointments until it is healed.
And if the abscess is due to a cut vein, then you must hold with your hand whatever you can of the abscess with the scab, and then insert the needle below the area that you have held with your hand, and drive it in; and have a double-threaded fillet there until you pull it through from the other side, and then tie the abscess with a strong ligament as I have shown you for a grape-like ligament, in two parts with two ends of the thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert the thread with the needle below the entire abscess, beneath the first incision of the needle, and tie your threads in four places.
Then dry the abscess in the middle of it, until what is inside it comes out, then cut away the excess scab, and leave what is tied to it.
And then place a poultice, which you have soaked in wine and oil, on it, and then administer the treatment made with herbs and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter 1 of the abscess that arises from a twisting of the nerves.
Thus, abscesses occur in the arteries and veins, and similarly, abscesses occur in the nerves when they are injured by impact or by excessive strain, and by similar causes.
And it (the abscess) is most often found in the locations of the tendons or the heel, or in any place where the joints are injured.
And it is a serious abscess, and it is similar in color to the rest of the body, and it is in the worst of locations, without relief, and when it is tight with violence, it causes the patient to feel a kind of numbness.
And it is not an abscess that spreads throughout the body, and therefore is localized beneath the skin, and it is inflamed on all sides, and does not spread forward or backward.
Therefore, those that are in the joints should not be incised with iron, for a strong contraction will easily follow.

Chapter 11.
Of the incision of an abscess that occurs in the cheil (lip) that arises in the abdomen.

Chapter LII.
Concerning the treatment of a swelling of the navel.
A swelling of the navel is caused by various things; either by tears in the membrane covering the abdomen, and there the tissue or intestine protrudes as it does in other ruptures; or by blood flowing from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or by trapped air in the area.
However, if it is due to a tear in the membrane and the protrusion of tissue, then the color of the abscess is similar to the color of the body; and it is painless, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the exit of the intestine, then its location differs significantly from what we have described; and when you press it with your fingers, it is hidden, and returns afterward; and it is painful there, and is greatly swollen near the opening of the abdomen, and causes a lot of distress.
But if it is due to moisture, then it is painless, and is not hidden when you press it with your hand, nor is it constricted, nor swollen.
And if it is due to blood, then the abscess shows signs of a darkening color.
But if it is due to dead flesh, then the abscess is serious, large, and hard, and is embedded within a certain amount of tissue.
But if it is due to wind, then the touch of it is painless.
And the treatment for it is that you must consider if the swelling near the navel is due to blood from an arrow wound, a vein, or wind;  if it is, you should be forbidden from treating it, for in such cases there is danger and fear, as is taught in the chapter where I discussed abscesses from arrow wounds and veins.
And if the swelling near the navel is due to an intestine or hernia, then you must instruct the patient to hold their breath, and stand extended; then gently probe around the entire navel with a probe, and tell them to lean between your hands on their sides, and then make a circular incision with a sharp scalpel around the area you have marked with the probe; and then cut open half of the abscess in the upper portion with large forceps; and then tie it tightly around the circumference with strong thread or a linen bandage, and tie it in a proper way.
Then lance the center of the open abscess over the ligament, and introduce your finger into that incision and search for the bowel; and if you find it and keep it held by the ligament, then gently pull it out and push the bowel back into the abdominal cavity.
And if you have found the hernia, then grasp it with forceps; and cut away the excess tissue.
But if it is an arrow wound or a vein, then circle around that area, and then return to your procedure; and take two needles, and put strong threads on them, and put the two needles into the circle you made around the abscess, in the shape of a cross, allowing them to penetrate; and then squeeze the abscess into four parts over the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and let the area be until the spread flesh softens and falls off on its own; or cut it when it is softened and about to fall off, and then treat it with whatever appropriate ointments are needed until it is healed.
If the protrusion of the navel is of flesh swelling there or due to moisture, then you must lance the abscess as we have said, and drain the blood or moisture you find there, and then treat it with whatever is appropriate to consolidate the area.
Chapter 53.
Of the treatment of cancer.
We have already discussed, in the division, the types of cancer and how it is treated with medicine, and that surgical treatment with iron is dangerous because it may cause ulceration; and we have discussed cancer originating from the womb and the precautions for its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes cancer is in a place where it is possible to remove it completely, such as cancer on the breasts or thigh, and similar locations on the limbs, by removing everything, especially when it is small and newly formed.
However, when it is advanced and severe, you should not try to remove it yourself, for I have neither been able to cure such a case nor have I seen anyone else do so, nor has anyone succeeded in such an undertaking.
And the operation in such a case, as we have said, is that you should first treat the patient and purge them of black bile in some cases, and subsequently bleed them if a clear accumulation of blood is found.
And then prepare the patient with the preparation appropriate for the operation, and then insert hooks into the opening, which are suitable for that; and then, with a sharp curette, carefully scrape away everything around it, until no trace of the cancer's roots remains. And let the blood flow, and do not cut it lightly, and press the affected area, and remove the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instruments you have available.
However, if you encounter difficulty with thick blood flowing from a vein or artery, then cauterize that until the flow of blood stops, and then treat the area with the rest of the procedure until it is healed.
Chapter 148.
Of the treatment of dropsy.

But if the dropsy originates from a liver ailment, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its origin is from the spleen, then your incision should be on the right side.

But evacuate that according to the quantity of its power, and according to what the patient's condition, the pulse strength, and the patient's constitution indicates to you.

Chapter 60.
Of the extraction of stones.

And the cure is easier for children before they reach fourteen years of age; and the cure is difficult in the elderly. But in the young, it is more manageable because it is among them (i.e., easier to treat when it occurs in younger people).
And for that in which the stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if it is small, it is the opposite.
Therefore, to arrive at a cure for this, it is necessary at the beginning to cleanse the patient with a clyster that removes all the impurities in their bowels; for this impurity sometimes prevents the stone from being expelled from the body.

And then, extract it and all of it from outside.

Then press on it with your finger, and expel it outwards, away from the place where you made the incision.

And know that there is some part of the stone, an angle and margins, that makes its removal difficult.

But if you cannot do that, then you enlarge the hole slightly, and if you see blood flowing, bind that tightly with powdered vitriol.
But if the stones are more than one, then at the beginning you press your hand on the opening of the bladder, and then cut on it; and then gently press near it; and do similarly if there are more than two.

of triangular tip, sharp, fitted into a handle.

Chapter 62.
Of incision on a strangulated hernia.
An acute hernia is nothing more than a collection of moisture in the white sac, which is beneath the groin fold containing the testicles, and is called the sac.
And sometimes it is in the proper pouch that nature has prepared in the area of the testicle, until such time as another testicle is already present; and it is the groin fold between the white sac that we have mentioned, and this is nothing but a tumour.
And the generation of this hernia is due to weakness which affects the testicles, so that this material is poured into them, and sometimes it results from a blow to the testicles.
And this moisture has many colours, for its colour is either inclining to yellow or blood-red, or reddish, or black and slimy, or watery and white, and this is what matters most.
And the signs by which one knows where this accumulation of water is are these: for if it is in the white sac we mentioned, then the abscess is round, inclining slightly to length, like the shape of an egg, and the testicle appears, because the moisture contains it from all sides.
And if the moisture is in the proper pouch, then the abscess is round with a part of the testicle, and thus one thinks that it is another testicle.
And if the moisture is between the scrotum and the white sac, then it is felt lower down.
And if you want to know the color of the moisture, then you probe the color of the moisture in the abscess with a pointed probe, the shape of which is known beforehand; and according to what comes out on the trace of the probe, judge what is inside it.
Therefore, when we come to treating it with iron, then it is necessary to order the sick person to be bled if it is possible for you, and to see if their body is full.
And afterward, let him be placed on his back on something a little raised, and put many cloths underneath him.
And then, sit on the left side of the patient, and instruct the attendant to stand on the right, extending their stick to one or two places at the scrotum and the abdominal cavity.
And then, take a sharp spatula and split the scrotum in the middle lengthwise, up to the pubic area.
And let the incision be made along a straight line, equally distant from the line where you have separated the skin of the scrotum, until it reaches the white tissue (or membrane) encompassing it.
And scrape that away, and observe, and be careful not to cut that; and your scraping tool (or instrument), with which you scrape that away, should be suspended with a thin thread and carefully examine the scraped area to the best of your ability.
And then pierce the water-filled sac with a wide opening, and remove all the water.
And then separate the two flaps of the incision with forceps, and spread the sac open; and do not touch the scrotum encompassing the testicles, and cut the sac in such a way as is possible for you. And cut that completely or into pieces, and especially the thin side of it, for if you do not carefully examine the incision, there is no guarantee that the water will not return.
However, if the sac protrudes from its sheath during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the sac, return it inside, and then suture the two flaps of the scrotum encompassing the testicles together, and then treat it like other wounds until it is healed.
And if you find the sac corrupted by another disease, then you must tie the vessels that are around the suspensory ligament, and remove the sac, and then treat it with what we have said.
But if the water is gathered in two parts, for example, then know that there are two hernias; therefore, incise that part as you have done in the other inguinal area; and if it seems to you that it is one operation, then do it as one.
And then you cleanse the blood, and place wool soaked in oil or warmed oil in the incisions; and put another piece of wool soaked in wine and oil over the testicles and the navel area; and place over this folded cloths and powders, and bind them together with a bandage in the following manner:

And sometimes this hernia is treated artificially with a cautery instead of cutting with an iron; and for this you should take a sharp, small scalpel and use it to cut the skin around the genitals and heat it as we have described until the white sac containing the water is exposed, and then use a cautery in this way: And it resembles a Greek letter.

And know that this operation is safer and further from blood loss, and better than an operation with a cut/incision.

Chapter 63.
Of the incision on a fleshy hernia and its treatment.
Know that in the incision of this hernia, there is a fear of harm in the more serious cases; for I have seen its dismissal and been saved by this better [method/approach].

But that which is caused by the swelling of a vein is not dispersed by any means through pressing it with the fingers.

Of the treatment of inguinal hernia.

And then, treat the wound as you would other wounds where pus is expected to form, so that the veins which nourish one of the two [parts/regions] may fall; for this is not harmful.
But if you find veins throughout, then it is necessary that the testicles be removed or that the testicles become blocked with the veins, so that they are deprived of all nourishment by the incision of the veins, and that they be withered, or that they become blocked so that they are not nourished by them.
Chapter 65.
Of the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The occurrence of this hernia is due to a rupture in the extended diaphragm over the abdomen, by one of the intestines being pulled out from the rupture onto one of the intestines.
And this rupture is either of the diaphragm or from a tearing of it, and these two types occur due to many causes: a blow, or a fall, or a loud cry, or by lifting some heavy object, or similar actions.
And the sign of this, when it is due to a stretching of the diaphragm, is that it occurs gradually over a long period, and not suddenly, and it is a swelling that gradually approaches the navel because the diaphragm presses on the intestine.
And the sign of it, when it is due to a tearing of the diaphragm, is that it initially causes sudden, intense pain; and it is a diverse swelling apparent beneath the nearby ribs, and this is due to the displacement of the intestine, and the body of it is outside the diaphragm.
And sometimes the intestine is pulled out along with the tissue, and this is called a complete intestinal hernia.
And sometimes it is caused by gas, and sometimes a crack occurs in the intestine, and it is trapped there.  This is a great harm to the patient, and sometimes causes severe pain, especially when pressed.
And in treating these types of illnesses with iron, there is danger, so the use of that should be avoided.
And the method of operating on this is to tell the patient to push the intestine back into its cavity with their hand if it will return, and then place it over their stomach between your hands, and raise their legs.
Then tie off the cord that connects to the testicles above, and cut the cord of the testicles completely lengthwise; and then separate the edges of the cut with surgical hooks, as you would need to open the cut with those.
And if the cut is deep enough, you can hope the egg (testicle) will be expelled from there.
Then scrape away the tissue underneath the cord of the testicles until the hard white scrotum is exposed on each side.
Then insert your finger into the area where the testicle is, in the space between the white scrotum, which is under the cord of the testicle, and the scrotum again, and separate any adhesions that are behind the testicle.
Then, hold the cord of the testicles outward with one hand, and with the other, pull the white scrotum upwards, and elevate the testicle with the scrotum toward the incision;  and instruct the assistant to hold the testicle upwards, while you break the adhesions behind the testicle with proper means.
And with your fingers, check that there is no cause for the intestine to be wrapped in the hard white scrotum. But if you find any such cause, then you push it back into the abdomen below.
And then make an incision in the area below the testicles' cord, where blood and pus will flow, as we have described in what we have already stated.
Then apply wool soaked in oil to the wound.  Apply the bandage we've described (and sometimes the white scrotum is cauterized, which we mentioned after the incision to prevent bleeding, as this often happens). Leave the bandage on until it falls off by itself.
However, if the wound's healing is delayed, then apply warm water compresses and keep them on until it falls off, as we have mentioned.
And then treat the wound with the usual treatment for wounds until it heals.

Chapter 67.
Of the rupture that occurs in the groin.

And then take the rod and place it on the prominent area of the groin, and press it against the abdomen.
And then stitch the two prominent areas on the groin's rod's extremity together, and join them with stitches.
And then pull out the rod, and do not cut the groin completely, nor extend the [testicle/scrotum], nor any other thing, just as I have taught you in the treatment of an intestinal hernia.
And then treat it with the treatment for wounds, and as you would dress wounds, cleanse it and restore the wound until it is healed; for the groin is contracted and the rupture's prominence is not adjusted.
And cauterization is better in this location, for it is closer to health.
Chapter 68.
Of the softening of the scrotum's hardness.
Often the hardness of the testicles in some men is softened by this, because the flesh that is between them is softened, and their watching over it is effective.
Therefore, it is necessary for the one who is engaged in the cure to have the patient lie according to his side, and to gently soften all the hardness that is above the natural hardness; and then to bring the two edges together with stitches.
And if you wish, stitch the excess softened hardness at the beginning with three or four stitches, and with firm sutures.
And then, cut between the stitches; and then treat with the cure for other wounds until it is healed and the pus has subsided.
Chapter 69.
Of castration.
Know that castration, in our laws, is forbidden, but by reason it is allowed to me to remember it in this book; and its mention is not necessary except for two reasons: one is so that when a physician is questioned about it, he knows the cure for those affected; the other is that we often need to castrate other animals for our benefit, such as oxen, goats, pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is done in two ways: either by crushing, or by a fissure/section.
But in that done by crushing, the method is to immerse the animal in hot water until its genitals are softened and hang down limp. And then grasp them with your hand until they are crushed and no longer appear beneath the touch.
But in castration done by fissure and section, you must hold the animal and press the scrotum of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut into each testicle with a section until both testicles are separated.
And then cut them further, once you have scraped each one. And do not leave on them any cause from the tunic, except for the subtle tunic which is on the vessels.
And this method of castration, which is done by friction, is better, for strong friction leaves no cause in the testicles by which the animal desires intercourse.
And then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter 70.
Of the treatment of hermaphroditism.
Hermaphroditism in men occurs in two ways: one is when a space appears in the scrotum, between the testicles, shaped like a woman's vulva, covered in hair.
And sometimes urine flows from the area within the scrotum.
But in women, it's a different type: the female vulva appears as if two testicles of a man have shrunk slightly and protruded on the exterior resembling a penis with two testicles.
And the treatment is of three types: for two types in men and one type in women. And it is necessary that you cut those adhering tissues until the protrusion subsides, and then treat them with the same treatment as other wounds.
And in the second type of male case where urine is in the scrotum, there is no cure or operation for it.
Chapter 21.
Of swelling and protruding flesh, due to causes affecting women.
A mild swelling is caused by natural factors until it subsides and is barely noticeable; and sometimes it grows quite large in some women, spreading as it does in men and interfering with intercourse.
Then you should remove the excess swelling with your hand or a lancet.
And do not overdo the lancing, especially at the base of the root, lest it cause a flow of blood.
And then treat it with remedies for wounds until it heals.
But flesh born within the mother's womb, filling it, and protruding through the parts of her body in the form of a tail (and for this reason, some earlier physicians called it "tailed illness"); then we must lance it as we lance swelling, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 72.
Of the treatment of erysipelas.
Erysipelas is a condition where the vulva of the woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is caused by an illness that has preceded it, and that illness is caused either by poorly adjusted flesh or by a subtle or thick (excessive) discharge.
And it is located either in the mother's womb, or in her ribs, or in the upper part of her body, or in the lower part of her body.  Avoid (prohibit) cooking, nourishment, and childbirth, and strongly prohibit menstruation.
And the diagnosis of this is made by observing the woman; if the obstruction is apparent, treat immediately.  But if it is not apparent, then you should search for it with your words or with a probe.
But if the obstruction is caused by a subtle discharge, and it's located near the two lips, then you should cook and break it apart.
And you should place powders on the two lips, then gently place your index fingers from both hands on top (or near) them.
And the woman should be on her back, her thighs extended, and then forcefully extend both lips until that subtle discharge is broken and the obstruction is opened.
And then take wool, soak it in oil, and place it over the area.  Let the person lie down with a companion by their side all day, so that the cut/laceration does not heal.
But if the discharge is thick and large, then it is necessary that you cut it with a sharp, flat instrument or a myrtle leaf.
But if the obstruction is due to a clot, then suspend that with tweezers, and cut it.
And have with you the medicines that stop blood flow without causing injury, such as acacia, dragon's blood, and goose dung, mixed with egg white.
And then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal too quickly.
And keep it there for several days, and apply a warm compress.
And then it should be treated with other healing remedies until it is healed.
And sometimes there are injuries to the surrounding flesh, and then it is necessary to cut along this method. However, if this is due to a brain abscess, the brain abscess, which is in the head, should not be treated with iron in any way.
Chapter 73.
Of the treatment of hemorrhoids, and of tail and red swelling that occurs in the vagina of women.
Hemorrhoids are the appearance of many veins around an opening such that from them flows much blood continuously; and if the hemorrhoids are old and are painful.
And I have already stated in the division the types of those [hemorrhoids] and the signs of those, and will now say which receive treatment and which do not receive treatment.
Therefore, I say that hemorrhoids and painful ones, when they are deep within the rectum and do not appear on the surface, then treatment with iron should not be performed on them.
But those which are at the opening of the rectum, over which one feels [them], are those which are treated.
And you should place the woman in a cold room and then carefully cut them with scissors, or with a rough cloth, and cut them along with their roots.
And then powder over them, to stop the flow of blood, with one of the incisive powders without causticity, like acacia and similar ones.
And then soak their feet for an hour, and then place them in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists, then make that person sit in a decoction of the root and bark of pomegranates, and walnuts, and similar things.
And then take barley flour and mix it with honey and sour wine and make a poultice with this and place it on the back of him/her.
And if the bleeding has stopped, good; and if not, apply two leeches, and to the breasts without incision.
And then take wool and soak it in the juice of coriander or the juice of arnica or the juice of comfrey mixed with diluted wine and apply it to the area; and then treat the area with ointments until it is healed.
And treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIV.
Of the perforation of a bone injury in the arm.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the arm, such as cancers, stony abscesses, ulcers, inflamed sores, creeping sores, fistulas, hemorrhoids, sores and swellings, and other abscesses. And I have already described these illnesses and their types, their symptoms, and their cures in the division.
And it is necessary that I describe in this treatise the hot abscess which comes in the arm when it is of the abscesses that collects pus, and if you wish to know in what manner the operation of it is performed with iron.
Then you must consider if the pain of the abscess at its beginning is sharp with percussion and phlegm and fever, and if it is sometimes red and if you touch it you feel it, then do not rush with puncturing it, and treat it with that which assists in maturation until the sharpness of the condition subsides.
Then it is necessary that you have the woman lie on a table with two surfaces; and have her recline on her back and place her feet at the furthest part below her stomach, and have her thighs spread, and place her arms beneath her elbows, and bind each with a suitable bandage.
And then the midwife, standing on the right side, administers the instrument with which the opening in the arm is made, the form of which will be evident in the procedures which follow.
And when you wish to administer this instrument, you must insert with the shaft the tip of the woman's arm, and that which from the instrument enters the arm should be only the tip of the arm and ease the patient's pain. But if the instrument is larger than the tip of the arm, then you must place powders on the texture of the arm's tip, so that the entry of any instrument into the arm's tip is prevented.
And you must place the string that runs through the instrument on the side that is above, and the administrator must hold the instrument and turn the string until the opening of the arm is made.
And when the discharge appears, and it is touched by the hand and is bloody and moist, then you must dry that part of their head with a soft cloth.
And when the blood is evacuated, then you must place on the wound a compress soaked in rosemary oil or green oil in which there is some astringency; and place the bandage over the wound on the arm's tip.
And place outside the arm, and on the bandage, clean wool soaked in water in which mallows have been boiled.
And then it should be treated for about two days with an ointment suitable for this purpose until it is healed.
And sometimes the arm and the wound are washed in such a way that it is treated with water and honey, and then with water in which lily root or long-stemmed iris has been boiled, and then returned to the ointment.
And if the wound is deep inside the arm, then you must refrain from treating it with iron, and treat it with what we have discussed in the previous section.
Chapter 75.
To teach the midwives how to treat living infants when their birth is not natural (or in a difficult way).
It is necessary for the midwife to know the signs of a natural delivery from the beginning.  So, when you see the woman straining downwards, breathing heavily and desiring fresh air, and she is relieved by what is above her in the process of delivery, and hastens the infant's emergence, then know that this is a natural delivery.  The infant emerges with its head first, and the afterbirth is either hanging or attached to the umbilical cord.
Therefore, when you see those signs, then the mother's abdomen should be compressed so that the baby descends easily. For when it descends with its head first, it leaves the afterbirth, which is purged of superfluities through proper cleansing.
However, a birth that is different from this is unnatural and undesirable. For when the infant emerges feet first, or the body comes out before the head and the feet, or one hand or the foot comes out first, or the head, or one hand, or it emerges towards the belly, and its posture is strongly turned towards its neck,  these are undesirable forms of birth.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be knowledgeable and skillful in all these matters, and to guard against error and deception.
And I am showing all the forms of these, and what ingenuity is in them, so that it is signified by this and based on this.
When the birth takes place with the head emerging naturally, and this is difficult and laborious for the woman, and sometimes her strength is weakened, then administer a sitz bath or have the women use it, and vaporize the area with collected fenugreek and moist oils.
Then the midwife should insert her fingers between the membranes, and separate the afterbirth, or cut it with her fingernail, until it is freed from its moisture. And press the woman's abdomen until the child descends.
But if it does not descend, then it is necessary for the woman to be treated with a poultice of ptillium and fenugreek with cumin.
And then command the patient, or apply the poultice, to cough and sneeze with determination; and hold the patient's mouth and nose for an hour, so that the child will be expelled soon.
And if the two hands of the child are expelled at the beginning, then you should gently return them slowly, bit by bit. But if it does not go back inside, then place the woman on a stool and lift her feet up, and then lower the stool towards the ground; and the woman must be held to prevent her from falling during the movement.
And if the hands are not returned inside and the child dies, then cut those open and remove the remaining parts of the child or tie the child's body to the hand with a cloth and remove it evenly, for that will be expelled.
When the child is on its feet, then you should push them upward, and then gently turn the child over, then swallow it down, then grab one of its feet and slowly extend it.
But when it has come out between the thighs, then you command it to strain, and make it sneeze with force, for it will be expelled if God wills it.
And if it is not expelled by what we have described, then you return the child slowly until you have placed it in its natural position, for it will then be expelled with easy birth.
And if it is still not expelled by all that we have described, then take marshmallow root and dissolved gum, pound them thoroughly in a mortar, and then apply this mixture to the vulva, at the lower part of her abdomen. And then apply a poultice in warm water, until it reaches the hypochondria (lower ribs/side of the chest).
And when the areas below her are soothed, then give her a fumigation of myrtle and place it beneath her. And after the fumigation has been applied for an hour, then make her sit on a cushion, and make her sneeze, and pinch her nose, and remove [the cushion/the source of the fumigation], and gently compress the lower part of her abdomen, for the child will soon be expelled.
The child emerges lengthwise, hanging from one of its hands. Be ingenious in reducing the hand of her, but if you cannot reduce that, then lift the woman and make her go.
And if she cannot go, then make her lie on the bed, and forcefully compress her thighs, and then administer dressings that I and you have recommended for poultices.
And if labor has begun, then make her sit on a stool, and then study the reduction of the hand, and suck the child back into its natural position, and make the woman sneeze and command her to push until it is expelled.
The child is lying on its back, its arms outstretched, and its face is towards its mother's side. The midwife should hold the child's hand and gently and easily turn it around.
And when it is sucked back in, then put on its forehead what we have recommended of oil and poultices.
And then tell her to move slightly, and when you have done this, then make her sit on a stool and tell her to lean on her right side, and then tell her to push; and make her sneeze with conditions. The preparation of the dead fetus should be done on its side.
When the mother is delivered of the afterbirth in this manner, consider, and then you cut the placenta with your fingernail until it is emptied of moisture.
And if the fetus is hanging by the face and neck, then you cut that of the umbilical cord, so that it is not retained and dies.
And then push the fetus into the parts within until you place it where it should be, and tell the woman to push herself several times, and it will be expelled easily, if God wills it.
Delivery of twins or multiple fetuses. Know that twins are born frequently, and sometimes three or four, but that is strange and rarely happens; however, such things are outside nature, and not [considered] alive.
And count those, whether they have multiplied, as a second birth, and separate them by a cut, distinguishing between the ligaments at the umbilicus of each of them. And sometimes they are expelled just as a single fetus is naturally and easily, and sometimes this happens in an unnatural way, as we have reported.
And if some of them are difficult to deliver and reach the point we have described regarding unnatural forms, then treat them as we have recounted.
And if the midwife is skillful and does this with ease and subtlety, and with patience, and is resourceful in every way according to the preparations made to bring about health.
A recollection of that which is formed in the womb from embryos and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or even ten are formed in the mother's womb.  It has been verified by me that one woman miscarried six, and another ten, all formed, the cause of which was determined by the wise scholars.
And all this must be within the knowledge of the midwife, for it is easily overlooked in such small matters, and therefore she must attend to it carefully.
Chapter 76.
Of the extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus using the methods we have described in the section on medical procedures, and it has not been expelled naturally, and you are forced to use manual procedures, then you must consider whether the woman is healthy and not suffering from any condition that might cause her harm.
Then the woman must be positioned on her back on the bed, and her head should be lowered and her thighs raised.
And the attendant should hold her in that position, or she should be tied to the bed, so that her body does not move during the extraction of the fetus.
And then you should moisten the mother's mouth with moist oils and with mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and linseed.
And then the midwife should anoint her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then gently insert her hand into the mother, and then seek with her hand the location where she can hook her fingers into the fetus.
And suitable places for this: then consider if the fetus is positioned so that its head is downwards, and then you should hook your fingers into its neck, or mouth, or palate, or under the chin, or into the fork of the neck, or into the appropriate places on the sides, or under the ribs.
But if the position is with the feet downward, then in the groin, or in the middle of the back.
And you should hold the finger with your right hand, and place the curve of it in the space between the fingers of the left hand, and gently insert the hand and hook the finger into any of those places we have mentioned, until you reach the empty space.
And then place another finger, or a third if necessary, on the side of it, so that there is a pulling force, and not only an extension, according to its direction.
And move it in all directions, so that its expulsion is eased, just as is done in extracting a tooth.
And it is necessary, in the space between them, that the extension be softened; and if any part of it is retained, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some of her fingers with oil, and insert those fingers and turn around the retained part.
Therefore, if the part of the fet is forced out, then the fingers should be moved to a higher location; and do this until all of the fet is expelled.
But if the hand has slipped out before the other, and it is not possible to return it by compressing it, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it; and then pull it out until the whole is expelled, and cut it either at the joint of the hand or at the elbow.
And thus you must do the same for the other hand and the two feet.
But if the head of the fet is large, or if compression causes it to swell, or if water has accumulated in its head, then it is necessary to insert fingers between the spines of the vertebrae and break the head open and drain the water, or to cut it open with an instrument called "alundat," the shape of which will be found with the shapes of other instruments in the chapter that comes after this one.
And similarly, do so if the fet has a large head by nature: and then it is necessary to cut it with a scalpel, or to grasp it with an alundat as we have said; and then extract the bones with forceps.
And if the head has slipped out and is compressed against the furcula, then it should be split open until the moisture that is in the foot is expelled.
But if it is not complicated, nor subject to swelling, then you cut the head as soon as possible.
And if the lower part of the belly is abscess-ridden or hydrops, then it is necessary that you pierce it until the moisture within it flows out.
But if the fet is on its feet, then the pull on it is gentle and the direction towards the mother's mouth is gentle.
But if it is compressed against the belly and the foot, then you must extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and cut open the belly and foot until that which is within them flows out.
But if the other limbs are removed and the head remains, then the midwife should insert her left hand; but if the mother's mouth is open, then she should insert her hand into the air pocket, and search for the head with it, and pull it with her fingers towards the mother's mouth, and then insert a probe, or two probes, and pull it with them.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted by a hot abscess that attacks the area, then it should not be forcefully treated; instead, a soothing application of fatty, moist substances is necessary beforehand. And administer plasters; and keep the patient in waters that soften and moisten.
And if the condition is on the patient's side, if possible, it should be lanced; and what we have said about a live fetus should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you leave no cause within the second skin.
And when you have completed all your procedure, then administer what we have said about the treatment of boils, which occur from the mother.
And if a flow of blood occurs, then you should apply styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have already seen a woman in whose belly a stillbirth had occurred, and subsequently, she became pregnant again, and another stillbirth occurred. A long time ago, a boil occurred on his navel; and it swelled until it was opened, and began to discharge pus.
And I was called to treat him, and I medicated him for a long time, but the wound did not heal.
Then I applied some strong-drawing plasters to him, and a bone came out, and after a few days, another bone came out; and I was very surprised, since there are no bones within the belly.
And then I thought that they were bones from the stillborn child.
Then I cleaned the wound, and removed many bones, the woman being in a very good condition, and she lived in this way for a long time, recovering from a little bit of pus.
And I did not consider this strange, but rather, as it is a science and an aid to what the skillful physician administers with his hands for healing.
Of the forms of the instruments that are necessary for extracting the fetus.
Form of dizziness with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of tightening in which the books are bound, with two twists at the two ends of two cords. However, this twist should be finer than a tightener, and should be made either of leather or of wooden dowels.
And let the width of each dowel be two fingers and the margin of each be one finger, and the length of each be one and a half palms; and in the middle place of the two dowels there are two joinings of the same dowels, already fastened, whose length is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mother's mouth so that it is opened when you twist the two prescribed twists.
Another form similar to this, finer and lighter:
It should be made of leather or wooden dowels, in the style of forceps. However, at its extremity, there are two additions, just as before, and the length of these additions is about one palm, and the width of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with these, then you have the woman lie on the bed with her feet hanging down, opening the space between her thighs. And then open your hand with the instrument, just as you would with scissors, to the desired extent of the opening of the mother's mouth until the midwife does what you want.
The form of vertigo that the ancients described:
A form of propulsion by which the fetus is propelled:
A form of friendship/assistance by which the large head is contained.

A form of a hook having a spine/point:
A form of a hook having two spines/points.
A form of a spatula having two ends, with which the head is severed/removed.
A form of two wide spatulas.
All those instruments, and the more their varieties are multiplied and prepared by craftsmen, the lighter their operation and the more valuable they are to people due to their quantity.
