Of the extraction of the afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then you should instruct the woman to hold her breath, and then make her sneeze with a suggestion, and press your hand over her mouth and nose. If, through this, it comes out, it is good.
And if not, then you take a bowl and pierce a hole in the cover, and put in it herbs opening the mouth of the mother, such as mint, rue, anise, chamomile, Arabic cicada, cinnamon, and St. John's wort. All these herbs, or some of them, should be submerged in water in the bowl, and place that on the fire.
And then place a reed cannula in the mother's mouth, and hold it there until the vapor reaches the mother's cavity.
And then make her sneeze, as we have said, and the afterbirth will be expelled quickly.
But if this treatment remains ineffective and it hasn't been expelled, then you order the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or marshmallow decoction, and then introduce that into the vagina and try to pull out the afterbirth with it.  And when she has located it, she should take it and pull it out slowly. But if it is stuck to the mother's womb, then she should introduce her hand as we have described, and when she has found the afterbirth, she should extract it bit by bit according to the appropriate procedure, so that the mother does not suffer from sudden forceful traction. Before that, it is necessary that the [object] be moved easily to the right and left sides.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already described the treatment with sneezing and with herbal remedies.
But if it is not expelled by all the methods we have described, then be careful not to exert too much effort in trying to extract it. However, what has come out of it into the mother's mouth should be placed onto the woman's thigh.  Then, treat it with an ointment and a four-ingredient medicine; this will cause it to decay in a few days and it will dissolve and be expelled.
However, when it decays, a foul odor rises to the head and stomach, and this affects the patient.  Therefore, fumigations and appropriate remedies must be administered.
But some of the ancients had already tested fumigations of narcissus and dried figs.
Form of instrument with which the woman is fumigated for the retention of menstrual blood and the afterbirth:
It should be made of glass or bronze.
And the slender end should be placed in the cone, and the wider end over the fire, and the fumigation should be placed over the flames of the fire.
And let that one remain until the fumigation is consumed, and then do another.
Of the treatment of the unperforated anus of the child.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which are already narrow and obstructed. Then it is necessary for the midwife to pierce that narrowness with her finger; and if not, to pierce it with a sharp instrument, and be careful not to touch the rectum.
And then place over it wool soaked in wine and oil; and then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wants to swallow.
And sometimes anal obstruction occurs due to a consolidation of a plague or an abscess; then it is necessary that this consolidation be incised, and then it should be treated with what we have mentioned about treatment and the use of lead.
Of the treatment of the fistulas that come afterwards.
Fistulas that develop in the lower part, often swollen and thick, which appear near the anus externally or within the area of that part.
And it is a fistula or many.
And when that swelling is old and comes from moist white fluid or subtle pus, and sometimes these fistulas are somewhat penetrating.
And those that are penetrating are known because of what comes out of them through the discharge and by the windiness; and sometimes the patient experiences discharge, and often worms come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the bladder, and into the opening of the penis; and some of those penetrating ones reach the joint of the thigh and the bone of the buttock.
And how a penetrating fistula is known to be in the anus and not penetrating is that you put your index finger into the anus, and you insert a thin probe of lead or iron into the fistula when there is no twisting.
But if there is twisting in it, then insert a thin probe of lead or horsehair until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
And if you don't feel any of that at all, nor does any discharge, worm, or windiness proceed from the opening, as we have said, then know that it is not penetrating, and therefore, cease your treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the opening of the penis, then the symptom is the emission of urine from there, and prohibition of that until the affected area is healed with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the hip joint or bone, and pain radiates around it, then the sign is that the probe penetrates straight into the bone, if there is no twisting, and pain radiates around the affected area; and that it may persist permanently; and that no treatment nor ointment will heal it.
And in all these penetrating fistulas, there is no complete cure, and the treatment of those is the work and vanity of foolish physicians upon whom you should not rely.
But in those that are not penetrating and are not old, healing with iron is expected, as I have told you, and as I have already tested.
And the patient should lie on their back with their hands under their hips, and raise their legs upwards, their thighs inclined towards their stomach. Then insert a lead probe, or a ram's horn if the fistula is twisted, until you know the point where the probe penetrates.
And if the patient feels pain around the anus, then you must insert your finger into the anus.
And if you feel the probe penetrating on your finger, or if you feel something between your finger and that flesh or tissue, then you certainly know that it is penetrating; and therefore do not work on it, for there is no healing in it, as we have said.
But nevertheless, some might say that it heals in some men slowly.
And the treatment by which improvement is expected in him is that you ignite a thin cautery according to the width of the fistula, as previously described, and insert that heated cautery into the fistula until it reaches around the anus; and then repeat this two or three times until you know that all the flesh around the fistula-like reed-feather-tube has been cauterized.
And then treat it with some bandages until all that cauterized flesh is healed, and then treat it with medicines that promote flesh growth and healing. If it is healed, well and good; but if not, then it will not be healed at any time by any other treatment.
And if the probe enters, but does not penetrate all the way to your finger in the anus, and between the probe and that, there is a thick layer of flesh or tissue, and the fistula extends to the surface of the bowel, then cut the bowel from the beginning of the fistula, and cut along with the probe.  Let the probe be in the fistula until the cut reaches the spot where the probe's tip ends, and by the probe, it is freed and extracted.
And then cleanse those fistula-like tissues which resemble a bird's feather quill, and no cause remains of them.
But if bleeding occurs between you and your operation when you are cauterizing those tissues, then the best and easiest instrument is to cauterize with fire or with a sharp medicine, for cauterization with fire possesses two beneficial properties:  it cauterizes the affected flesh, and it stops the flow of blood and removes excess moisture.
And then treat it with bandages or a dry cloth until the area heals, and then treat the wound with ointments that promote strong flesh growth and healing.
And those medicines which are astringent and desiccant; when this is done, it is already healed, and you need not fear a recurrence.
However, if the fistula extends to the anus and is distant from the body's surface, then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
And if, upon probing, you find a membrane or flesh obstructing the passage, and it is close to the opening, then there is no operation there but rather a path of trust and hope.
This condition can be addressed in one of three ways: either cauterize it as we have said; or cut it open until you reach the cavity, allowing you to insert medicaments and facilitate healing.  Do not proceed with extensive cutting, as you might sever the surrounding tissue, causing further injury to the patient without their consent.  Afterwards, treat the area with the remedies previously mentioned; this will lead to a swift recovery.  Or, gently separate the membrane and cleanse the pus that collects there.  Cut the fistula as close to the anus as possible, as described previously, using a probe or another sharp instrument. Cleanse the ulcerated flesh created by the incision, allowing for scab formation.  Then, diligently strive to close the entire wound using surgical techniques. If the opening above is not yet healed, and the fistula persists in the anal region, the patient will suffer further, potentially serious complications. And sometimes the fistula presents in this manner: when you insert the probe into the fistula, it is located in the anal fold near the body's surface, with the opening along the rim of the anus.  In this case, take a probe with a pointed end, resembling a needle used to prepare thread for surgical use, in this fashion:
and insert it into the fistula wrapped in five threads or more;  and then introduce the threaded probe into the fistula until you reach the cavity within.
However, if the opening is close to the anal margin,  you withdraw the thread from the hole,  as if you were inserting your finger into the anus;  remove the thread tip,  and tie the two ends together, tightening each knot.  Leave it like this for a day or two.  If the thread has cut into the flesh,  tighten it further until the flesh between the two ends of the thread is cut and falls away.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and completely closed.
But if the fistula opening is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in whatever way is suitable for you, but don't press too hard, so you do not injure the sphincter; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the pointed probe used to open the fistula: one curve is very sharp, the other part is not, so that you do not injure what is not necessary, as often happens.
Of suturing hemorrhoids in which blood flows during the incision.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, either they are like lumps in the buttocks, or they are like bunches of grapes. And of these, some are small, some large; and the blood flows from them constantly, sometimes one, sometimes many, and they are sometimes outside the anus, and on its edges.
However, those outside the anus are according to the condition of low moisture, from which flows yellowish water or a little blood with a continuous consistency, and the color depends on the consistency.
And the treatment for those inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and to strain until the anus opens and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you grasp and lift them with tweezers or your fingernail, and then cut them away from their roots.
However, if the tweezers do not hold them due to their moisture and softness, then grasp them with a rough cloth, or cotton, or a piece of wool, and extract them with your fingers, and then cut them away and sprinkle some warm powders over the cut, so they act as a cautery in place of the cauterization; or cauterize them as described in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat them with the other remedies until they are healed.
And if the anus does not yield to discharge, then prick the patient with a small, biting cauterization, and wash it with whatever is in the anus, so that it yields to discharge easily when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then the cause is superficial, and you should grasp them with your finger or tweezers, cut them, and then treat them with the remedies we have mentioned until they are healed.
And whoever has made an incision should administer sutures to them in this manner: take a linen strip and thread it onto a needle, then pull the hemorrhoids apart at the top edges, and make the needle pierce the root of the other side.  Wrap both ends of the linen strip on the lower side of the needle, laying them horizontally.  Tighten the hemorrhoid firmly, then tie the linen strip off and pull the needle out.
Do this to all of them, and leave one of them unsutured, so that the excess blood may drain from it.
Then place a cloth soaked in heated oil or ointment on the anus;  place it over the area and tell the patient to rest. And then leave it until it subsides; and when it has settled, treat it with ointments or other remedies until it is healed.
And when they are old and no longer respond to that treatment, then you must scrape them with the tip of a spatula or with your fingernail until they become moist and the scab of pus on top, which prevents them from healing, is removed. Then treat them until they are healed.
Then scrape them with a more forceful scraping than the first until much blood and scabs are removed; and then treat them until they are healed.
Know that inflammation, this is key, and it is not anything other than a red round spot, resembling a flower bud, that is found throughout the body, but more so in the parts below the feet and in the fingers, and it is often the cause of pain in those places.
And then it is necessary to cut what is around the inflammation, and hold it with forceps or nippers, and to remove it from its root; and then treat that.
And if you wish, cauterize it according to what is explained in the previous chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, fistulous, round elevation on the surface of the body, and it frequently appears on the hands of children.
And its treatment is to scrape and cut it, or to press it with a file or a needle until it bursts. And if you wish, cauterize it, as we have said, with fire or with a sharp medicine.
But a pimple is a small, festered or fistulous swelling on the surface of the body, often appearing on the forehead, and when the cold catches it, a sensation like an ant bite is felt, and it is formed around the body.
And its treatment is to take a hollow tube made from an eagle's or a vulture's feather, and make it strong, and place it over the pimple until the tube encircles the pimple's edges; and then you should rotate your hand with the tube so as to cut the material surrounding the pimple, and place it on the forehead of the flesh, and remove the pimple from its root.
And if you wish, do this with a tube of bronze or iron according to this method:
Let the part above the tube be thin, smooth (that is, not pierced), wrapped so that the grip on it is reduced, and its rotation is easy.
And if you wish, cauterize the pimple as previously described in the cauterization of a boil in the chapter on cauterization.
Of the forms of instruments that are administered for the treatment of ulcers.
Sometimes ulcers are treated with silver or bronze or molten or hammered bronze; and sometimes they are treated with those small and large instruments according to those which are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children should be small; and those whose bottoms are painful or sore, their ulcers should be treated with small ones.
The form of large and small ulcers:
The head of the cannula, on which the bag is tied, should be wide in this way; and there should be a distinction where the bag is tied, just as before.
And the lower end, which is inserted into the bottom, should be completely closed (that is, not open) very narrow, almost subtle; and on one side of it should be two holes, and on the other side one hole, and the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays and a little thicker.
And the bag, in which the medicines for the bladder of a beast or sheep's skin are placed, and its bottom should be one palm wide.
And it should be perforated with many holes, as it is rolled; and if the space between the holes is the thickness of the finger.
And then insert into those holes strong, sharp, crude, and strong fiel (likely a type of medicated substance), about ten of them, or thereabouts; and fasten the bag to that.  And when the bag is placed in place, tightly bind the head of the device (cristeri) between two dividers with a firm constriction, and then seal each hole with that (cristeri).  However, the technique of sealing with that is that you fill the bag with medicine, which should be warmed to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is not too hot nor too cold.
Then the patient should lie on their back, raise their legs up, and place a cloth or a sheet under them; then anoint the end of the cautery with oil, or egg white, or fenugreek mucilage, or similar.  Then gently insert this into the anus, and the attendant should grasp the bag and squeeze it with both hands with force until everything within the device has been expelled into the intestines;  then remove the device, and the patient lowers their legs to the floor.
And if the medicine causes that expulsion, keep it in place as long as possible; and if they sleep with it all night, it is more effective in aiding recovery.
However, if you cauterize in cases of bowel looseness and expulsion, you repeat the cautery until healed.
And if there are hemorrhoids or festering sores in the anus, then the cautery should be very fine and gentle, like the cauteries used for children.
Of the treatment of plagues.
I have already stated in the division of the treatment of plagues what was appropriate for the book, and I say thus the treatment of those with hands and with medicines, according to the condition.
Therefore I say that plagues are diversified according to how they are caused and according to the location where they appear.
And the causes why plagues are often inflicted are like a stone throw, or a cut from a blade or knife, or a blow from a spear, or a stick, or an arrow, or similar to these, from many causes.
But the plagues that are in the parts of the body are like the plague that falls upon the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the stomach, or the liver, and similar to these in the limbs.
And I am recounting the treatment of some plagues, so that you may apply that reasoning and rule to other plagues.
And I will begin with the simple plagues of the head, for the remembrance of the compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple plague occurs on the head, and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from a thrown stone or similar, and the skull has only been dented and it is a serious injury and you fear the onset of a hot abscess in the patient, then you should quickly perform phlebotomy on the cephalic vein, and do not delay this; and draw from it [the vein] according to its strength, if nothing prevents the phlebotomy, especially if the blood that has come out of the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the injured part; and keep the patient from fullness, and from strong drinks and foods.
But if any reason prevents you from diminishing [the phlebotomy], then reduce the patient's food and do not let him approach meat or wine.
And apply to the wound, if a hot abscess has arisen, a poultice of softened oil or wine in which something astringent has been mixed.
But if you are certain of a hot abscess, then apply to the wound, if it is recent, its own blood, and the air has not changed the powder that I will briefly describe below.
And if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointments until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or caused by a cut or similar injury, and its edges are not brought together by a bandage, then bring them together with sutures, as I will describe in the section on suturing.
But if the wound has already exposed the bone and tendon, and there is not a retention but a small suspension, then cut it and then treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh gradually in place of the damaged part, and order the patient to eat foods with a thick consistency:  porridge and similar concoctions, until it is restored.
And if you find putrefaction in the damaged tissue and it does not adhere to the bone, then you cut away all the putrid material and discard it, and then treat it.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood can flow, and it is not severed by medicines, then you search for the artery, and if you find it unsevered, then you cut it with a spatula, or tie it off; and if necessity compels you to not do as described, then you cauterize it until the bleeding stops.
And if these wounds are simple and small, then the cause of them is slight; therefore you can sprinkle this powder on them, for it will solidify them before the air alters them.
And the method for the powder is this: take one part olive oil, two parts dragon's blood, and three parts of unquenched lime; thoroughly grind and sift them, and fill the wound with the mixture, and bind it firmly until it adheres well to the area, and it is coagulated by the blood.
And then you bind it with dressings and leave it, and do not remove it as long as it is not inflamed, for the medicine will not be effective until it is healed.
And sometimes this works alone, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood present.
And you perform this same operation on large wounds when it is necessary to place it on them and bind them, and on fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, especially during summer, then this powder is not appropriate; but instead treat them with what we have said, or apply digestive ointments to them, or a poultice of barley flour with water and honey until they form a scab; and afterwards treat them with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if a wound occurs with a fractured bone, and it is small, then you extract this with a poultice.
And I will describe the treatment of head fractures in what follows.
Of the wounds of the neck.
But if the wound occurs on the neck, then there is no difference in treatment, and between the wound of the head when it is simple.
However, if the nerve or artery of the neck is cut, then if it is a nerve, there is no remedy but to avoid treatment with something that causes extreme astringency, such as applying zinc and verdigris (which is vitriol and similar to them), because these hurt the nerves and constrict them. And do not apply any cold thing to the wound, because the substance of the nerves is cold and its continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of all the members.
And if it is treated with gentle remedies, such as washing with soft water, sometimes infused in oiled or un-oiled oil, and a poultice when washed with soft water and other softening stones in this manner, and moist ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer sutures, or constrict its edges, and then treat it with powders until it is healed.
However, if the wound is deep or has reached the underlying (or deepest) part of the flesh, then pierce it in the most depressed area.
But if an artery has already been cut in the wound and blood is flowing, then separate or cut off that artery, or tie it off, or cauterize it if necessity prompts you to do so.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or all of them have been cut and the neck veins are saved, then you should bring the edges of the wound together with stitches on the neck bone and do not touch it; first straighten it and bring it back to its natural shape, and then hold it firmly with a suitable bandage, and leave it for several days; and then treat it with ointments containing astringent and stimulating substances until it is healed.
However, if the wound has already affected the bone deeply, then you must treat it.  And if there are bone fragments there, remove as many as you can; and what you are not able to remove, leave it until the wound can fester and the fragments are separated and the discharge from it is relieved.
And if the wound is recent, then sprinkle the powder that has been recommended on it. But if a fistula has formed in the wound, then treat it with what the remembrance (or recollection) will bring forth in its appropriate chapter.
If the wound is from a spear or knife, and considering its depth,  then consider if there is a windiness when the patient breathes (or the patient is breathing heavily/has labored breathing);  then know that the wound is mortal.
And if it is not deep and the wound is recent with its own blood, then you place on it, from the first hour, powder, and do not press it so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the patient, but place an attractive ointment there, and loosen it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available to you, then you place on the wound's opening a piece of old cloth, so that it absorbs whatever humidity is there; and make the patient sleep on the wound, so that what is collected there runs out.
But if three days or more have passed since the wound and there is no spasm or trembling of the heart or tightening of the lungs, nor other bad conditions of the patient, then know that the wound is saved; and then cure it with poultices and other treatments until it is healed.
But if the healing of it is hindered, and it has remained open for a long time, then know that a fistula has already formed; therefore, treat it according to its specific procedure.
And if the wound is from a cut with a knife or a sword, and is simple on the surface of the foot or the back, then cure it with what precedes suturing, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has already caused damage to the bone, and has already cut pieces out of it, then you should probe the wound and try to remove those pieces, if they are detached. But if they are not detached, then leave them until the wound can close, because their removal is difficult.
And for other wounds that occur on other limbs, the treatment is the same as what we have described.
Of wounds of the abdomen.
The ailment that occurs in the abdomen is either severe, slight, or moderate.
And a small rupture is more difficult to fix in another way, and the reduction of these parts is more difficult if you do not quickly return them to their proper place after the intrusion, for if it opens and swells, the re-insertion becomes difficult.
Therefore, a moderate rupture is the best, for in that case the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in the two aforementioned types.
And know that suturing of the abdomen is done in four ways, but two sutures are common and suitable for suturing other wounds on the body.
And two other specific sutures are common to other wounds, but are even more specific to abdominal sutures.
And I will explain each one to you with exposition and declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small and some part of the intestine has come out through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this is in one of two ways: either due to the smallness of the rupture, as we have said, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is the case, you should warm it by immersing a damp sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or by soaking it in water with squill, cyperus, and spikenard, and apply that to the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes, this is done in water with astringents, and is better than plain water in resolving the inflammation.
And when you see the inflammation to be resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which althea and mallows have been boiled, for by this the interior of it is relieved with little trouble.
But if the interior of it is not relieved with this treatment, then cut the rupture a little with an instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better, when it is proper and does not involve cutting, and there should be no recourse to cutting when there is necessity and weakening.
Of the instrument, see its shape:
One part of it is sharp and pointed, the other part is blunt, the tip is slender, and it is not as slender as a spatula, but rather slightly blunt.
And therefore, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine is outside, then its reduction should be according to its natural form, into its proper place, if possible, for that is better.
But when the rupture is wide, and is in the lower part of the abdomen, then it is necessary that you have the patient lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head; and if it is in the upper part of the abdomen, then you raise the head and feet higher than the lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, then constantly direct your attention and consideration to this, so that the part where the wound is is higher than the other.
And thus it is necessary that you administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
And in small wounds, place what you have prepared, and then have the assistant hold the rupture entirely with their hand and bring the edges together, and then investigate if there is any other reason for the suture or something else.
And this is the common method of suturing: take a needle, or a set of needles, according to the size of the wound; and then place the end of the rupture on the ring finger, and tighten the needle so that you don't leave slack in the two ends of the suture, with the two ends of the thread underneath the suture of the inner parts, until you penetrate from that side; and you have now joined the two ends of the suture and the two ends of the thread, making four knots.
And then tighten with a double thread around the needle, and sometimes from both sides together, and then close the two edges and the wound with a proper closure; and then leave the thickness of the finger and insert another needle in it, and then tie it with thread just as you tied with the first needle; and do not cease doing this with needles if it is necessary until you have completed the entire closure of the wound.
And the needles should be of medium thickness between thick and thin, because thin needles easily pierce the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to pass through the suture, and therefore they should be medium in thinness and thickness.
And the needle should not be inserted too close to the two ends of the suture, so that the flesh is not pierced easily and the wound bursts before its consolidation.
And do not extend the sutures so that the wound is not prevented from healing.
And then cut the ends of the needles so that they do not disturb the patient's sleep; and place on the suture expanded powders from all sides, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is already healed.
And this type of suture with needles is most convenient for small wounds, for in their suture one or two or about that number of needles are sufficient.
But the second suture is common: and it is that you join with sutures the four ends, of the suture and the two ends of the bandage, in one go, with a needle which is wrapped evenly in fineness and thickness.
And then, when you have pierced these four ends with the needle, withdraw the needle from the same part from which you began, so that the thread retracts from the most superior part of the wound, so that the suture is like a cloth stitch by which the cloths are tightened.
And place between each stitch the thickness of the little finger.
And this suture is known to men.
And with such a suture I healed the wound which had befallen a man in his belly, which had been wounded with a knife, and the rupture of the wound was more than a palm, and the intestines had protruded about two palms from the middle part, and the rupture was in the middle of the limb; and then I reduced that rupture after the intestines had been outside for 24 hours.
And the wound was consolidated in the space of fifteen days or so, and I cured him and he is healthy; and afterwards he lived for many years, and he is renowned for his good condition.
And the other physicians had judged that he would not be cured; and by miracle it was, for I did not cure him with ointment, for it was in a place where ointment was not found nor any medicine, but I applied a piece of old cloth twice a day and washed it diligently with honey water until it was healed.
But I shall tell you two kinds of sutures proper, according to the account of Paraula.
And it is that you begin with a suture of the thread, and you insert the needle from the outer part to the inner part.
And thus, as the needle pierces the thread and in that same laceration, according to its length and across the entire body, and leaves the end of the thread in which you have inserted the needle; and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inside to the outside on the other end of the thread.
And when you have made that penetrate, then you make it penetrate again in that same end of the thread from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread that is on this side, and make the needle penetrate the other end from the inside to the outside.
And make that penetration with your penetration, with which you make that penetrate into the thread at the end of the thread that is on his side, until all of that penetrates.
And then, starting from this same side, stitch it with the end of the thread on the other side, and remove the needle from the thread that penetrated, and then return that to that thread and stitch the end of the thread that is on the other side with this end of the thread; and remove that from the thread that is on his side.
And do that once and again, and do that once, then again, until you stitch everything that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suturing is that you stitch with a skin-like suture, with which you sew skin, so that you stitch the thread once from the side of the thread's end, and then leave it another time until you have completed it.
However, the second type of suture, properly called the Gaulish suture, and this is as he and by him recounted, is that you sew it according to a similar method with which some medics stitch it, in such a way that they add each part to its corresponding part to him conveniently by nature; and they apply one end of his and the end of the thread to the other end of him, and this is done according to what I have recounted to you.
You should place the needle at the end of the thread near the exterior part, and make it penetrate to the interior in that same place, and leave the two ends of the thread; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from the exterior to the interior in the two ends of the thread.
And then turn each of those, and make each penetrate from the interior to the exterior in two other ends of the thread that are on the opposite side.
And this manner of suture is better than the common, simple suture, and is the suture of which the previous remedy spoke.
This is the Gaulish method.
And some may say, the suture of the belly is rectified with two needles; this means that you insert a thread into each of these, and begin with the insertion of the needle from your side, and make it penetrate to the other side according to the suture of those who know the skins equally.
And know that when the rupture is in the middle of the belly, then the suture there is more difficult than in other parts of the belly.
But the judgment of the treatment of plague is the judgment of the other plagues.
Which means that when you catch the plague fresh, with its blood before the air alters it, and you return the intestines and stitch it up and prepare it properly, then apply a flesh-forming powder to it.
And if the air has already altered the plague, then apply some ointments which you have on hand until they form a hard stone and the fibers fall out, and be consolidated and observed; then treat it just as you would treat other plagues until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines on hand, then apply immediately, as soon as it begins to cast off pus, a strong poultice, and change it twice a day, as you were taught, until it is healed, for you will not need other treatment in many cases when the plagues are simple.
But if you fear that the plague will spread to the major limbs, then you must soak some linen in oil of tempered quality, or in warmed oil, and place that around the areas between the ribs and under the armpits.
And if he feels pain or putrefaction in his bowels, for this often happens, then cauterize it with black astringent wine, especially if the ulcer has penetrated into the cavity of his bowel and has made a penetrating exit.
And know that that which is in the large intestine is easier to heal than that which is in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal, for the intestine called "jejunum" does not receive healing from a plague that settles upon it completely; and this is because of the large multitude of veins within it, and because of its size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and because of its proximity to the nature of nerves.
But if what arises from the plague is a membrane, and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your technique for reducing the intestines as well.
But if it has spread to the surrounding area and it is already green or black, then you should compress that area with a cloth over the blackened spot, to bring about blood flow, for there are veins and arteries in the membrane.
And then cut what is beside that ligament, and hang the two ends of the cloth over the area below the plague, so that the pull from the affected area towards the joining of the membrane and the emission of the plague's arm is lessened.
A reminder of the plagues when they occur in the intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine, and it is small, it is possible for it to be restored in some individuals.  For example, I saw a man who had been injured in his abdomen by a spear thrust, and the plague was on the right side of the stomach. The plague was old, and had formed a fistula, and expelled gas and wind.
Then I considered his treatment, though I was not confident in his recovery, but I continued to treat it until it was healed and the area was consolidated.
And after I saw the area healed, I observed the patient for any further abdominal problems.  No further complications arose, and he remained in his usual state of health, eating and drinking, and then bathed and engaged in his activities.
And some experienced individuals have said that when a small intestinal injury occurs, it should be treated in this way: you should take large-headed leeches; then, join the two edges of the wound, and place one of these leeches with an open mouth on the two edges of the wound.  Then, when it has attached itself and closed its mouth, sever the leech’s head, because it adheres and is not released.
Then place another leech near the first, and do not stop doing this with leeches, one after the other, according to the size of the whole wound.
Then reduce the swelling and the wound; and those leech heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is healed; and no harm comes to the patient.
And it is possible that the intestine be joined with a thin thread, which is made from the intestine of an animal, joined to it, after it has been put through a needle; and you should take the end of this intestinal thread, and it should be very lean, and then tie that end to the injured intestine, wrapped around it, and then thread that thread through needles and stitch the intestine together, and reduce that into the rupture.
And this method of suturing with leeches is only according to a way of trust and hope.
However, if the wound is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the delicate intestines, for then there is no remedy but subtlety, and there is no complete healing of it.
Of the treatment of ulcers and fistulas.
Know that any wound or abscess, when it ages and becomes old, and has become an ulcer, and is not healed and continues to discharge pus constantly and is not incised, then, in any limb, is universally called a fistula, and we call that an ulcer.
And a fistula, according to truth, is a hardened, tough, whitish knot, with which there is no pain, and which has a cavity just like the cavity of a bird's nest; and therefore we call that an ulcer, called "rized ulcer," or  "nest ulcer.
And in some people it is moist, which discharges pus; and very easily the moisture is incised in a few hours. And sometimes this moisture is thick, and is thin, and is large, and is subtle.
I have already shown all those causes, and the signs of them, and their treatment with medicines in the division.
And it is necessary that I make known to you the treatment of a fistula, which is done by means of surgery with a hand.
And know that fistulas occur in all parts of the body; for some fistulas reach the major veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the spinal cord, or the intestines, or the bladder, or to any of the ribs, or to any of the vertebrae of the spine, or to any of the compound joints, such as the wrist and ankle, and others that affect the area close to the main limb and similar locations.
And therefore, those fistulas that are in this manner are among the difficult-to-heal illnesses, and do not receive a cure; and to work on them with ignorance is useless effort.
And if there is any fistula whose depth does not reach these locations that I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to gain knowledge of it, then try with a probe or an iron, and if the fistula goes in a straight line, follow it with the probe.
But if the fistula is winding, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead, for lead, with the lightness of its substance, is easily bent towards the winding path.
And if the fistula has many horrifications, it is not possible for you to mark it with a probe.
Then irrigate the orifices through their orifices, for the moisture with which you irrigate it will travel to the other openings and run through them.
And then inquire meticulously, by whatever means you can, to know if there are bones or nerves, and whether the fistula's cavity is distant or close, or whether it is a fistula with many openings, and consider all this with all your strength, with your questioning, with which you question the patient, and the location of the pain by pressing with your hand on the area, and the meanings and causes of the abscess.
And when you have considered all these factors with true judgment, then proceed to treatment according to your judgment: and you must now consider whether the fistula is a superficial, close, or exposed fistula; or one in a salty area, distant from a joint, or a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places I have told you.
Then dry the fistula as previously described, and remove what is in that filtering process and the corrupted flesh, and what has arisen in the opening of the flesh, or of pus and the like, and treat it until it is healed.
But if the fistula is deep and extensive, then it must be incised to the best of your ability, and then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh; then apply some bandages of sharp medicines and insert them into the fistula cavity, which you have opened with an iron instrument.
And do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine has corroded all that is rotten in the fistula's cavity.
And then restore it with ointments that generate healthy flesh until it is healed.
But if it is not healed by this, then cauterize it as we have described.
However, if the cause of the fistula is bone, and you are certain of this, then you should investigate and dry it as we have described, unless you see any reason to avoid affecting a vein, nerve, or major limb, as we have said.
But if the bone is exposed and there is any corruption or blackness on it, then you must scrape it until all the corruption is removed; and then treat it with something to make it flesh again until it is healed.
And if it is not healed and still discharges pus as before, then know that you have not fully eradicated the corruption; and then you must expose it again carefully, thoroughly scrape and clean it with your strongest tools, and then restore it.
And if it is healed, good; but if not, then know that the corruption is deep within the body, beyond your reach, and in that case, there is no remedy but to leave it.
And if the corrupted bone is small, or it is possible for you to remove it, then you should remove it with thin forceps suitable for this.
And if there are many bones, then carefully remove all of them, and leave nothing behind with your strength.
But if any moisture causes you difficulty, and there is no fear or mistake in their incision, then cut those ligaments or flesh if there are any, and free the bones.
But if you are not prepared to remove the bones immediately, then place something over the wound that might protect it, and leave it until it is surrounded by flesh and ligaments, and keep the wound from healing, so that the incision you made in your operation is contained.
However, if you are concerned, place on it cotton soaked in crushed oil, or cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, or in some other green ointment, because when those ligaments soften, and the bones are separated from them, the removal and extraction will be easier.
But if there is a large bone, like a leg bone or thigh bone or similar, and only the surface is corrupted, then cut that with a rasp until the blackness and corruption are removed, and then restore the wound.
However, if the corrupted portion is large, and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to cut that entire area, cutting into the spot where the corruption has spread, and then treating that until it heals.
And I will tell you what happened to a man in his thigh, so that you may use it as an example and aid in your operation.
There was a young man, around 30 years of age, who experienced pain in his thigh, and from the parts within the body, the matter spread until it reached the thigh, and it was a matter of an abscess with a large abscess, and it was not an external cause.
And so the time passed with that, through the doctors' error, until the abscess opened, and a lot of matter escaped, and its cure was delayed until the leg became fistulaed, and many openings appeared in it, which all discharged pus and the body's humors.
Then a multitude of doctors treated him for about two years, and none of them were truly skillful in the art, until I was called.
And I saw his leg and the matter flowing from those openings, a great deal of it; and the man's entire body was emaciated, and his complexion was yellowish.
Then I inserted a probe into one of those openings, and the probe reached the bone.
And I then probed all the openings, and found them to extend to all parts of the leg.
Then I carefully incised one of those openings until I exposed a part of the bone, and found it decayed, already earthy and corrupted and blackened; and a part of the leg was already decayed, having penetrated down to the marrow.
Then I closed what I had uncovered and prepared of the exposed bone.
And I concluded that it was not in the bone itself, but that corruption which I had cut away and closed, and I had already cured it.
And then I applied restorative medicine for a long time, but it did not heal.
And then I reopened and exposed the bone again, on top of the first exposure, and found continued decay with the bone.
And I removed what seemed to be that decay, and then I was forced to perform the cure and restoration, and it was not restored.
And then I exposed it again, and I did not stop cutting the bone little by little, specifically, and forced its restoration, but it was not restored until I cut around a hand's breadth of it, and extracted that with its marrow, and then I restored it with medicines, and it was slightly consolidated and healed.
And no further repetition of the operation and section is needed except for the weakness of the patient, and due to their little tolerance and fear of death, for in the patient, there occurred at all times a harmful syncope (fainting) due to the excess of evacuation.
Therefore, it is completely healed, and there is no hard, fleshy tissue in place of the bone; and its condition in the body is now good, and its strength has returned; and it has been restored to its original condition, and it experiences no difficulty in walking or harm in any way.
However, if the bone is prominent in a part of the body already fractured, then you must cut it in this way: take a bandage and tightly bind it to the end of the protruding bone, and order someone to pull on it; then place a thicker wool bandage over the first bandage; then bind that over the flesh below the bone, and pull on the end so that you draw the flesh down, and expose the flesh over the place where you wish to cut, so that the cut does not damage the flesh.
And place a stick or board under the bone properly, for when you do this, the cut will not be prevented from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And the incision should be made over the corrupted area little by little, for if the corruption is within the cavity of the bone and does not appear on its surface to the touch, it may need to be incised again.
And if the bone is corrupt but not protruding, with one part still connected to the other, and the corruption is in the middle or a portion of it, then you must uncover all the surrounding flesh; and then place a stick underneath, and then cut from the first part where the corruption is until the cut continues to the other part.
And let the incision be made along the location of the corruption, a little at a time, as we have said.
And if the corruption is in the joint, cut that corrupted joint, and saw the bone where it is connected to the other part.
And if the corruption is in the continuity of two joints, then it is not in a rational place but is unnatural.
And if the corruption is in the flesh of the hand or foot, it is very difficult; however, you must investigate the corruption in whatever manner appears to you, and expose it, and clean it according to any possible disposition, and with whatever ingenuity is available to you, so long as it does not impede or injure a vein or nerve.
And know that the incisions and saws used to cut these bones are very much dependent on the location of the bones, their preparation, their thickness, their subtlety and size, and their hardness and rarity. Therefore, you must prepare suitable instruments for every type of operation.
And understand that this operation demands knowledge of the specific instruments you will need; for great study, long experience, and understanding of the methods of this art, and experience with illnesses, are necessary, so that you yourself know what instruments are appropriate for every illness.

And if that fails, then you should make an incision in the arm up to the elbow joint.

And similarly, for the foot, when corruption reaches the toe, then you should incise it up to one of the joints.
And if it spreads to the sole of the foot, then you should incise the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then you should incise the leg up to the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached the knee, then there is no remedy but to allow the patient to die.
And the method of incising or cutting the limb is that you should tighten the ligament in the area just below the part you want to cut, and tighten another ligament on the spot; and let the assistant pull a ligament downwards, and let another assistant pull a ligament upwards; and you separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments, holding it apart with a spatula until all the flesh is exposed; and then cut or sever it.
And it is necessary that a cloth be placed on all sides so that the area is not touched, and that the patient experiences a controlled, contained wound and abscess.
But if you encounter a flow of blood during the operation, then you should cauterize the bleeding spot carefully, or place some blood-stopping powders on it; and then return to your procedure until it is finished.
And then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it is healed.
And I relate to you what happened to a man on his foot, the same accident that I am relating to you, which is that he suffered a blackening on his foot, with a burn similar to a fire burn.
And that corruption began first in his toe until it spread to his entire foot.
And then the man himself, seeing the treatment was not reaching the limb with the intensity he needed because of the pain and the burning, incised the foot right up to the joint, and it healed.
For a long time, the same corruption happened to him on the tip of his index finger.
And then he came to me, and I was forced to counteract that excess with what I could apply to his hand from among the medicines, to purify his body; but the excess was not counteracted, and it began to spread to the other fingers until it spread to his whole hand.
And he urged me to cut his hand, but I did not want to do that, hoping for a counteraction of that excess, and more than fearing the death of his hand due to the incision, for the patient's strength was wavering.
And after he left me, he returned to his own land, and it came to me that this man had prepared himself and cut his entire hand, and it healed.
And I have not related this story except so that it may be helpful in understanding this illness, and that it may be a sign by which it may be recognized and according to which it may be treated.
Of the treatment of sinuses and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess develops on a fleshy part of the body and the abscess cavity extends to collect pus, and it then bursts and is opened, and the pus comes out, leaving a hollow space like a vessel, and the skin covering it is thin, and has not progressed to further decay, and does not affect the bone, nerves, or ligaments, it is called a sinus, and not a fistula.
However, if the cavity extends so that the decay affects any part of the body, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the treatment of it is to cut away that skin completely, as it is turned back, and especially when it is thin and like a cloth, and to make sure that it does not adhere to the area because of its decay.
But if you suspect that the skin is adhering and that the decay has not yet spread further, and there is a wound on the skin, then you treat it with a clyster.
And if you consider it a sinus, and if it is large, and the discharge has a foul odor, then administer a clyster with Egyptian medicine, which is made of oil, sour wine, and vinegar in equal parts, mixed in a vessel, heated over a fire until the medicine becomes thick and resembles honey.
Then take what is necessary from it and dissolve it with water and honey; and administer this mixture as a clyster to the sinus, and tighten the opening, and leave the medicine there for two hours.
And then remove it with a compress; and do this for so many days until the sinus is cleansed and the odor is gone.
And sometimes, if this medicine isn't available, it's administered with water mixed with ashes.  Take the ashes from a fire, mix them with water, then strain it and administer this as a clyster until you are confident that the sinus is closed.
And if the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine or the ash water, then administer a clyster with water and honey, and some crushed vinegar mixed in, or a clyster with wine and honey mixed together, for honey's property is to cleanse and wash, and wine causes the sinus to adhere, especially when the wine has an excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and are confident that the sinus is healed, then administer a clyster with something that promotes the growth of flesh there.
And you should take diafinicon ointment, dissolve it in warmed oil and astringent wine; or administer a clyster with other medicines and ointments that have the properties which I have already written about in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the sinus is narrow, preventing the clyster tube from entering, then widen it gradually with a small iron tool, and apply heat wrapped in Egyptian or Basil ointment until it is widened.
And similarly, if the opening is wide, then you should bring the lips of the wound together with stitches and leave them in a manner that allows the clyster tube to enter without difficulty.
And if the opening through which the fistula runs is elevated to the upper parts, then close it in the lowest area and allow the fistula to run downwards, for when the fistula is located deep within the sinus, the flesh will grow there.
And if you cannot close the sinus down to the lower bone as desired, then force yourself to prepare the limb with a preparation so that the fistula runs easily as you have prepared, and so that no cause of the fistula is retained within it.
But the one over the sinus opening, which remains at all times, so that what is accumulating within the fistula may exit. And so that you may know if the discharge from the fistula is copious or scant, or if it is digested or not.
And by this, consider the location of the sinus; if the patient feels pain there, and if an abscess is present.
And as your treatment extends in this way, and you see the matter that has collected over the sinus opening has adhered or is showing signs of adhesion, then, on the third day, detach it, and when you detach the scab, change the cloth and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus a subtle, spreading virus has emerged, then do not despair of the adhesion, for healing in such cases tends to take time, as there are some people in whom the generation of flesh in their wounds is slow, and others in whom the opposite is true.
And if from the sinus for many days undigested matter has emerged, then know that the sinus has not adhered; and if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and the cause prolonged, then you should adjust the drying effects of the medicines, and if the medicines are moist in their substance and in their virtue, dry them like *unguentum diafinicon*, when the superfluous addition of pitch is adjusted to it.
And the final remedy with which it is cured is to take *unguentum diafinicon*, dissolve it in warmed oil, and add ancient wine to it, which is thickened in its substance, and mix this together thoroughly, and use this remedy.
And similarly, in the absence of matter, use *unguentum diafinicon*, honey when it is heated until it is thickened, and apply this; or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, grind them all into powder over honey that has been heated on the fire, then spread this on a cloth and apply the cloth to the sinus; and apply the honey, which you have heated until thickened, and sprinkled over a cloth, and press it against the sinus.
Sometimes, aristolochia longa, roots of the lily of the valley (and it is the lily of the valley), and the flour of eryngo and centaury are administered with it.  Grind and sift these herbs into powder over the honey, as previously mentioned; and use that.
And if the sinus is prolonged and not cured by this treatment, then know that the corruption is in the bone beneath it, or in other hard members or ligaments.  Then treat it with fistulous treatment, as was described above.
Of the treatment of felon (panaricium), and of the injured or damaged nail, and of the incision of the afflicted finger, and of the section.
A felon (panaricium) is an abscess that arises beneath the nail of the finger of the hand or foot, and it often arises on other fingers.  When it is prolonged and its treatment is less successful, or is an abscess of a severe abscess, or is corrupt, and it sheds matter until it rots the roots of the nail, and often corrupts the entire nail, and often spreads its corruption to the bone until it produces a foul odor, and the end of the finger becomes discolored and dark.
And therefore, when you have done as we have said in the division and your treatment does not succeed, then you must cut away all the excess matter that remains from the nail with a knife, and then cauterize the wound with it, for cautery is very helpful in this.
However, if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the nail bed has already begun to compress the flesh in the affected area, and begins to pierce and irritate the area, then you should place a fine blade under the nail's edge where it is piercing the flesh, and lift that flesh and cut it easily.  Apply burning and corrosive medicines to what remains of the flesh until it is all removed; and then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already reached the bone, then you must cut into the bone and remove it, for a bone that is corrupted will never heal.
And if the corruption and decay spread to the finger, then you should cut into it at one of its joints, according to how its recovery is expected, and then treat it until healed.
But if the nail has suffered a blow or abrasion, and there is intense pain, then you should initially perform bloodletting on the patient, and then carefully cut the nail with a sharp blade in a transverse cut from top to bottom, being careful not to cut into the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause the patient intense pain, and cause a buildup of excess flesh in that area.  Then treat that area with whatever remedies alleviate pain.
But a swollen finger, which is often found in some men's hands, is easily swollen in the flesh, and easily swollen in the bone, or easily swollen in the nail.
And some of these swellings are born at the joint, or their origin is in certain joints of the fingers. But the one that is born in certain joints of the finger is not harmful, and the one that is born in the joints is easily inflamed.
And for those that are fleshy swellings, the incision is gentle, and you should cut it away from its base with a broad blade.
But for the one that is born at the base of the joint, the cure is difficult; therefore, be careful of severing it.
But for the one that is born on the finger near the joints, you should initially cut the flesh around it in a circular incision to the bone, and then trim it with one of the suitable saws for this, and then treat it until it heals.
However, if the fingers become fused together – either naturally or due to a birth defect, a plague, a burn, or similar causes – then it is necessary to sever that fusion until the fingers return to their natural shape.
And then place between them strips of cloth soaked in heated oil, so that they do not fuse together prematurely, and separate them, or place a thin sheet of lead between them until they are healed properly.
And similarly, if a fusion occurs between a finger and the palm, then sever that fusion as the preparation and shape of the limb requires.
Of incisions of veins, they are varicose veins.
Veins are varicose veins, and are thick veins filled with melancholic excess fluids that accumulate in various parts of the body, and predominantly in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and those who carry heavy loads.
Therefore, you should administer purgation of the body of black bile several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
However, their treatment with iron is in two ways: one is that the veins are incised and that black blood is drawn out, and the other way is that the vein is raised and the whole thing extracted.
The section of it is done in this manner: First, soak the leg in very hot water until the thick, feculent blood in the leg is dissolved, then tightly bind the patient's leg from the highest part of the thigh down to the groin with a bandage.  Then cut the vein at one, two, or three places with a wide incision.  Next, raise the black blood with your hand from the bottom of the leg upward, and downward to upward, until the desired amount of blood has been drawn and the patient's condition is relieved.
And then bandage it, and instruct him to abstain from foods that generate black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are filled and this benefits the patient.
But their elevation is performed in this manner: you are to lift the patient's leg, if it is very swollen, and then immerse it in a bath and soak the leg in hot water until it turns red and the veins are enlarged; or, if a bath is not available, to be exercised vigorously until the limb is warmed.
Then cut the cord directly over the vein by incision, along the back of the leg below the groin, or at the lowest part of the leg below the ankle.
Then open the cord with hooks and scrape the vein from all sides until it appears sensitive, and it is at the point where you see the red turning to black.
And then, as you open it, from the cord you see that white which is the string.
Then place a reed under it so that it is raised and separated from the cord, and suspend it with a hook or forceps.
Then make another incision near that one, three days later, and then scrape the cord from the part above the vein until it appears.
Then raise that, and cut it in a second incision towards the heel, and then pull that out and raise it until it comes out through the second incision, and then pull it out of the incision above it.
Do this until you extract it from the incision that is highest or most superior of all the incisions, until everything is out; and then cut it.
And if you are unable to extract and elevate it, then insert a needle with a strong double hook and tie it and pull it out, or insert a reed underneath it and move your hand around in all directions until it is thrown out. Be careful not to cut it; for if it is broken, it is very difficult for you to elevate it, and it has become a problem for the patient due to that damage.
And when you have entirely expelled it, then place on the affected area wool soaked in wine and rubbed oil and un-rubbed oil, and treat it until it is healed.
And if the vein is constricted, having a tortuosity to which it is wrapped in parts, and is not straight as we have said, then it is necessary that you cut along every part of its tortuosity and at the places where it is wrapped, and then suspend it with hooks until you have lifted the whole thing.
And be careful in your incision not to cut the vein and not to damage it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and use your prudence in this.
Form of the incision with which a vein is cut:
It is not sharp like hooks, nor has a sharp tip so that it does not damage the vein; and it is of considerable flexibility, like a lion's paw, for if it were thin, it would cut the vein because of its thinness; however, it has a superfluous thickness as we said.
Of the extraction of a vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, just like in the lands of the Arabs, and in the hot and arid lands of India, where little vegetation grows, and neither grass nor trees flourish.
And it is easily generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is from putrefaction arising from beneath the skin, like snakes, worms, and maggots that breed between the skin and flesh.
And a sign of the onset of this vein is a vehement inflammation in the leg; then the area becomes blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the area of that blister, as if it were a plant's root.
And then, when its extremity appears, you should wrap a lead weight around it, which should be the weight of a drachma.  Up to two drachmas, and bind it and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
And as many times as any cause of this is outside the parts, wrap that in lead and bind it. And if it is prolonged, then you should incise a part of it and wrap the remainder; and do not incise that part until it has fully emerged, for if you incise it while it is still inside, it will retract into the flesh, causing an abscess and possibly a foul ulceration in that area.
Therefore, it must be treated gently and extracted little by little, until it is completely expelled from behind it and no part of it remains inside the body.
And from this vein, some men have had it grow to a length of five palms and ten fingers, and it has come to my attention that one has grown to twenty palms in length.
And if it is incised during treatment, then insert the probe into its opening and penetrate it with a long probe, until all that is in the matter is evacuated, and administer treatment for the putrefaction of the area for several days with medicines; then treat it like a festering wound.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot or on the foot, and has many openings, and a branch emerges from each opening.
Therefore, treat it in the same way as we have said in the division and with the things previously mentioned.
Of the sectioning of worms generated under the skin, and it is called the bovine disease.
This illness is called "bovine disease" in some lands near us, because often it affects cattle.  It's a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh, and it travels throughout the body, moving up and down, and its presence is felt as it moves from limb to limb until it breaks through the skin and emerges.
And its generation is due to the putrefaction of certain humors, just as worms, snakes, and other intestinal worms (lombrics and ascarids) are formed within the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice, for when it travels through the body and ascends to the head, and reaches the eye, it often causes severe inflammation there and enters, destroying the eye.
And therefore, when you wish to cure it and extract it, it is only done by following its movement and appearance as felt, and then you must tightly bind and constrict both what is above it and below it, and then carefully cut around it and extract it.
And if it is hidden within the flesh and you cannot find it, then you apply a cautery with fire to the area until it dies.  The most feared consequence is the corruption it causes, which corrupts the area, as we have said.
And if you see this creature near the head, close to the eye, then bind tightly around the eyebrow, and then cut around it and extract it.  The patient should use medicines that evacuate bad, putrid humors from the body, and should avoid foods that cause putrefaction.
Of the section on the illness of the nafir.
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is pain that comes upon certain limbs, and it then moves from limb to limb.
And I have already seen that, as I have told you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I examined her arm and saw a small swelling in the vein near the arm's joint.  After remaining there for an hour, I saw this swelling move like a worm climbing up it, but more quickly than it would seem possible, as if it were living silver moving from place to place.
And then the pain went to that place and settled in the muscle, and there it stayed for an hour.
And then it moved to the other arm until it reached the other arm.
And then it continued to move throughout her entire body as I watched; and I was very amazed by its movement and the speed with which it traveled from one limb to another. And I had previously seen this sort of thing with my own eyes, but I have seen many cases where pain travels from limb to limb. But I did not see this with my own eyes like this in a woman.
And I did not think it was anything else, but rather because the woman was old, with dry body and exposed veins, and in this way the wind-like movement appeared to the senses.
And it is necessary that this sort of retro-healing not appear in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
And if you want to cure them, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not immediately apparent to the eye as we have said, so you should gently and slightly press on the area until that constricted wind-like movement is released, and then cauterize the location.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes, then you should treat it with remedies for the body and with things that expel wind, and cure it with smelly pills and pills of Serapion and the like.
Of the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their types and the parts of the body they strike, for their diversity is that some have large points, and small ones, and concave, and blunt ones, that is, solid ones; and some have three angles, and some have tongues; and some have wings.
And the signs of the brain when an arrow strikes it, and pierces the bone, and emerges from the skull, which is over the brain, are sudden violent pain, and dizziness, and vertigo, and redness in the eyes, and inflammation and redness of the tongue, and spasms and confusion of thought, and vomiting of bile, and a strong voice is cut off and the voice is lost, and a white moisture like dust comes out of the wound, and the surrounding area looks like the flesh is becoming liquid.
Therefore, if you see these signs, then you refrain from treating and extracting the arrow, unless it is out in the open.
But the signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is near the left breast, are that the arrow is stuck in a hard, solid substance, and not in an empty space; and the arrow's movement is strongly similar to the movement of the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, and the extremities become cold, and cold sweat breaks out, and syncope occurs; therefore, know that death is imminent without any doubt.
And the signs when the arrow has struck the lung are the coming forth of frothy blood from the wound, and the blood vessels near the neck become inflamed, and the patient's complexion changes, and they breathe heavily, and crave a cold breath.
And if the arrow strikes the membrane in the foot, the pain then is near the small ribs, and the breath becomes heavy with intense pain and sighing, and all the muscles of the limbs are not working properly.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then easily some undigested food comes out of the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen, and is embedded there, there is some kind of rupture from the wound, either of the tissue, or of the intestines, which is torn, and there is no hope for the treatment or removal of the arrow.
And if the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine is coming out of the wound, and there is some cause for this discharge from the outside, and severe pain affects the patient, then know that it is fatal.

And the procedure for bloodletting him (or her) is that the patient should place their foot in hot water and rub it until the veins are visible; and tie the area around the foot with a bandage; and the location of the vein, which becomes visible near the toe, has many small branches running across the surface of the foot.
And therefore, bloodlet the widest branch, either below the leg towards the heel or towards the area of the swelling, because it is better and more effective.
And if you bloodlet that on the surface of the foot, then be careful of the nerves that lie beneath the surface of the foot, and make your bloodletting cut across it, like if you were trying to split it.
And let the bloodletting be done carefully.
But if the release of blood is forbidden; then you immerse the patient's foot in hot water and let the blood flow into it until it is evacuated.
And if the bloodletter errs the first time, then repeat the bloodletting a little further, because the area is sensitive; and don't fear harming them, but rather be careful of the nerves as we have said.
And similarly do the same for the sole of the other foot as well.
And for the sciatic vein, the location, as we have said, is below the heel, below the bone of the outside part.
And the benefit of bloodletting for him is for hip pain when it is caused by hot blood.
And the method of bloodletting for him is that the patient enters a bath, and tightly bandages the thigh from the wide part up to above the heel with a long, thin bandage for four days, because it only appears by this means.
And therefore, when it appears, then you bleed him according to the best possible method and according to the transverse way, and it is better; either cut it with an incision or cut it with a section, for the location is saline.
And it is in many men very hidden; and if you find it, and it does not appear to the touch, then bleed any branch of it, and it is that which appears on the back of the foot near the so-called auricular and annular (areas/structures).
And beware of the nerves, and let the blood flow the amount you desire.
And then loosen the constriction and place a cloth over the site of the bloodletting and bind the area tightly, for it will be quickly healed.
Of cupping and the qualities of their use.
Cupping is of horns, or of earth, or of glass.
And cupping is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and the extraction of blood, and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping that is without scarification is in two ways, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And for cupping that is done with scarification and blood extraction, there are fourteen locations on the body, of which one, where cupping is applied, is the nape of the neck, and it is the back part of the head; and the middle place of it is of two parts of the neck together; and cupping of the chin below the jaw, then below the mouth; and cupping of two shoulder blades; and cupping of the armpits, and it is above the end of the ribs; and cupping of two temples, and it is of two arms; and cupping of two calves; and cupping of two thighs.
And cupping does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore it does not exhaust the virtue through them, as is the case with bloodletting; nor is it proper for cupping to be done with or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the entire body has been evacuated.
But if necessity compels cupping for illness or for a persistent condition, administer it at any time, at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, in the middle, and at any other time.
Therefore, when in any of those cases, and specifically if it is in the middle third of the month, then we recommend cupping be done after two or three hours have passed.
And the benefit of cupping the neck is that when it relieves heaviness in the head and what is clouding the eyes; however, this should be with the evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of a phlebotomy of the cephalic vein.
And it is not permitted for those with a cold constitution, or those with a catarrh, to use it, for it brings great harm to them.
And therefore, it is not suitable for the elderly, or those with cold ailments in their heads, to use it.
But for whoever is continuously working on the patient, oblivion is likely; and therefore, you should command the cupper to slowly move the cup downwards, to prevent the generation of oblivion.
But cupping with a leech is in place of phlebotomy of the vena fusca and basilica: and therefore, it is effective for asthma, and constriction of the windpipe, and fracture of the windpipe, and coughs, and fullness.
