And the leech-cup should be lifted slightly, for if it is placed too low, it engenders weakness in the body and stomach.
And cupping with an *alhagdahan* is effective for accidental pains in the head and ophthalmia, migraine, feverishness, and pains in the roots of the teeth; and it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the basilic vein.
And you should instruct the cupper not to press too hard with the scarification, so that they do not cut the artery and cause a flow of blood.
And cupping beneath the chin is effective for colds in the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar illnesses in the mouth, and it is a substitute for phlebotomy of the veins in the lips.
But cupping on the two shoulder blades is effective against *caphatan* (a likely ailment, possibly heatstroke), which is caused by an excess of heat.
And cupping on the two parts inside the armpits is helpful for three vein phlebotomy – that is, the basilic, the cephalic, and the humeral vein – because it draws blood from all the smaller veins in the flesh, and these small veins draw from the larger ones until the drawing effect reaches the three major veins.
And instruct the cupper not to proceed too deeply with the scarification, for the area is bare of flesh, and below it are nerves and arteries.
And a cupping treatment applied to the buttocks is effective against hemorrhoids and ulcers in the lower parts of the anus.
And you must instruct the cupper to use a large, strong cupping glass, for the area requires a forceful suction;  weak, flimsy cupping glasses are ineffective, and the area should be scarified with a cut.
However, cupping of the legs is less effective due to its obvious limitations, as it draws blood from the entire body and helps with long-standing aches in the hips, back, bladder, and menstruation.  It can also ease pain and swelling, and serve as a substitute for phlebotomy using two needles, and later two cauterizing probes.
However, it greatly weakens the whole body and can cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit from these cuppings is similar to the benefits from cupping both legs.
And the proper application of cupping is to initially apply an empty cup and draw out the blood with moderate suction, and do not prolong the application of the cupping glasses, but rather place them lightly, and remove them gently, allowing the humors to return to the area gradually. And do not stop repeating this process until the area becomes red and swollen, and the redness of the blood is apparent.
And then you scarify and repeat the suction gradually, and then consider the disposition of the body.
For that person with delicate, easily bruised skin, you should scarify only once, to prevent the area from becoming ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification slightly, and to gently moderate the suction.
And if the blood is thick, then you should scarify twice, for in the first instance the path is opened for the blood and its moisture to be drawn out. But in the second, you take advantage of the knowledge gained from the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very impure, then a third scarification should be performed to achieve the final result.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood slowly or a little bit, one scarification is sufficient.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we scarify with many scarifications; and if we know that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to scarify with deep scarifications.
And the depth of the scarification should only be the length of a fingernail.
And if the skin over which the cupping is to be performed is thick, hard, dark, and its pores are tight, the places for the cupping glasses should be treated with soothing, opening, and resolving ointments; if it is summer, this could be almond oil, violet oil, or oil from soft almonds, or from poppy seeds; and if it is winter, then narcissus oil, lily oil, chamomile oil, or bloodroot oil, and similar ones.
And if the excess is thick and cold, then the oil should be amaranth oil, or balm oil, or almond oil, or dill oil, and similar ones.
However, if the cupping area is full of pores and has tender flesh, then it is necessary to avoid using oil, and instead, the areas around the cupping glasses must be washed with water or cool water from the solanum plant, or poppy water, or water from the borage plant, and similar ones.
And for someone whose blood has excessive moisture, wash the cupping areas with sour wine, or water with myrtle and sumac, and similar things.
And for someone whose excesses are thick, wash their cupping areas with old wine, or with water of yarrow (which is majorana), or with an infusion of dill, or with chamomile, and similar ones.
And the cupping should be avoided before a bath; after the bath, it should be administered approximately one or two hours later, and it is necessary that the patient rest after the cupping.
What is that which should be governed/controlled for the bloodletting or cupping before the cupping procedure?
One should consider at the start: if the person to be cupped or bled is choleric and has hot, inflamed blood, then they should take cooling things, such as milfoil (milgranas acetosas), endive in sour wine, lettuce, dry herbs (secanabin), juleps, and similar things.
And provide them with foods like small birds, and game birds' flesh, and foods seasoned with sour ingredients, and similar things.
And for one whose complexion is cold, they should drink syrup of licorice or syrup made from the seeds of alsecanabin, and sumac, and very fragrant wine—a mix between young and old—and they should be advised to consume little food and drink, and have as meals small birds, hemp seeds, pigeons, and small birds and doves.
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping and bloodletting, the drink should be more than the food.
And it is necessary that some people be given to drink at certain times theriac alpharac, or musks—that is, musk—or other medications before cupping or bloodletting, or shortly before, so that the main members are strengthened and the blood is refined; and they should not take those heated.
But the cupping that is done without incision should be cupping placed on the liver, the spleen, gout, and the navel, on the knee-caps, and the groin area, for these areas do not tolerate incision upon them.
And it is not desired by these [cuppings] except for drawing blood from a limb, just as we place a cupping glass on the breast in illness of blood flow from the nose; and we use this to dissolve cold, sticky airiness from the limb, just as we place one on the abdomen and the navel; because that one refines the limb, warms it, and eases the pain by resolving the airiness.
And it is already placed on the kidneys when they become blocked or filled with impurities, for by the virtue of strong suction, it quickly opens the blockages and eradicates the impurities from their location.
And similarly, it acts when placed on the liver and spleen to remove the airiness contained within them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty, with suction only; and sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in illnesses of the spleen.
And this is so that the cupping glass is filled and becomes larger; it is either just warm water or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
The form of a cupping glass administered with fire:  Let the width of the opening be two fingers, as we have shaped it; and the amount of heat be half a palm; and it should be on the patient's side, in the area that is approximately halfway around the body, the hole should be small, enough for a needle to enter.
Let it be made of red or citrus wood, with thick edges, smooth, and even so that it does not displace the limb from its position on the patient.
And in the middle of it should be a transverse rod of wood or iron, on which a candle with fire is placed.
And this cupping glass should be significantly larger than the one we have described; and the smaller one should be smaller depending on the ailments and the age of the person using it.
The cupping glasses for children and the weakened are different from those for young people and those with strong physiques.
And the method of applying this cupping glass with fire to the limb is to gently heat the cupping glass with wood fire in front of a small wax candle, and place this over the middle of the transverse rod in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is kindled around the lower part, to prevent the patient's body from being burned.
And then it should be placed on the patient's limb, and the finger should be over the hole we mentioned, until when the cupping glasses are applied and we do not need the finger anymore and the vapor escapes through that hole, then the cupping glass will immediately be detached.
And repeat the process according to its (the cupping) method, and repeat it if necessary.
But a cupping glass used for pleurisy, with water, in which there is no transverse rod or hole, and is not applied otherwise than being filled with water, and placed directly on the limb.
And this is its method:  The larger this cupping glass is, the better, so that it can receive a large quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not applied to many limbs, but only to those parts where cupping is impossible due to their small size, such as the foot and the groin, and similar areas; or to limbs lacking flesh, like the penis and the nose, and their like.
And the quality of using those leeches is found in still, clear waters of muddy bottoms.
And then they should be left in fresh water during the day and night until they are hungry, and no food remains in their stomachs; and then the body should be emptied at the beginning with bloodletting and cupping; and then rub the patient's limb until it turns red; and then the leeches should be placed on it.
And when they are full and engorged.
And if suction from the area with a cupping vessel is possible, it is better afterward for healing.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and afterward with clean water; and let it be rubbed and squeezed.
And if the body of blood is prolonged near the attachment of the leeches, and there is a discharge, then you should soak a cloth in cool water and place it over it until the discharge is stopped.
And if the blood is excessive, then sprinkle powdered vitriol, or other similar astringent substances, on it until the blood is stopped; or place roasted chickpea husks over the area, and leave them until the husks adhere to the location, and the blood is stopped.
And if necessary, repeat the application of leeches, when it is possible to replace them.
And if the leeches are forbidden for application, then the area should be rubbed with fresh blood or a needle should be inserted into the locations until blood comes out; and then they [i.e., the leeches] should be applied, for when they sense blood, they immediately attach.
And when you want them to detach, sprinkle a little powdered aloes, or salt, or ashes, on them, and they will detach quickly.
Thus begins the treatment for the third part: restoration. This chapter is very often necessary in the art of medicine; and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations accidentally occurring in the bones.
Oh, son, know that foolish and vulgar doctors, and those who have never looked at the books of the Ancients or read a single letter from them, boast of this chapter.
And for this reason, this method of learning in our land is neglected; and I have never forgotten this at all; and I have not adopted this from what I learned, but through the length of reading the books of the Ancients and studying their meaning, until I extracted knowledge from them.
And afterward I devoted myself to experiment and study throughout my life.
And I have now described in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge and upon which my experience has been passed; since I have summarized this for you and freed it from lengthy explanations; and I have summarized it at the end of the summary; and I have demonstrated this at the end of the declaration; and have formed in it many forms of the instruments that are administered in this, as is done by adding a declaration, just as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
First Chapter.
A summary and collection of the nature of bone fractures, and of the prerequisites before we begin to remember fractured limbs and their various types. Therefore, let us begin this chapter with a summary of the subject matter; and the chapters on which the artisans are initially required to understand the truth of those things; that is, you and those who are eager to know or learn this noble art, along with others who may follow.
Therefore, I say that when an accident occurs involving a fracture, dislocation, twisting, or injury, then it is necessary for you to begin with bloodletting or relieving the area, or both together, unless some cause prohibits it, such as weakness, or the person affected is a child, or an elderly person, or the time is one of intense heat, or intense cold.
And afterwards, the patient should be given food consisting of cool herbs and the meat of birds and rabbits; and we forbid heavy meats and filling foods, and all food that fills the veins with blood, until you are certain of avoiding a hot abscess, and if you do not fear the discharge of matter in the limb.
And then, return the patient to their former regimen, with which they are accustomed.
And thus, as the broken bone begins to be restored, it is necessary that the patient eat nutritious foods, of a substantial, thick, and heavy nature, in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and soups, and peas, and cow's stomachs, and eggs, and fresh fish, and thick wines and similar to those, and with this regimen the fracture is more easily and better consolidated.
And know that broken bones in the elderly and the very young are not likely to be joined and consolidated according to their nature because of the drying out of their limbs.
However, nature brings together and connects over the broken bone from all sides some substance similar to glue, in which there is thickness that adheres to and strengthens it until they are completely glued and bound together, until they reach the point of strength and firmness, just as fire at the beginning until no cause prevents it from its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that the patient eat foods that are heavy, viscous, and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the limbs, for a fracture of the leg bone is different from a fracture of the skull bone; and a fracture of the foot bone is different from a fracture of the arm bone; and similarly all the other limbs are different from each other.
And I come with a reminder of every type of fracture, explained in its own distinct chapter.
There is great diversity in fractures themselves. And the types of bone fractures themselves are diverse; for there is an arched fracture, and another where splinters occur; and sometimes a fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of the shaft, and splinters, and proceeding and non-proceeding additions, just as in a pen when it is prepared for writing, and there is a fracture with a break and a crack in the head, and there is a small fissure fracture.
And for every type, there is a specific method or treatment in its restoration, according to the memory of it being distinct in its places.
A clear sign of a bone fracture. And by this the fracture of the bone is known: it is its twisting, and its protrusion and appearance to the touch, and this is of it when you press it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the area, nor is it felt by your touch when you touch the bone with pressure and the patient does not experience great pain, then there is no fracture here.
However, it is possible that there is a twisting or small crack, so it is not necessary for you to manipulate it with your hand and with overall compression; instead, place on it the medicines from which a cure comes at that moment which is appropriate for the location; and then it should be made tight by a subtle constriction.
And know that when the place is bent and is not split in two or otherwise harmed in its alignment, as long as the entire part of its structure is intact, then it is necessary to begin at once to rectify it, before a painful abscess occurs there.
And if an abscess develops there, then leave it for a few days until the abscess subsides; and then treat it by whatever means you can of gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that the restoration and mending of soft tissue is easier than that of bone, where damage has occurred.
And then strengthen it according to the cure that follows.
But if the bone is damaged, then the broken member (be it hand or foot) requires extension of the two parts;  if the member is small, use your hand; or use two ropes or one rope and a hand.
And maintain the position in which you place the mended member according to its natural form, while the broken member parts are extended.  Then you must work with ingenuity to restore those additions to their proper places.
And watch, using your strength, that your procedure does not cause pain or injury to the patient.  Strive to your utmost to join the bone with its companion according to their best forms.
And it is necessary at that time to skillfully touch it with your hand, and if there is any different cause, correct and adjust it with the strength of your skill.
Avoid excessive extension and compression, as some foolish doctors do, often causing suppuration or causing contraction in the limb, as we have seen in their many procedures.
The appropriate level of restraint for the calmed limb is what I recommend; so that you know that broken limbs vary in their smallness and size, and in their form.
And for those that are small, such as the arm, finger, and toe, and similar parts, then the wrapping should be of a very thin, soft cloth.
However, for those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, a firm and strong wrapping is needed, for a firm bandage connects the large limb and binds it tightly all around, so that no fluid enters.
And now, begin your treatment, placing a suitable bandage over the fracture site, using three or four wraps depending on the limb's needs; and gently tighten the bandage with your hand.
And now, move towards the most superior part of the fracture site, and tighten the bandage less tightly than you did on the fracture site itself. And now, gently loosen the wrapping around the fracture site a little, and gradually ease the tightness, until a large part of the site is loosened.
And now, take another bandage and wrap it again over the broken site with a bandage, and now move the wraps to the lower part of the fracture; and let your procedure involve tightening the bandage and loosening it according to what we have said in the first bandage above.
And now, place between the wraps a pad of linen or cloth, to address any twisting in the fracture, if there is any; and if not, place no additional material there.
And now, place another bandage over these neatly made wraps, immediately, if there is no inflammation or a painful abscess in the area.  If there is, place on it something that reduces the abscess and relieves the inflammation; leave it for a few days, and then tighten the bandages.
Let the splints be made of half-cane, or well-formed wood; or of pine wood; or of palm fronds; and let the width of each splint be according to this shape and this figure:
However, the splint placed over the fracture should be slightly larger and wider than the others, and the length of the splint should match the size and smallness of the limb; and then tighten the splints with another bandage according to your first tightening; and then tie it securely on top with thread, as we have already stated about tightening,  meaning that your tightening should follow the location of the fracture most closely, and the further it is from the fracture, the looser the tightening.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and made of linen; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the operation of some fools, using ropes or thick twisted threads, that is a great error, when the tightening loses its evenness.
Likewise, thin threads are not suitable, for you will not achieve the desired tightening with them, and there should be at least one finger's width between each splint.
And if the patient is sensitive at the ends of the splints near the tight bindings, then place under them some soft linen or wool that is not irritating, until this does not cause him injury.
Sometimes a fracture occurs with a wound, and a separation happens in the bone, and I will return to the discussion of this in the second chapter on specific cases.
And know that not every broken limb should be bound with splints on the first day; the reason is that when the limb is large, you should not place splints upon it until approximately 5, 7, or more days, depending on your judgment about the possibility of a suppurative inflammation developing.
And this is the method of plasters with which the Ancients used to heal fractures, dislocations, and twisting injuries.
A method of a common plaster for fracture healing, suitable for many constitutions, and especially for women and children, because it does not induce heat or cold: take powdered flour, the pith of the flour, which comes from the grinding process in the walls of the mill.
And prepare this as it is, but sieve it with egg white, and apply the mixture neither thick nor thin; and use it thus.
Another method of plaster for healing fractures, dislocations, and twists: a recipe using asafoetida, ladanum, acacia, enula, and wild chamomile root, all 1/4 cup; myrrh and aloe, 1/8 cup; aloes, 2 cups; all ground and sifted, then mixed with rose water or egg white if the patient's constitution is hot.  Use this plaster, as it is a good composition and heals slightly fractured bones and is beneficial for many people.
Another method of plaster for fractures and twists:  recipe using wild chamomile and white marshmallow root, 1/4 cup; myrrh and aloe, 1/8 cup; acacia, 3/8 cup; Armenian bole, 2 cups.
All are ground in a mortar with much grinding, sifted, and mixed with water or egg white, and then applied.
A method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places, and to relieve accidental pain in them, and for fissures and fractures:  take absorbent wool and soak it in sour wine and cooked oil, and place it on the affected area.
However, this plaster does not have the virtue of restoration, but rather of relieving acute inflammation and expelling pain effectively.
Another method of plaster to restore a broken bone: take leaves of wild fig and leaves of wild poppy; crush them all together and make a plaster from them.
A shortened method of plaster for assisting in the restoration of a broken bone, and when you want resolution of remaining inflammation: take roots of marshmallow, chamomile flowers and violet flowers, and powdered yarrow; pulverize all, and mix with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
But if it is inflamed, then temper it with moist coriander water, or with water and vinegar.
Another, stronger type of plaster for resolving deep inflammation for the restoration of a limb: take roots of marshmallow, linseed, baluster, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile; pulverize them all, and mix them with willow water, or fresh water, or wine, depending on the nature of the limb and its colour.
However, Hippocrates in his book does not say that it should be placed on the broken limb during restoration, but rather a cerot (plaster) made of wax and oil, and nothing else; and recommends that it be of medium thickness, neither too thick nor too thin.
However, in Gaul, it is seen that these are placed on the broken limb during restoration, and the ingredients for which drying with little warmth are good, such as myrrh, frankincense, and similar substances to those we have mentioned.
A plaster for injuries and pain: take equal parts of cedar, human skin (or animal skin), marshmallow, and salt; grind and sift them, then mix to form a plaster.
However, regarding the amount of time the bandage should remain on, and when to remove it, consider if there is no pain, swelling, or if the bone is broken or dislocated. In this case, you should not remove the bandage for many days. And if intense pain, swelling, or inflammation occurs at the site, then you should remove or loosen the bandage immediately, and remove the plaster.
And take clean cloth and a dampened piece of sea sponge, soak it in lukewarm water, and wash the affected area with it until the swelling subsides and the pain is relieved; then allow the limb to rest for an hour.  Then place on the area a piece of wool soaked in sour wine and oil or olive oil, and bandage it for one night until you are sure there is no festering boil, and the limb's inflammation is reduced, and the pain is gone.
Then, repeat this delicate compression and small plaster application, and do not apply the initial pressure again, but use it gently until it heals.
And if the boil, hardness, pain, inflammation, and all other symptoms have completely subsided, and you no longer need a plaster and compression, then repeat the process as you did at the beginning in the same manner.
And if no symptoms like those we have mentioned occur in the limb, then do not remove the bandage for three, four, five, or seven days; and sometimes this is left on for twenty days, depending on how the limb looks, as we have mentioned, until the fracture adheres and the flesh around it coagulates.
Then, apply more pressure to the bandage than you did originally, and increase the patient's food intake, as the patient's recovery progresses.
But if you find the fracture site already dried out and weakened more than necessary, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it.  Therefore, bathe it with lukewarm water every time you remove the bandage, and do so every three days.  Ease the compression slightly, and by this action, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals more easily.
However, what foolish bone setters do, restoring the fractured limb a second time when it wasn't properly set initially,  and setting it in a twisted manner, is an error in their practice, and a great risk.  If they had done it correctly, the ancients in their books would have shown it, and would have worked this way.
And I have not seen any of them do this, which makes it more reasonable that you should not do it.
Second Chapter: Of fractures when occurring to the head.
The types of fractures are numerous, and their shapes are diverse, and their causes are distinct, for some fractures are caused by a blow from a weapon, and the blow crushes all the bone up to the skull, which is beneath the bone, just as it does to a tree trunk.  Therefore this type is called a crushed fracture; or the weapon cuts a portion of the bone, and cuts the flesh but does not penetrate to the back of the bone; and this type of fracture is called a simple fracture.
And the severity of these two fractures is either great or slight, and a fracture is either a crushing or a grinding; and the cause of them is a blow with a blunt object or a fall on a stone, and similar things.
And this fracture is either penetrating, coming close to the membrane beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone, and the severity of these two fractures is either great or slight.
And a fracture that is hidden within the bone, in the smallness of a hair, and because of that this type is called a capillary fracture.
And a fracture is made either by falling or by a blow from a stone and similar things, which causes a sliver of the bone to be driven inwards, and is made in the hollow part just as happens to the spokes of a wheel when they are struck and are splintered from their entrance to the inner parts.
And this is more often done in the soft bones of children's heads.
And all these types of fractures, there are fragments, like separated stars. And I will come with a restoration of all that to its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures by probing them, and by examining them with probes and the presence of decaying flesh on top of them.
A specific fracture is recognized because it is uncovered on the bone, and is scraped clean and probed, for the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of fractures, consider first the patient's symptoms, and if you see through those symptoms a clear indication of fear, such as vomiting, trembling, loss of understanding, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, and swelling and stiffness of the limbs, and similar symptoms in severe cases, without doubt it will not be cured.
And if you see symptoms that are not severe, you hope for recovery, then you begin the treatment of it.
The reason for this is that if the injured person comes to you at the beginning, as they are injured and it is during the winter days, then you must study the removal of the bone by the fourteenth day, according to all circumstances.
And if it is during the summer days, then you must attempt to remove the bone by the seventh day, before the tissue underneath the bone decays, and the symptoms we have described occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the tissue covering the brain, and is with fragmented and crushed pieces, then you must cut out the fragmented and crushed part, as I will describe to you.
And you must shave the head of the injured patient, and expose the bone according to the custom and manner possible for you, according to the nature of the injury, and that it is easy on the patient.
And if you encounter bone exposure, blood flow, or a hot abscess, then you must oppose it with what is necessary, and you should fill the area with cloths soaked in wine and rubbed oil, and allow the injury to rest until the abscess is calmed and the blood flow is certain; then begin the process of cleaning the bone and removing it.
And this should be done according to one of two methods of operation.
And one method is that you cut the bone with a narrow, sharp scalpel, and this is its form: And then you place another scalpel alongside it, a little wider than the first; and this is its form:
And then place another scalpel wider than the second one.
And you need a variety of scalpels, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, with the sharp edges of their ends, and they should be made of hardened or tempered steel.
And use them with ease in striking the scalpel so that you do not strike the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you need to make holes around it before you use the scalpels with the drilling tools, which are called non-deepening drills.
And they are not called that because they do not reach the surface of the bone close to it, for the drill has a rounded tip or edge on the one below the head, sharp like an edge or a small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take a large number of these drills, each suited to the thickness of each bone, so that you have on hand for all the skull bones, the tip of each being sharp, the length and shortness being according to the thickness of each bone.
And this is the method for three kinds of drills:  The method of drilling around a fractured bone is to place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know the bone is penetrated; then change the drill to another spot, and place whatever is between the holes the size of the bone's thickness or around that.
And then cut with scalpels what is between each hole in the bone, and make it as fine as possible, as light as possible, as we have said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand or with some other tool such as fine pliers or forceps.
And you must carefully ensure that the scalpel does not touch any part of the skull.
And therefore, once you have drilled around the bone, and if it is joined to the skull, you must separate it; and once you have extracted it, you must rasp and smooth the rough area of the bone with another instrument similar to a scalpel, but it must be thinner and more delicate than the other scalpels.
And if any small bone fragments remain, then remove them easily with your tools; and then treat the wound with liniments and ointments as we have described.
And another method of operation is easy, free from fear, which Gualtier and Launcelot highly praised; and this is their teaching: it is necessary for you at the beginning to begin to expose the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and noticeable, until when you have exposed that part and have placed under it the tip of this slow and gentle scalpel, of which this is the shape:
And it is that the lenticular part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the pointed part is bent in two directions, forward in length, as you see; so that the lenticular part is fitted to the skull with the pointed cutting face against the bone.
Then strike the scalpel on a part with a small mallet until you cut through the entire bone easily as it is turned, and you are sure that no accident will occur, whatever fear you might have.  And if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them, certainly, and if he were asleep...
Then, if any remains attached to the bone of the bandage in any part of the bone, cut it with the tip of that lenticular scalpel, and release it easily, for it is released without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the bandage, and whatever is cut away or removed from the bone is its surface, and there remain roughnesses and subtle irregularities, then it is necessary to smooth those roughnesses and eliminate those irregularities with fine razors, of which you have prepared a variety of sizes, so that you may be able to apply the most suitable ones to every part and the most appropriate ones according to where you are directing your operation on that broken bone and its shape.
And it is necessary that in your procedure, with which you smooth the bone, you begin with the widest of those razors, and then successively use the finer ones, and do not cease doing this continuously until you reach the finest and most subtle of all of them.
However, in each of the other small, capillary fractures and subtle fractures, it is necessary to administer treatment according to what you perceive as the rectification of them.
And it is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied in that art. And we have shown in this what we have written about major fractures.
And if the skin is pulled back when you expose the bone, then it is necessary that you take a cloth in the quantity of the wound, and immerse it in heated oil, and place it over the wound opening; and then take another cloth, folded or tripled, and immerse it in wine and heated oil, and place it over the first cloth, and do this as gently as possible, so that you don't aggravate the injury; and then apply a wide bandage, and don't tighten it except to the extent that the wound is held still only.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of a warm abscess; and then remove it, and then administer medicines that address excessive dryness, such as flax seeds, and rye flour, and powdered fenugreek and similar herbs.
And you should make a powder from these medicines, ground into powder, so that they are dry; and in general, administer any medicine which has the property of cleansing but not irritating.
And be careful, based on your skill, that the wound in your treatment remains clean; and that there is no pus or other foulness; and do not allow any further virulence to accumulate; for when it is added to the skin of the brain, it corrupts and rots it, and this causes a very serious and harmful disease in the patient.
And it affects the skull, especially when the bone is exposed and the treatment is neglected, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore, if you see the patient experiencing the symptoms we have described, know that it is dangerous without a doubt.
And if the blackness is not from the wound itself, but rather from the medicine applied, and that medicine is the cause of the blackness, then you should take one part honey and three parts of warmed olive oil, and with them, soak a cloth.  Then place it on the skull, and then treat it with the appropriate healing spices until it is healed.
Chapter 3.
Concerning the restoration of a broken nose.
Know that a broken nose is not simply broken in two; rather, it may be fractured in several places, sometimes only in one, because there are two bones in the nose.  The lower part is cartilaginous and not actually fractured, but is damaged by friction, twisting, and bending.
However, if only one of the two parts is broken, you should insert your little finger into the nose, and use your index finger to grasp the fracture, pushing outward with your middle finger, until the nose returns to its natural shape. And do this with ease, and be careful not to cause further pain to the patient because of your procedure.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, where the finger cannot reach, then it must be manipulated with the tip of a rounded, slightly thickened instrument (a type of probe/spatula).
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you should do the same procedure as described above.
And try to restore it on the first day of the fracture if possible; if not, then approximately on the 7th or 10th day, you should carefully treat the resulting inflammation.
And if the fracture is in one part, insert a small piece of linen into the nasal opening; or if the fracture is in two parts, insert two pads, each having enough thickness to fill the nasal cavity.
Some ancient healers advised that the pads be soaked in broth and changed daily, but I don't find this to be the best approach.  Instead, the pad should be soaked in a mixture of beaten, sweetened egg white with ground spice/powder; and leave it in place until the bone is set and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a pad, a linen bandage is used inside the nose, especially if the nasal fracture is severe and the patient is not able to breathe normally.
And this method is not strictly necessary; if you wish, use it, and if you wish, use the pads instead.
And if another boil appears, cover the nose with a cerot (plaster) or with cotton soaked in sour wine and olive oil, or with diachylon.
But if no further boils appear, then you should cover the existing one with barley flour and powdered herbs mixed with beaten egg white; then place a linen bandage over it and do not tie the nose with anything else.
But if the nasal bones are fractured, even slightly, or broken into pieces, then you must cut (remove) over those parts, and extract them with instruments appropriate to this; then close the fracture and treat it with things that promote healing and strengthening, and with suitable plasters.
And if a plague appears inside the nose, then you must treat it with pads and administer lead cannulae until it is completely healed.
Chapter 4.
Concerning the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture isn't accompanied by an infection, then consider whether the fracture is only outward, and not broken in two and bent inward, in which case its reduction is easy.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you must insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jaw, then insert the said index finger of your right hand; and push the protrusion of the fracture inwards easily towards the outside, with your other hand on the outside of the bone; gently bring them together.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to administer extension to both parts according to their alignment until their reunion is possible.
And if you encounter displacement of teeth along with a disruption, then secure those that you expect to remain with gold, silver, or silk thread; then place a compress on the fractured jaw, and then place a doubled cloth over it, and put a large, well-made bandage, or a piece of linen the length of the jaw, over that; and then bind it according to your skill in bandaging and the need for application until it is not damaged.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and give him easily digestible food.
And if you estimate that the form has been altered in any way, then you should remove and loosen it on the third day; then correct whatever is altered, and apply a poultice of ground chalk, egg white, or fine flour, after you have removed the compress, and place a soft wool covering over the poultice.
And then, when this poultice is properly affixed and the bone position is not altered, leave it; do not remove it until the fracture is healed and the bone is strong; for many times, this fracture is healed in three weeks.
And if, again, a festering boil occurs, then administer what we have often said for its resolution until it is healed and the boil is gone.
And if the fracture is with a gap, then consider if it is a detached piece of bone or splinters; then you must carefully remove those splinters with whatever is appropriate for removing them from the instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with leeches according to your need.
And after you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains, then you close the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, place one of the appropriate poultices over it and allow it to heal until it is sound.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is fractured.
Often the collarbone is fractured on the part in front of the muscle, and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
When it is fractured, or separated, or separated into two parts without splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is such that splinters occur, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is such that the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation in that case, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two assistants present, so that one holds the aid and steadies the fractured collarbone, and the other extends the neck toward that area.
And then hold the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correct, as is appropriate, and neither protrudes too much nor is concave.
And if you need more extension, then you should place under the patient's neck a pad of cloth or wool, and its size should be according to your need; and extend and elevate the collarbone and compress the pad with your hand until you align the fracture as is appropriate.
And if you cannot pull the collarbone's ends outward, because it has become impacted, then the patient should be placed supine on their back, and a pillow of medium size should be placed on their muscle.
And the assistant presses on the patient's muscle from below so that the collarbone bone is elevated from its impacted position, and then you straighten the fracture and hold it with your fingers.
But if you feel splinters of the collarbone and they are loose, then you should cut around them and easily remove those splinters.
And if it is lodged in the bone, then you make an incision with one of the scalpels you have prepared for this, after you have placed under the collarbone the instrument that serves to hold it; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is its shape:
And it is similar to a spoon, of which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to what you need, according to the size of the bone or its smallness.
But its length should be as possible for the operation; it should have two ends like a pair of tongs, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
And if the opening of the wound you made when removing the splinter from the bone is wide, and you are certain of a hot abscess, then you should bring together the two edges of the wound with stitches.
And if the opening is small, or you fear a hot abscess, then you should fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the size of the wound's gap.
And if a hot abscess arises, then soak a cloth in sour wine and rubbed oil and wine, and place it over that.
And be careful that the bandage does not move from the broken bone. Therefore, you must check the patient every day.  When the ligament is softened or you see the bandage has come loose, then you should rectify this and tighten the bandage.
Then, place the patient's sleeping position on their neck, and place a small cushion under their armpits, underneath the pillow, so that the patient's shoulder is elevated, and thus the fracture of the collarbone is raised to the height of the muscle; or the arm is secured to the neck.
And do not loosen the bandage if there is no obvious suppuration or abscess at the site, until twelve days have passed.
And then renew the plaster if you see the patient's skin reacting to it, and adjust it to the fracture and keep it there until the fracture of the collarbone has healed and solidified.
And it is strengthened and comforted within twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some individuals, it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a fractured shoulder.
The shoulder blade is fractured on its outer side, and it is not broken in the middle but at the edges.
And therefore, when the middle of the shoulder is fractured, and it is not apparent but known only by touch, then, according to the nature of the fracture, you must prepare it and restore it to its natural form by every possible means.
Then place on the area powdered millstones with egg white and linen cloth; then place folded cloth; and then place a thin, flat wooden board, sized to cover the entire shoulder blade or a little more.
But if underneath the board there is a hollow in any part of the shoulder blade, then you shall fill that hollow with linen cloth, and then raise the board evenly.
And then tightly bind it from above with a long bandage, with proper constriction and firmness of the board, until it is fixed in place.
And examine the bandage each day; and whenever it becomes loosened, bandage it again, tightening it and repositioning the board if it has shifted from its proper place.
And let the patient lie on their sound side.
And the shoulder blade is restored in 20 or 25 days.
And therefore when this number of days is complete, then you dissolve the bandage, and be sure that the injury is of the bones, whose connection is not feared, nor their separation.
And if you proceed from the place of the heavy bone and place the splint underneath, then you cut over it, remove it, and act as we said in the fracture of the furcula, or the treatment of an abscess, if that's the case here.
Chapter 7.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle, and this is often delayed; but the extremities are often more rapidly fractured and crushed.
And the symptoms when it is broken in the middle are that it tilts to the sides, and great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, and a strong, fast pulse, and vomiting of blood occurs; and the cavity in the broken bone is evident, and not hidden from touch.
And the restoration of it is that the patient should be laid on their back, and a cushion placed between their shoulder blades; and then press on the muscles and align the sides of the foot using the hands on both sides, and carefully proceed with the operation and readjustment in every possible and suitable manner until the bone returns to its proper shape.
Then apply a plaster and bandages, and place a thin splint of willow or reed or similar lightweight material over it, and wrap it in cloth; and then carefully bind it around the broken bone so it is not dislocated; and use a circular bandage to support the back in some cases, and tighten it appropriately; and then check the bandage frequently, and when it is softened, tighten it again.
And if the bandage causes irritation, swelling, pain, or an abscess to develop in the area, then you should loosen it and remove the plaster, and treat any problems that result from it with appropriate methods; and then reapply the plaster, if necessary, to the affected area; and adjust the bandage until it is healed.
Chapter 8.
Of the restoration of broken ribs.
Know that a fractured rib usually occurs only in the thicker, middle sections, and the ends or front portions are not easily bruised in that way because they are cartilaginous. And its location is not hidden from examination through questioning.
And the restoration is to align the fracture with the fingers, in the most possible way, until it is properly aligned, and then apply a poultice and bind the broken bone with a splint, if needed.
And if the rib fracture is extending inward, the patient experiences intense pain and stabbing, similar to the pain of someone with pleurisy, because the bone pierces the membrane; and there is difficulty breathing, coughing, and vomiting of blood; and this is a difficult condition to cure.
And the ancients were ingenious in dealing with this.
And they said:  You must give the patient foods that generate inflammation and gas, so that the belly is swollen and stretched, and the fracture is pushed outward.
However, we would avoid this, because it is not a light matter to cause a hot abscess, if it does not already exist; and if it already exists, then this approach might help.
And some of them said that a cupping glass should be placed on it and then suction applied forcefully; and this seems like a reasonable approach, but nevertheless, it is feared that the cupping glass might draw the superfluities to the area due to weakness.
And some of them said: the area should be covered with wool that has been soaked in hot oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that the bandage is tight when wrapped around it according to its shape; and then the patient should be treated with a pleurisy-like diet and medicine.
And if a severe cause restricts the patient, who cannot endure it, and the bone pierces the covering with a harmful puncture, we fear for the patient, then we must cut open the area, expose the fractured rib, and then place an instrument to hold the rib in place, the method of which has been explained before, and we must cut the bone easily and remove it.
And then we should bring the two edges of the wound together, if it is large, with stitches, and treat it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then you should mix powders in rubbed oil and apply it to the area, and treat the patient with what is appropriate for the internal abscess.
And let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Chapter 9.
Of the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and sometimes they are caused by friction, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And when this happens to someone and you want to know if they will be healed or not, then consider this: if their muscles are already softened, numb, dead, and you cannot move them, extend them, or squeeze them, and when you press them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and they do not feel pain, then know that they are not healed, and in this condition it is deadly.
And if you move them and feel constriction with your fingers and pricking, then know that the bone's integrity is preserved and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
And if you see a similar condition in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know if it will be healed or not, then consider the patient's feet.
Then if you see that the feet are already softened, observe the condition of the muscles, and when the patient lies on their back, they are affected by involuntary movements and/or bowel movements, and sometimes they are lying on their back, wanting to urinate but unable, then know that it is fatal, and thus do not bother with their treatment.
But if you observe no such symptoms, the cause is milder and less severe.
And the treatment for this is to apply a sedative poultice to the inflamed/irritated vertebra, either with oil or roasted egg yolks; apply this poultice for some time each day until the inflamed area is soothed.
And then apply a comforting and soothing plaster, and bind it tightly; and command the patient to rest and remain tranquil, and not to sleep on the affected area until it is healed.
And if you observe signs of bone breakage or any other detached piece, then you should cut away the surrounding tissue and remove that bone; then bring the two edges of the wound together, using sutures if it's a large wound; and then treat it with a healing ointment until it heals.
But if the back of the bone in the hip is broken, and it is the end of the thigh bone, then you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the rectum, and align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible according to anatomical order; then apply a plaster and bandage, if necessary, and bind it tightly.
And if you detect broken pieces in that area, then you should cut away at it and remove the broken pieces, and treat the wound with the previous treatment until it heals.
Chapter 10.
Of the restoration of the wide/broad (or large) area/region/space.
The bones of the hips are often broken, and if they break, their fracture is only a connection at the extremities, and they are cut along their length and deviate to the inside; the patient experiences pain in the area and puncturing, and the leg is paralyzed when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it until it is over the fracture, which is the shape of it.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, then you align it according to what is prepared for you by equating it until it is assembled in its natural shape.
But if the fracture is along the length or already deviates towards the lower parts, then you make the patient lie on their stomach until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. And therefore, when you have aligned it, place a poultice on it, and then place a splint of wood or cane on it, and tighten it firmly so that you do not fear puncturing the fracture or displacing the splint.
And align the concavity with whatever fills it until it takes on the tightness according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on their back or on their sound side.
But if a suppurative abscess occurs, then you cease the extension and restoration until the suppurative abscess is subsided, and place on it what calms it according to what has preceded; and then return to the restoration, and tighten it as is appropriate.
And if the bones are shattered or comminuted, then you must remove them.
Chapter 11.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured limb.
Adjutori" is that which is between the elbow and the top of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two ways, of which one is that you take a smooth piece of wood of medium thickness, in this fashion:
And having tied two bandages at both ends, it should then be suspended from a raised position.
And place the patient on a stretcher; then, gently lay the patient's broken arm on the wood until the elbow reaches the middle point where the curve of the wood is attached.
And then suspend something heavy over it, or the attendant can extend it further downwards; and then the physician should hold the fracture with their hands together, until the fracture returns as it should.
The other method is for the patient to be lying on their back with the arm held by a bandage around the patient's shoulder; then instruct two assistants that one of them holds what is above the fracture with their hands, and the other holds what is below it; and each of them extends in their own direction.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you tighten a bandage over the fracture and another bandage over that, and each of the two assistants extends in their own direction.
And therefore, if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, it is necessary that half of the bandage be placed over the elbow and the other below the fracture towards the arm.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the elbow, then it is necessary that the bandage be placed over that spot and over the elbow.
Apply that easily and without effort, and when it is wrapped as needed and properly applied, then tighten it if a painful abscess does not occur.
But if a painful abscess occurs, then leave the tightening until the seventh day, and place on it wool soaked in sour wine and rubbed oil until the abscess subsides, and then you tighten it.
And the way to tighten that is to place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of fresh cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm over that support, and place their open hand over their muscle.
And place the cloth and tighten the arm over the support so that the arm is in a straight position, unless some cause from the broken bone prohibits this.
And then use splints.
And you should place over the fracture that splint which is widest and strongest of the other splints; and place between each splint the width of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the break, with an addition of three fingers on each side.
And then tighten the splints with the binding which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And make sure your binding over the fracture area is tighter. And the longer the fracture, the less tight the binding should be.
And therefore, adjust the positioning of the splints and the binding as we have described, at the time when you restore the limb to its proper position, then you do so.
And if you fear a suppurative abscess, then you leave the binding and splints on for seven days, as we have stated.
And then consider the limb in all its parts for three days, so that no bruising or inflammation occurs in the area; or the limb is forbidden food due to the excess of binding; and therefore, rectify everything according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that no cause for this will occur, then do not loosen the binding until approximately three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands be on their stomach.
And place on the injured area a poultice of full wool, and make sure at all times of night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not distorted, or that the binding is softened; therefore, correct all of this with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned before, so that the food is subtle at the beginning until it begins to be strengthened; and then it is necessary to make the food thicker.
Because the custom of the injured area and leg is that they be bound for 40 days; and then it is necessary that they be unbound, and a bath be administered and medicated with ointments which correct that.
And if the fracture is severely inflamed, then you do not loosen the bandage and splints for at least a day or up to two months.
Chapter 12.
Of the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two "foils"; of which one is small, and it is that which sits above the said knuckle, and the other is large and it is that which is situated below the small knuckle, on the lower part.
And frequently the larger foil is broken alone, or the smaller one, and frequently both are broken together.
And when the smaller foil is fractured above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the foil is fractured below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And even worse than that is when both bones are fractured together.
Therefore, if the bone that is fractured is the small foil above, then it is convenient for the physician, for its restoration, to apply a small extension with ease until it is set.
But if the large foil is what is fractured, then it is necessary to apply a more forceful extension.
And if both foils are fractured together, then it is necessary for you to apply the pressure much more forcefully.
And it is necessary that you place the shape of your hand towards the restoration of it and the extension of it stretched over a cushion, and let the palm of the hand be at the upper parts, higher than all the other fingers, and the said thumb be lower than the other fingers, and the patient be lying on a polished, smooth surface.
And there should be a cushion in front of him, at his height or level, so that the patient does not experience discomfort.
And then the minister extends the arm of the lower part with his hand or a bandage, and another minister extends the upper part similarly.
And then the physician repositions the bone until it is reduced to the best possible shape.
And if in the fracture of the bone there are obstacles, then you must use all your skill to reduce each piece to its proper place.
And if there appears a separated obstacle that impedes the setting of the bone and you are not hopeful about restoring it, then you must proceed on it and remove that obstacle using the method mentioned above.
But if the fracture is with pus, I have already made a separate chapter on that, and therefore take care of it accordingly.
And if you encounter a boil or abscess at the beginning of your restoration, then you should line a cloth with a plaster made of roasted oil and white wax, and let it be moderate in thickness and subtle, and tightly bind the cloth over it until the abscess subsides; and then remove the plaster and apply a prepared plaster of powdered mulberry root and egg white, and then place poultices; and the poultice that is placed over that pus-filled fracture should be the strongest and most potent.
And know that the name of the poultices for the arm is given in the major arrangements, or in other words, the fracture is in one or two parts at once.
And then apply your bandage to the site of the fracture as strongly and forcefully as possible, and moreover, if the bandage is applied to the parts above the fracture or the parts below, apply it somewhat less tightly, according to what was mentioned in the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths wrapped around the fracture should be soft and damp, not hard or rough, and the bandage with which it is bound should be moderately fine and coarse, just as we have described.
And observe the limb and its ligaments for several days. And if any cause arises requiring correction, such as swelling that occurs in the limb, then you must soak the limb in hot water until the swelling subsides.
And leave the limb unbound until it rests, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the bandage has softened, and the bone has returned to its proper place, then correct everything with your skill.
And consider if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess bandaging, for then you must loosen it slightly and leave it for a few days until nourishment flows to it, and then tighten it again.
But if the patient has no such cause as we have mentioned, then it should not be loosened for approximately twenty days or thereabouts.
And then the patient's hand should be suspended by its own power of movement, and allow them to sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or a very mild one is restored in 28 days, and all of this is according to the constitution and the potency of the individual.
Chapter 13.
Of the restoration of fractures of the wrist and fingers.
Know that a fracture of the wrist and joints of the fingers is often a delayed fracture, and that it frequently happens from repeated trauma rather than a single incident.
And when a fracture or injury occurs to the hand, the patient must be placed lying down on their back with the injured arm extended straight out, and then place their own hand over the extended arm.
Then the assistant stretches the broken bones, and the doctor holds them together until they are firmly rejoined.
And then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, otherwise a painful abscess may develop; then place a splint over the area, proportionate to its size, and you have already wrapped this in a cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts inside the palm of the hand, then make a bandage shape like a sphere of cloth, and tell the patient to grip it with their fractured palm; then it should be bound with a long strip of cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, with a cloth in which it is supported, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and bind it as is necessary.
However, if the fracture is in the outer parts, then it is necessary to place a splint over it and another splint on the lower part of the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply a bandage, as the hand is wrapped back towards the fingers with ligaments.
And if a fracture occurs in any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be bandaged as necessary, and then it should be held with the palm, and if you want, place a small, straight splint on it so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, or the index finger, or the ring finger, or the little finger, then it should be bandaged and tied with the finger next to it; or all should be tied together following the continuity, which is better; or place a small splint on it, just as we said for the thumb.
And consider, in your restoration, and afterwards, the disposition of a festering abscess and its opposition – with your operation – with what is necessary when any such cause occurs, according to how the account of it is repeated.
Chapter 14.
Of the restoration of a fractured thigh bone.
The thigh bone is often broken and is declared to be fractured according to how it is broken at the front and the most superior part.
And the restoration of it is done by tightening a ligament over the fracture and another ligament under the fracture, and the patient is laid prostrate on their stomach and on their face.
And then the attendant extends all the ligament to its proper position, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is close to the root of the thigh, then the ligament should be close to the knee, in the direction of the groin, so that the extension works on it, and the other ligament under the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is near the knee, then the ligament should be placed near the knee, and the extension should be towards the lower parts.
And then the physician aligns the bone with both his hands until it returns to its natural shape and the bone is properly joined; then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, otherwise the limb will develop an abscess. But if an abscess occurs, then you must leave it for a few days until the hot abscess subsides, and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be applied thus:  it is necessary to wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, fold it two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap the leg until you reach the ankle and the root of the thigh.
And insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the knee, towards the lower parts, and let the ends of the splint be on the highest part of both sides; then wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.
And then place pads (or, possibly, splints/sticks) on the thigh at the location of the fracture, and place one of these pads over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the fracture is properly secured.
Then start with a strong bandage in the middle over the fracture site, using three or four wraps, and as you move away from the fracture site, the bandage should be progressively less tight, lighter and softer.
Then extend the two ends of the splint downwards until they reach the ankle, and hold both ends of the splints on the other side to ensure the bandage stays in place.
Then leave the bandage on, ensuring that no ulceration, boil, inflammation or similar conditions affect the limb; and if any such problem arises, remove the bandage and correct the issue immediately, as we have often stated.
But if there is a piece of bone protruding, then you must push it back in as best as possible; and if not, cover it and leave it, and treat it according to the previous treatments until healed.
And sometimes this thigh fracture is held together with splints, as we have said regarding the forearm and arm.
However, in a restoration done in this way, the patient does not experience lameness; and if it is restored alone, or the other leg is applied, then it is necessary that the one which has always limped, does so.
And know that the thigh is held in place for forty days, or a little more, or a little less, and all of this according to the diversity of the constitutions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the restoration of a kneecap (patella) fracture.
Know that a kneecap fracture is often very painful, but often leads to considerable friction.
But if it is a fracture, then it is only a crack or hole in its parts, and this is with or without a bruise, and everything is based on the sensation.
And the restoration of it is thus: the broken parts are glued together with splints until they are joined together as much as possible by alignment, ease, and crafting.
And then apply a plaster and place it over the circular area, if needed; and bind tightly with a bandage appropriate to this.
And then consider all the treatments we have described in other fractures, just as in case of boils and similar conditions, so that it counters any accident with what is appropriate for it until it heals.
Chapter 16.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the thigh bone; and the other is thin, and is called the shinbone.
And the treatment for some types of leg fracture is similar to that for the two bones of the arm, and therefore its restoration is done in the same way as for the arm, and the operation is similar.
And if both bones are fractured together, the leg is dislocated in every direction.
And if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is dislocated forward.
And if the thicker bone is fractured, you find it underneath, and it is one of those things that is not hidden from you.
And then administer extension, equating, and ligating the splints according to the same procedure.
However, if the leg fracture is large with many splinters, the extension should be smaller and gentler, and alleviate with your skill.
And if there is some operation on the arm for the leg, and when you have splinted and completed all your operation, then you will take pieces of wood that are placed beneath the supports (which are placed under padding), and they are also made from palm branches; choose those of small thickness and not large ones, and their length should be according to the length of the leg, from the top to the bottom.
And then wrap each one in two layers according to its length, placing one on the one side of the leg and the other on the opposite side, and they should extend from the top to the lowest part of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places, at the two ends and in the middle, for with this arrangement the leg is prevented from bending to the right or left, and remains in a good position.
And now a splint of wood has been applied, according to the length of the leg; and the leg is placed upon it so that it preserves its position (literally: so that it conserves that of movement).
And it is especially necessary that you do this in the case of a fracture, and when it is properly a fracture.
And then examine the leg every day and study it with that subsequent study, and watch for abscesses, inflammations, or other similar problems; and if any of these occur, then apply what is necessary to it until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
Of the fracture of the heel bone and the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture of the heel doesn't happen often; however, fractures of the foot bones do occur sometimes; and fractures of the toes are rare, and they are usually only affected by abrasion at the points of exposure.
And if the bones of the foot suffer a fracture, and the bones are sometimes elevated above themselves, then the patient places their foot on the ground, prepared as if walking.
Then, the patient places one foot on whatever has lifted the bones, and then presses and pushes on them until they return to their proper positions.
And then, apply a plaster and bandage over it, and place a small board with two flat ends underneath the sole of the foot.
And then, tighten that with a descending bandage, now that you have wrapped it in cloths and other things that are necessary.
And after three or four days have passed, then you loosen the bandages, for you will find the bones either pushed together, or broken, or dislocated, and the operation for those is as you know.
But if any bones are broken, then restore them, and put them together in the same way as I have described to you in the restoration of fingers of the hand. And then, place a splint along the length of the broken finger, and let it extend slightly beyond it; and then, under the foot, place this board which I have described to you, and tighten it with a descending bandage.
But if what is broken of the bones is two, three, or more, then you place splints of their respective sizes, wrapped in linen cloth, over each finger.  Tighten the board at the lowest part of the foot.  Place one end of the board outside the foot’s belly, so that it holds firmly.
And it is important for you not to forget that you should consider all the causes which I have told you about other fractures and accidents that we have discussed; and prepare yourself for all eventualities with what is necessary.
Chapter 18.
Of the fracture of the thighbone, the pelvic bone, and the penis in the male.
When the thighbone is fractured, then make a splint of  a smoothed, planed wood, and gently incline it towards the back of the leg, and have it supported behind.
And then the midwife fills the thigh with wool, and it should be applied to the thigh just as it should be; and then the woman should be laid down, and lift the leg gradually, as everything should be placed before the thigh in such a way that the fractured bone returns to its place.
Then place poultices on their back, and when they want to urinate, the wool comes out easily and then they urinate, and you reposition and return it in the same manner as you did at first, and then return it to its original position.
And do this for seven days or so, for that is how it is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder and tightly insert a reed tube into the opening; then insert the entire bladder into the thigh, and then blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder is inflated inside the thigh, for the fracture will return to its place.
And then it should be filled with wool and left for several days, as we have recounted, until it is healed.
And when the hip bone of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and setting what we have recounted for the hip bone.
And what is right is not hidden in these close fractures and those that heal slowly, especially for him who has some study and use and understands this my book's words; for this fracture signifies to you, in the greatest ways, the correct path in its restoration and the binding of it, therefore understand well.
But in the case of a broken penis, take a binding made of bird's skin, and place it on the penis; then apply a cloth bandage, and let it remain bound for three days or thereabouts, and it will be healed.
Chapter 19.
Of the restoration of fractures of bones when [the injury] is from a blow.
It is convenient for the one who has sustained a fractured bone from a blow, especially if the bone is large, such as the thigh bone or the shinbone, or similar to those, that you bleed them and lance them immediately if the circumstances of bloodletting indicate it, as we stated above.
And if the blow causes blood to flow, then it is necessary that you apply a styptic to it, in such a way that you powder it with crushed zegi, if another cause is not present.
And then immediately begin to restore the fracture on that same day, and do not delay this if an abscess does not arise.
However, if a serious abscess arises, then you should delay the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you will cause bad complications.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin, then it is necessary to carefully reduce and align it with your hands with ease and little force.
However, if your hands are not equipped to reduce and align it, then you reduce it with these instruments; and this instrument is made of iron, its length being seven or eight inches and its width according to the size of the wound; and for this reason it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these instruments, according to the needs of the treatment for every type of fracture.
And it should be round, and of little thickness so that it is not compressed against it during the operation.
And let it have a sharp edge at its extremities; and let the upper part of it be thicker, and the middle portion thinner.
And this is its shape:
And it is called in Greek "labre volentz atelarem," that is, a small spatula.
Therefore, it is necessary that the sharp end be placed high above the prominent bone extremity.
And you push it in one stroke until the bone returns, and it is aligned with some equality.
Therefore, exert yourself to align the fractured extremities, and if the broken extremity is delicate and does not accept the instrument readily, then it is necessary for you to cut the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
However, if you cannot fully reduce the bone with what we have described, then cut it with whatever surgical instruments are appropriate; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And then scrape away any remaining roughness and small splinters from the bone.
And when the bone is reduced, and shortly after the reduction the patient experiences intense pain, then understand that the bone has not returned to its natural position; and then do what you believe will greatly benefit the patient.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, then soak a cloth in black astringent wine, and specifically if it is summer, do not apply a plaster or any other oily substance to the wound, so that you do not cause swelling and corruption.
And then administer styptics at the time of your completing the bone's restoration, and leave the wound uncovered, and then cut the wound with scissors and use dressings according to the size of the wound.
And be very careful not to compress the wound with the fracture, because foolish doctors have often done this, and caused death or a corroding or spreading illness in their patients; this is gangrene. And if your constriction is slight, soft, different from the constriction of other fractures.
