And if the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear any of the bad accidents we have recounted, and you find pain in the area, then you should not apply styptics, but rather make a dressing of hard cloths instead of styptics and bind it firmly.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to fester, that a suppuration has arisen there, then remove the cloth you had placed on it with wine, and then administer poultices, medicaments with which, according to our custom, we treat wounds, like tetrafarmacon and similar medicaments.
And you should loosen the bandage and examine the wound every day morning and evening until it is healed and firm, and the limb should be prepared in such a way that pus can drain from it to the lower parts with ease.
If the wound has passed many days and is not healed or the pus is not draining from it, then know that there are complications from which you must investigate the wound with a probe. And of those complications, those that are separated, remove and extract them; those that are not separable and cause pain to the limb, force you to cut them as it is possible for you.
And if the wound develops a swelling or inflammation, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then you must apply to it one of those things appropriate for its cure, of which the preceding narrative in its chapter describes.
And concerning this, in which my discourse should end and you should demonstrate your skill: when a large bone is broken, treat it upon the limb as you would the bone of the thigh or the arm and similar to those of larger limbs, and do not presume to pull or extract it, because often death results from that; let it be until it can set itself, for it often sets within 20 or 30 days; and then treat the wound if it seems appropriate, and if it does not set, leave it.
Chapter 20.
Of the treatment of the swelling that occurs at the site of any fracture.
Often this swelling occurs at the site of healing from a fracture, especially when close to the joints, and it distorts the shape of the limb; and it often hinders the limb's natural function.
Then consider if the swelling is recent, and apply medicines that have a styptic effect, such as alum, vinegar, myrrh, ceroc, and similar ones; use some or all of these, and mix them with wine, egg white, or vinegar, and place this mixture on the swelling with a bandage, binding it firmly.
And leave it bound tightly for several days; and then loosen it and reapply another, until the swelling is gone; or apply a lead sheet over it gently, for lead has the property of resolving any swelling in the limbs.
However, if the swelling has hardened and become tight, and it is necessary to remove it forcefully, then cut it open at its most prominent part, and remove the excess and prominent part, or cut it with some instruments until it is removed; and treat the area until it is healed.
Chapter 21.
Of the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and afterwards the limb remains weak and frail, not according to its natural strength.
When a fractured bone is restored, and afterward the limb remains weak and frail, this is due to several causes: one of which is the excessive loosening of the ligaments and their binding not being done correctly; or by excessive tightness of the ligaments that prevents nourishment from reaching the limb; or due to excessive application of poultices; or by unnecessary movement not at the appropriate time; or by a lack of blood in the patient's body and by their weakness.
And the cure for this is to feed the patient and make their body tender until blood is replenished; and administer baths; and introduce cheerfulness and joy.
And then place a poultice on the limb, so that the poultice draws more nourishment to it, or continue applying warm water poultices until nourishment flows and returns to its natural form.
Chapter 22.
Of the treatment of broken bones when they are improperly set and prevent the operation from being as it should be.
When, after the limb has been set, there is still a crookedness, prominence, or bump on the broken bone, and this has changed the shape of the limb, but the limb's natural operation is not prevented, then you should not accept the word of someone who claims the limb is broken again.
But many foolish physicians and bone-setters have done this in our land; and this procedure is highly reprehensible, leading to great fear, the sign of which is death.
However, if the limping and swelling are recent, then they should be soaked in warm water in which softening herbs are steeped, such as elder leaves and roots, and melilot, and similar ones; and an emollient plaster should be applied, such as well-made diachylon; or a mucilage from elder root should be taken and mixed with hen's egg yolk or beeswax oil, and plastered with that; or fat figs should be taken, crushed with pigeon dung, and similar remedies for strengthening consolidation.
And sometimes it is dissolved by consistent gentle rubbing with wet hands, and movement of the limb should be administered in every direction at all times.
However, if the crookedness has progressed, becoming strong and stony, and requiring iron to be cured, then the pus must be stopped and the protruding bone dissolved.  This excess swelling on the bone itself must be cut away with sharp scalpels, and care and attention must be given to this process.
Chapter 23.
Concerning the treatment of dislocations.
Dislocation is the displacement of any joint from its proper place, preventing movement and causing the limb's shape to be distorted, bringing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore, when an accident causes a dislocation, it is essential to try to reduce it immediately, and not delay its reduction, for if delayed, the area becomes inflamed and hard, making the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And therefore, it is not advisable to move or stretch the limb during the inflammation, for often it causes spasms and prolonged pain.
But when this happens, you should apply a poultice to the inflamed area of the patient, and allow it to remain until the inflammation subsides; then the limb should be bathed in warm water and oil, and the dislocation reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with this, so that the affected area returns to its proper place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation, following the precedent set for fractures; from the most prominent part of the body down to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
If the two jaws are dislocated quickly, and the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly displaced from their location and slightly softened; or they are completely and thoroughly dislocated, so that the joint is softened, saliva drips from the patient, the patient cannot retain it or cannot close their mouth, and the tongue is bound in the mouth.
However, when the dislocation is slight, it often returns on its own with little effort.
But if the dislocation is complete and thorough, then a gentle reduction must be administered immediately, and not delayed at all.
And the minister should hold the patient's head, and the physician should insert the fingertip of one hand into the area of the jaw dislocation inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or insert both thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers of their hand should be outside, holding them steady; and command the patient to relax their jaw muscles and move it in each direction; and the physician should push the jaw until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, then apply warm water and oil vapor to ease the reduction of both, and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And once they are returned and held in place, and the patient's mouth is closed and not sore, then place over those cloths powders of wax and oil; and let it be made of wax and oil mixed; and then be bound lightly with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and their head be supported between two pillows, so that it does not tilt to the right or left side; and do not force them to chew anything; let their food be soft until the pains have subsided and the jaws have healed, then let them eat whatever they wish.
And this should be administered with ease, and do not put pressure on opening the mouth for eating, drinking, and speaking, until the jaw is healed and sound.
And if the reduction of the two jaws, when dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, often causes fevers and constant sweating, and the patient's belly is strongly affected, and there is considerable vomiting of pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is fatal; and often the one who experiences this dies within a few days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated inward from its connection with the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated outward, and this is apparent to the touch.
And the restoration of it is that the patient lies on their back and extends their arms. And then press the area with your palm with firm pressure, for it will return.
And then place a poultice and powders on that area and press it down.
But the extremity of that which sits on the thigh and is connected to it is not dislocated at any time; therefore, it must be reduced and held in place as we have said and as is appropriate for you.
And then apply a poultice, powders, and a bandage, and order the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until healed.
And with this same treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of a muscle dislocation.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated in more than three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated from the upper part to the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated to the upper part of the muscle, and this is done hard; and it is not dislocated to the rear parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated to the front parts by the nerves.
And it is mostly dislocated and comes out to the lower parts towards the upper part, and especially in those whose flesh is little, it is easily dislocated; but in those in which there is a lot of flesh, it is difficult to come out.
And often, accidents befall some men through blows or falls, and their muscle is inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is believed to be dislocated because of that.
Then you should verify this until you are absolutely certain, and then proceed with the treatment of it.
And the dislocation is recognized when it is in the lower parts towards the top; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, because you find a clear difference between them; and you find a cavity at the head of the muscle, and below the top, near the touch of the head of the muscle, just like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear, nor move it with all movements.
If it is dislocated towards the feet or above, and you then find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And a slight reduction of this dislocation is made when it is fresh and the patient is young.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises the patient's hand to the upper parts; and then places your fingers below the patient's armpit, and lifts the joint with strength to its place, and the minister raises the patient's hand, and extends it upwards; and then places it below where it will easily return to its proper position.
But if, despite what we have said, it does not return, and it has been a considerable time since the dislocation occurred, then the patient should be bathed in hot water and given an application that softens and smooths, like a decoction of roots of althea, fenugreek, and melilot in water, and that should be applied.
And then the patient should be placed on their back, and under their armpits placed a wool sphere that is balanced between lightness and firmness, and then the physician places their heel on the sphere, and elevates the head of the muscle with force, and pulls the patient's hand so that it is not moved downward, and another minister holds the patient's head, so it is not moved downward, so that it will quickly return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce it in this way: have a man present who is taller than the patient, and have him stand on the affected side, and let him place his arm under the patient's armpit and lift the patient's arm upward until the patient is suspended in the air, and another minister pulls the patient's hand from the lower part of their stomach. But if the patient is light, then something else should be suspended with them, so that it adds weight, for the dislocation will return quickly.
And it is easily reduced by another method: a long wooden post, round like a mortar pestle, neither thick nor thin, is placed on the ground, under the patient.  Then, heavy cloths are placed on the top of the post, and the patient stands along the length of the post. The patient's hand is then extended towards the opposite side, and their body is pushed forcefully in the opposite direction, so that the joint quickly returns to its place.
And if the reduction is difficult due to all the causes we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a stick whose length is two arms long, and its width is four fingers wide, and its thickness is slightly narrowed at the armpit in this way:
And heavy cloths should be tied to the round top so that the post does not injure the patient.
And then, place the post under the patient's armpit, and extend their hand or arm along the post towards the lower parts, and tie the post to the arm and the hand.
Then, the arm should be placed on a ladder rung according to its width, and the hand extended downward, and the other side of the body suspended to the other side, so that the joint quickly enters into place.
And when the reduction is completed in any way possible, you should place a thick layer of wool under the armpit; then apply a prepared plaster of ground powder, ashes, and egg white to all the surrounding muscle, wrapping it around; afterwards, bind the armpit with a suitable bandage.  The bandage should be wrapped around the plaster, and the patient's hand should be tied to the neck.  Leave it undisturbed, and do not move the hand for seven days.
And the patient's diet should be light until the limb is strengthened, as this aids in their recovery.
And then, the diet should be increased after seven or five days, and the affected part should be exercised; and if it is firm and not swollen, then it is healed.
But if the joint has been dislocated multiple times due to moisture or another cause, then a cautery with three punctures, as described earlier in the chapter on cauteries, must be administered.
And if all this is done, and after seven days the bandage and joint are not firm, and the plaster and bandage are removed several times, and it is still not firm, and the arm cannot be raised to its usual position, then understand that the nerves within the muscle are severed, stretched, or swollen, and that the joint will never be fully restored.
For dislocations of the foot, nipple, or back areas, reduction is achieved by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to place.
And another bandage should be applied, and treat it until it is healed.
And if after healing, there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should use baths frequently until that heaviness is relieved and it returns to its original condition.
Chapter 27.
Of the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all directions, and more so in the front than the back, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because it is evident from looking or touching, whatever the configuration of the dislocation; and when you adjust the dislocated elbow with your hand, this is evident to you, for the joint is hollow and cannot bend the arm, nor touch its muscle.
And you should act quickly, and reduce the dislocation before it becomes chronically inflamed, for if it becomes chronically inflamed, the reduction is difficult, and the limb may not be completely healed; and especially when it is in the back part, for it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and causes more pain, and often results in death.
And the restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's arm should be extended with two hands, and the patient's arm outstretched, and the physician's hands are on the elbow and below it, and he pushes the joint with the fingertips of his hands together, or with the base of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front, then it returns by bending the arm with his hand, until he strikes the elbow with the base of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer vigorous extension and very strong force.
And it is that two assistants should extend the arm, and hold that extension, so that it is not pulled back against the extension.
And then the arm should be rotated in every direction, since it is wrapped in a folded cloth long over his hand, or bandage.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that these things aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with vigorous pressure until it returns.
And after the reduction, it is necessary to place a poultice on it containing astringent and dried egg white, and bind it tightly downwards, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and left for several days.
And then it should be loosened; and if the joint is in its proper place, then you loosen the bandage and leave it.
And if you see the joint not properly tightened, then you repeat the poultice and the bandage, and leave it for several days until it is tightened; and then loosen it.
But if during the reduction of the joint, the condition is still moving, then administer moistening by bathing and gentle rubbing, and compression, until it is relieved; or place upon the joint a soft, oily substance; and then bandage it and leave it on the limb day and night. And then remove that, and place it in a bath; and when it is softened, then you rub the joint with moderate rubbing, and then repeat the application of soft oil, once, and a second time, and a third time. with intermittent bathing, until it is healed.
And if you wish, place over that a warm, damp cowhide, bandaged tightly; and do this several times, for this is moist and helps return it to its proper position.
Chapter 28.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the arm, that is, of the joint between the arm and the hand.
Dislocation of the hand sometimes occurs, and the reduction of its dislocation is relatively easy. The opposite is true for other joints, however, speed is needed in reducing it at the time of dislocation, before the area becomes inflamed or a hot abscess forms there.
And the reduction of the dislocation is that you place the patient's hand joint on a table, and the assistant extends the patient's hand, and the physician places his hand or palm over the prominence of the joint, and pushes until it returns to its place.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm is already turned towards the back of the hand, in which case the patient places the back of their hand on a table during the extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is located on the back of the hand, then the patient's hand should be positioned palm-up on the table, so that the physician's hand can be placed over the prominence of the joint.
And so, if it returns quickly, all is well; and if not, then firmly hold it with a bandage to prevent an abscess and leave it; and do not repeat this, for otherwise, you will not endure nor succeed in reducing it after several days have passed.
However, if the joint remains twisted, and you find no cause for the patient's ailment, only that the hand is weakened and cannot grip anything, then know that the nerves are severed or damaged.  In such a case, there is no remedy but to cauterize firmly near the injury, and this will provide some relief.
Therefore, once the ailment is corrected, apply the bandage we have described, and let it remain bound for five days; then loosen it and leave it, and exercise the patient's hand.
However, if the movement is restricted and you find some heaviness in the area, then alleviate it with warm water and some friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter 29.
Of the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in various directions.
And when any of these fingers is dislocated towards the palm side or the underside of the finger, then straighten the finger and push the dislocation away from you with your fingers, until it returns to its place.
Then support the head of the finger and suspend it over the part to which it was dislocated, and leave it there for two days.
And then suspend and extend it until it is properly aligned in its place.
And when it is night, bind it according to this narration, and do not cease to loosen it during the day and exercise it with movement, and bind it at night.  Do this for several days until it is straight.
And do the same if it is dislocated toward the underside of the hand, and bind it to that side and perform your initial procedure until it is healed.
And similarly do this for dislocated areas elsewhere.
Chapter 30.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the spinal vertebrae.
When a complete dislocation of any of the spinal vertebrae in the back or neck occurs, and the dislocated vertebrae remain out of place, there is no cure in that case, for death awaits the patient.
And a sign of this is that the patient's bowel movements are involuntary and they cannot control them; and often some of the patient's limbs are paralyzed, or two legs, or two arms, or one of them; or a vertebra is out of place, for often it is out of place and the removal of it is done in four ways.
Therefore, this is now displaced behind and is called a "gibbous" condition.
But the treatment of this is that one should consider if the gibbosity happened during childhood, for then there is no cure, nor a complete healing.
However, for that which comes about by accident or impact or similar causes, the ancients described methods of treatment at great length, from which the most benefit can be derived; but I shall now shorten that, avoiding unnecessary details, for the sake of clarity and conciseness in my presentation, with which I have explained and organized this, drawing on various instruments, with which I will demonstrate and elucidate this.
And of those, only the one that occurs on the back itself is cured by what I am recommending.
And it is necessary that the patient be laid face down on a soft, padded surface close to a wall, and be supported underneath with soft padding so that the patient's foot is not injured.
And then a straight piece of wood should be placed, fixed into the ground at the end of the padded surface near the patient's head, and another piece of wood positioned under the patient's feet at the other end of the padded surface.
And one attendant should hold the first piece of wood, and it should not be buried in the ground; and another attendant should hold the other piece of wood in a similar manner.
And then wrap a bandage around the patient's head and secure it tightly underneath the temples; and extend the end of the bandage to the piece of wood situated near their head, and tie it there; then firmly secure another bandage around their hips, covering their thighs and legs; and finally, connect all the bandages and tie them to the other piece of wood situated near their feet.
And then each attendant should extend the bandage to the corresponding piece of wood, and the two pieces of wood and the bandages should not be moved from their current positions, however, they should not be fastened as we have described.
And the physician should place their palms upon the spine with force until it returns to its normal position, or that a board is placed beneath the spine, and then adjust the patient's feet onto the board until they return to a normal position.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a board whose width or length is approximately three arms' lengths, and make a cavity in the wall, mentioned previously as being near the patient, into which the end of the board can be placed. And then place the middle of the board on the spine, and the physician places their feet on the other end, and presses firmly until the spine is straightened and returns to its proper position.
And if you wish, open the wound with a turn that is turned by the hand; and you should place in the ground, behind the patient's head, two pieces of wood, each one arm's length long, and, behind the patient's feet, also two similar pieces of wood; and the space between each pair of pieces of wood should be a palm's breadth.  A hole should be made in each piece of wood through which the turning tool can pass, and each should be firmly fixed in the ground so as to prevent movement.
And place a round rod on which the turning tool is located, and let it turn within the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And on the end of the rod is a hole into which the turning tool fits, its length being a palm's width.  A similar arrangement is made in the other two pieces of wood.
And then tighten the bindings you have placed on the patient's feet, in the hole located towards the head, and the bindings you have placed on their thighs, in the turn located toward the feet.
And then, near each turn, there should be a helper who turns the turning tool with their hand, while the physician adjusts the vertebra as we have described.
And after the vertebra is returned and the area is adjusted, then apply a thick plaster with egg white, and afterwards, a bandage.  Then place a board splint on the plaster, whose width is about three fingers, and its length sufficient to cover the affected area of the vertebra.
And then bind it with the bandage we have described, and have the patient use subtle nourishment until they are healed.
And if any lingering issue remains in the area at the end of the healing process, then medicines that soften and cleanse the area through long-term use, as we have recounted, must be administered; and a lead lamina is also to be administered to that location.
And sometimes a problem occurs in the back of the spinal vertebrae, and is worsened by that dislocation;  and that is the bone that is displaced, for which it is not appropriate to use this medicine, because it could lead to death.
Chapter 31.
Of the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint are only subject to dislocation, and not to the issues affecting other joints, such as small movements and concavity.
And the hip joint is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated inward, or outward, or forward, or backward.
And it is more commonly dislocated inward, and less so forward or backward.
A sign of a dislocation inward is that when you align the patient's healthy leg with the injured one, the affected leg is longer and the knee is more prominent than on the healthy side, and the patient cannot bend their foot towards the shin (because of) the affected shin being inflamed with a hot abscess, as the hip head has settled there.
The sign of a dislocation outward is that the symptoms are opposite to this one.
A sign of a forward dislocation is that the patient stretches their leg fully but cannot bend it without pain in the knee, and if forced to go forward, cannot move forward; and their urination is retained, and their shin is inflamed, and they walk on their heel.
A sign of a backward dislocation is that the knee does not extend, nor can it be bent forward to bend the shin, and the leg is shorter than the other, and the shin is softened, and the hip head is towards that place.
However, the reduction of these types of dislocations is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, already impacted by the passage of time, then you apply reduction to it, and it remains in its position; then there is no complete cure for it; and then, no presumption should be made in that regard.
But in the case of a recent dislocation, one of the four types of dislocations, then you try to return the joint and extend it in the inside and outside directions, and move it to the right and left, for a strong thrust brings it back, and you need no other treatment.
However, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare strong instruments and extend the leg from the lower part, either with your hands or with a bandage tied above the knee; and another person should extend the upper hand, so that they put their hands under the patient's armpits; and then apply a strong bandage around the hip, and another person should hold the bandage's end.
And the extension should be either from the inside of the shin to the crotch area, or from the back to the back.
And perform the extension in all of these directions until the patient is lifted from the ground by their body weight and hangs suspended.
And this type of extension is a common method for all four [types].
And if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then for all cases, it is necessary to speak of the proper treatment.
In the proper reduction of a dislocation, when it is dislocated internally, the patient should be placed on their right side; then apply bandages to the root of the thigh, in the area between the thigh bone's head and the area beneath the groin; and then extend the bandage over the area of the groin to the highest part of the crotch.
And another strong assistant should take the patient's arms, firmly grasp the thick part of the affected thigh, and extend it outward forcefully, for this will return it to its place.
And this type has an easier cure, with which this limb is reduced than other types.
But if you are excused and do not comply with this type of treatment completely, then the patient's two feet must be bound together with a strong, light bandage, around both ankles and both heels.
And let the length of each be about four fingers' width, and let the affected leg be extended more than the other by two fingers' width.
And then the patient should be suspended over the top of a post, and be in the house; and let the distance from the ground be about two arms' lengths.
And then command a strong young man to hold the head of the thigh, and let another young man hold the patient, and the other young man, holding the thigh with strength, should push, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
But in the proper reduction, when it is a dislocation to the outside, the patient should lie on a stool, as we have recounted in the event of a sprain; and let the bandage be tightened on the affected leg and on the patient's foot.
Then two posts should be placed, one at the patient's feet and the other at the head; and then posts fitted together in the middle of the hill should be placed, over which wet cloths should be wrapped, so that the patient is not harmed because the post is between their thighs, to prevent the extension to the lower parts from being pulled.
And then each attendant should extend their part, and the physician with his hand will guide the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be slightly extended, and it is according to this same description on a hill; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the dislocated limb, and then holds it with the other hand, and with this holding, he extends the lower parts to the front of the joint.
And if the dislocation is in the rear parts, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended downwards, but elevated from the ground; it is necessary that he be placed on a hard surface, as is the case with a raised position if their thigh is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about the patient lying on two cushions; and it is on his side, and the ligaments should be extended as we have stated, not much.
And pressure should be applied with a board over the area where the joint is.
Therefore, when the reduction is complete—and the sign of the reduction is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend both legs of the patient, and when you see them aligned and the patient clenches his leg and expands it without excuse, then know that the limb is returned as required—and then you bring the two thighs together and place a poultice and bind it so that the thigh is not moved in any direction; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the ligament and poultice, and compare one leg with the other.
Then, if you see them aligned properly, then know that the dislocation is now fixed, and then release the patient.
And if you see any cause for softening, then you return and apply a poultice to that, and bind it according to your first binding, and leave it for three days.
And then loosen that, and have the patient walk slowly for some days, until it is strengthened.
Chapter 32.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, for it is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, but not backwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that, if you tell the patient to apply their leg with the thigh, then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration in all these ways is that you should make the patient lie on their back, so that they extend their leg as much as possible; place your two hands on their side, and place the patient's foot between your feet; and then press the patient's knee with your palms, and hold them tightly between your fingers on the knee with strength, and another helper should extend the leg until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its reposition is that their leg will adhere to the thigh with effortless ease.
Then plaster it and hold the leg close to the thigh, and then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days, and then unbind them.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until they are healed and strong.
But if reduction by you is unnecessary, then administer strong extension with ligaments, as described in the treatment of the hip previously.
Chapter 33.
Of the treatment of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes dislocated by a small movement, and sometimes dislocated completely, and its dislocation is either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that the ankle often swells on the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment for a small displacement is a gentle return, and it is to be extended easily with the hands and pressed until it returns to its place.
However, in treating a complete dislocation, you must have the patient lie straight, and have a strong and able assistant stand behind the affected leg, in the middle of the afflicted area.
Then you, with your right hand, take hold of the foot at the highest part, and with your left hand, the lowest part of the foot, at the ankle's location.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with the left, twice, then extend with the left; and push the foot's ankle with the right toward the leg without effort.
And do this twice, as we have counted.
And then push the ankle of the foot toward the leg a third time, and you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one or two attempts in this manner, and if you see the foot is straightened, it is good; and if not, then repeat the procedure, for it [the foot] has not returned.
But if you are prevented from reducing it with what we have recounted, then have the patient lie on their back on the ground, and place a firmly fixed pillow on the ground, and position it between their thighs, and you have already draped them over with a cloth so that the patient is not injured.
And then the patient's attendant holds their thighs, and another attendant extends the foot with their hands or with a bandage tied around the foot's joint; and then each attendant extends it against the extension of their partner, and the pillow is positioned between the patient's thighs, holding it, so that the patient's body is not pulled towards the parts below their extension.
And then the physician straightens the dislocation with their hands, and another attendant holds the healthy thigh at the lower parts, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And so when the dislocation returns and its successful reduction is clear to you, then apply a plaster and bind it firmly with bandages, and bandage the foot to the leg.
And you should be wary of the nerves located behind the heel, so that the bandage over them is not forceful and harms them severely.
And then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage has softened, tighten it.
And then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from activity for 40 days. However, if the patient is forced to move about before that period, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not be further dislocated and damaged, and thus receive no cure.
And if an abscess occurs, then you should apply to it what we previously described in another location for healing and poulticing, until it is resolved.
Chapter 34.
Of the treatment of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
It is necessary that the dislocated part be gently put back in place without effort; and this is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the joints of the foot, then it is necessary to have the patient place their foot on a soft surface, like the ground or a table, and remain in that position as if they were walking.
And you, stand beside it, and place your foot on the area where the joint protrudes, and then press on it with your foot with gentle force until it returns to its place, and you see it fully seated and no longer appearing in the protruding position.
And then place a board beneath the sole of their foot that covers the entire foot, with two ends, and firmly and decently bind this with a bandage for three days; and then release it and keep them from walking for several days, until the healing is strong and the repositioning is secure.
Chapter 35.
Of the types of dislocation that are caused by blows or fractures, or both.
When any of these causes occur, and you exert yourself greatly in the treatment and restoration of the patient, death often results.
Therefore, one should not presume to treat such cases, but only those skilled in the art, with long experience, compassionate and merciful, slow, not rash or presumptuous; and who initially uses only medicines to ease painful inflammations, and leave the patient to divine judgment, except in cases where one hopes for recovery from death, with alleviation of the illness, and where some hope appears. Then strive to perform the reduction as soon as possible after the cause, before a severe inflammation develops.
And if the limb returns as desired, then administer a regimen that relieves the inflammation and treats the wound with whatever drying ointments are appropriate.
And if a dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and the bones are separated, then you must treat the separation, and do in this case what we have said concerning simple illnesses of the preceding parts of these.
And, according to your ability, avoid that which leads to fear in the ways, as my teaching has led you; for this is better for you from your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more healthful for your true body in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis, perfectly, of whom may God be blessed. Amen.
